The Essence's Indifference to Opposites
Al-Jurjani explains indifference to opposites and rational possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essence in itself does not necessitate any opposite and how rational possibility allows for opposite descriptions.

Core Idea
The essence in itself is indifferent to opposites; rational possibility permits description by any opposite.

Al-Jurjani builds a crucial distinction: the essence is not identical to its accidents, nor does it necessitate any specific opposite. He explains that rational possibility—the intellect's permission—is the criterion for distinguishing accidents from essential parts. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional statement
Read the opening conditional: if the essence were identical to or contained an opposite, it couldn't be described by that opposite. Since it can be described by opposites, it is neither the same nor a part of its opposite.
This sets the logical foundation.
2
Grasp rational possibility
Al-Jurjani defines rational possibility (al-imkan al-'aqli) as the intellect's permission to conceive the essence without an accident, even if that accident is impossible in reality. This is key to understanding how opposites can apply.
Do not confuse with real possibility.
3
Distinguish accidents from internal parts
For accidents, the intellect can conceive the essence without them; for internal parts, it cannot. This distinction shows why opposites are accidental and not essential.
Examples: man can be conceived without laughter (accident) but not without rationality (internal constituent).
4
Conclude and refute a misconception
From the above, the essence in its own rank does not necessitate any opposite determinately. Al-Jurjani refutes the claim that 'four is even' is a necessity of the essence itself, arguing that if it were, it would hold even in non-existence.
This reinforces that necessity arises only with existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شيء منها نفسها، أو داخلاً فيها لما أمكن اتصافها بما يقابله . ومن هذا يعلم أيضاً أنها ليست مقتضية ولا مستلزمة لشيء من المتقابلات على التعيين. قوله : ( لما أمكن إلخ) أراد به الإمكان العقلي، أي لما جوز العقل اتصافها بما يقابله؛ فإن العارض سواء كان لازم الماهية أو غيره بيناً أو غير بين يمكن تصور الماهية بدونه، وإن كان محالاً، فيجوز اتصافه بما يقابله بخلاف ما هو داخل فيها، فإن تصورها بدونه محال كالمتصور، وإليه أشار المحقق التفتازاني في شرح العقائد النسفية، حيث قال : بخلاف الضاحك والكاتب مما يمكن تصور الإنسان بدونه فإنه من العوارض. قوله: (ومن هذا يعلم إلخ) أي ومما ذكرنا من أن تلك العوارض ليست للماهية في مرتبة ذاتها، وأنه يجوز العقل اتصافها بكل واحد من المتقابلات يعلم أنها في مرتبة ذاتها ليست مقتضية لشيء منها ولا مستلزمة لها، وهذا لا ينافي اقتضاءها إياه باعتبار وجودها مطلقاً أو خارجاً أو ذهناً، وإنما ذكر الشارح هذه المقدمة مع أنها لا دخل لها في بيان المغايرة؛ تمهيداً لما سيجيء من بيان معنى تقديم حرف السلب على الحيثية وتأخيره. فما قال صاحب المقاصد من أنه إذا قيل : الأربعة زوج أو ليس بفرد يراد أن ذلك من لوازم الماهية ومقتضياتها من غير نظر إلى الوجود، ليس بشيء. كيف ولو كان ذلك مقتضى الماهية لاقتضتها حال العدم أيضاً . قوله : ( على التعيين قيد بذلك ؛ لأن الكلام فيه لا لإفادة أنها مقتضية لشيء منها لا على التعيين فإنه باطل لما مر من أن الإنسانية من حيث هي ليست إلا الإنسانية .
It [the essence] is not itself of these things, nor is it internal to them, because it is possible to describe it with their opposite. And from this it is also known that it is not necessitating nor necessitated by any of the opposites determinately. His saying: '(lama amkana etc.)' – he meant by it rational possibility, i.e., because the intellect permits its description with its opposite; for the accident, whether it is a necessary concomitant of the essence or other, evident or non-evident, it is possible to conceive the essence without it, even if impossible, so it is possible to describe it with its opposite, unlike what is internal to it, for conceiving it without it is impossible, like the conceived. And the investigator Taftazani indicated this in his commentary on al-'Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya when he said: 'Unlike laughter and writing, for it is possible to conceive man without them, so they are accidents.' His saying: '(wa min hadha yu'lam etc.)' – i.e., from what we mentioned that those accidents are not for the essence in the rank of its essence, and that the intellect permits its description with each of the opposites, it is known that it in the rank of its essence is not necessitating of anything from them nor necessitated by them. And this does not contradict its necessitation of it by virtue of its existence absolutely, externally, or mentally. The commentator only mentioned this preamble, even though it has no relevance in explaining the distinction, as a prelude to what will come of explaining the meaning of advancing the particle of negation over the consideration and delaying it. So what the author of al-Maqasid said, that when it is said: 'Four is even or not odd', it is intended that that is from the necessary concomitants and necessities of the essence without consideration of existence, is nothing. How so? For if it were a necessity of the essence, it would necessitate it in the state of non-existence as well. His saying: ('ala al-ta'yin) – he qualified it with that because the speech is about that, not to indicate that it necessitates something of them not determinately, for that is false due to what has passed that humanity as such is nothing but humanity.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof regarding the essence's relation to its opposites. It begins by stating a conditional: if the essence were identical to or contained an opposite, it could not be described by the opposite; but since it can be described by opposites, it is neither identical nor containing. The commentator explains that this possibility is rational possibility—i.e., the intellect permits it—and distinguishes between accidents (whether necessary or not) and internal parts. For accidents, we can conceive the essence without them, so they can be described by opposites; for internal parts, we cannot. Then the second conclusion: from this we know the essence in its own rank does not necessitate any opposite. The commentator clarifies that this does not negate the fact that with existence it may necessitate something. He introduces this as a prelude to the later discussion about negation placement. He then refutes the view of the author of al-Maqasid who claimed that 'four is even or not odd' is a necessity of the essence regardless of existence; the commentator argues that if it were a necessity of the essence, it would hold even in non-existence. Finally, the qualification 'determinately' is explained: we are not saying the essence necessitates something non-determinately, which would be false because humanity as such is only humanity.

Key Terms
ماهية (essence)
متقابلات (opposites)
إمكان عقلي (rational possibility)
عارض (accident)
اقتضاء (necessitation)
لازم الماهية (necessary concomitant of the essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The essence in itself necessitates its attributes, such as fourness necessitating evenness.
Correction: The essence is indifferent to opposites; attributes become necessary only with existence, not in the essence itself.

Checkpoint
What is the correct statement about the essence in itself regarding opposites?
A) It is necessitating of one opposite.
B) It is not necessitating of any opposite determinately.
C) It is necessarily described by both opposites.
D) It is identical to its opposites.

Key Takeaway
The essence in itself is not necessitating of any opposite; only with existence do attributes like evenness become necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your own essence—your 'self'—necessarily has certain attributes (like intelligence, patience, or charisma) that you cannot change? How does this lesson's teaching that the essence in itself is indifferent to opposites challenge your view of yourself and your potential for change?
?
When you say 'I am like this' or 'this is just part of my nature,' are you treating a temporary accident as if it were a necessary part of your essence? What would it mean for you to separate your essence from your attributes in your daily life?
?
The author refutes the view that 'four is even' is a necessity of its essence even in non-existence. Are there aspects of your life—like your status, your family, or even your faith—that you treat as if they are necessary to your identity even when they are not? How can you distinguish between what is essential and what is accidental in your relationship with Allah?

Definition and Proof of the Abstracted Quiddity (Mujarrada)
Defines the abstracted quiddity and proves it cannot exist externally, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the abstracted quiddity (mujarrada) is, why it cannot exist externally, and how an objection regarding non-contradiction is resolved.

Core Idea
The abstracted quiddity is defined by freedom from attachments, and that very freedom prevents its external existence.

Sanusi defines a key concept in metaphysics: the quiddity taken with the condition of being free from all attachments, called the abstracted quiddity. He then proves that such a quiddity cannot exist externally and addresses a subtle objection involving the law of non-contradiction. This lesson unpacks his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: definition, proof, objection, answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the definition of mujarrada
Identify that the abstracted quiddity is taken with the condition of being free from all attachments (bisharṭ al-khuluw 'an al-lawāḥiq). This is a mental concept, not something existent in reality.
3
Follow the proof that it does not exist externally
The proof is by contradiction: if it existed externally, external existence and individuation would attach to it, violating its condition. Thus it cannot exist externally.
This is a key point in understanding the nature of universals.
4
Analyze the objection and its answer
The objection claims that if it is not existent, it might also not be non-existent, leading to violation of non-contradiction. The answer clarifies that freedom is only from positive attachments, not from existence/non-existence, so the objection fails.
The answer shows that the abstracted quiddity is impossible in reality, which is the intended conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أخذت الماهية بشرط الخلو عن اللواحق سميت مجردة وبشرط لا شيء، وأنها لا توجد في الخارج، وإلا لحقها الوجود الخارجي والتعين، فلم تكن مجردة عن جميع اللواحق كما فرضناه، هذا خلف. قوله : ( وإنها لا توجد في الخارج) وما قيل إنها لا تكون معدومة أيضاً، وإلا لحقها العدم فلا تكون موجودة ولا معدومة، فيلزم ارتفاع النقيضين واجتماعهما في الماهية المجردة، فليس بشيء، لأن المعتبر في المجردة الخلو بمعنى التقييد بعدم اللواحق كما مر فلا يمكن أن يعتبر فيه الخلو عن العدم؛ لأن التقييد بعدم العدم تقييد بوجود العوارض فتكون مخلوطة لا مجردة على أن ما ذكره يستلزم أن تكون ممتنعة الوجود لاستلزامها المحال وهو المطلوب.
And when the quiddity is taken with the condition of being free from attachments, it is called abstracted (mujarrada) and with the condition of not having anything, and that it does not exist in external reality; otherwise, external existence and individuation would attach to it, so it would not be abstracted from all attachments as we supposed; this is a contradiction. His statement: 'And that it does not exist in external reality'—and what was said that it would also not be non-existent, otherwise non-existence would attach to it, so it would be neither existent nor non-existent; thus there would be a violation of the two contradictories and their conjunction in the abstracted quiddity. So it is nothing, because what is considered in the abstracted is freedom in the sense of being restricted by not having attachments, as passed, so it is not possible to consider in it freedom from non-existence, because restriction by non-existence of non-existence is a restriction by the existence of accidents, so it would be mixed (makhluṭa), not abstracted. Moreover, what he mentioned necessitates that it be impossible in existence due to entailing the impossible, which is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines the abstracted quiddity (mujarrada) and provides a proof that it cannot exist in external reality, along with an objection and its answer. First, the definition: when the quiddity is taken with the condition (bi-sharṭ) of being free from all attachments, it is called abstracted. The condition is that it has no attachments at all. The proof that it does not exist externally is as follows: if it did exist externally, then external existence and individuation (al-tashakhkhuṣ) would attach to it, which violates the condition of being free from all attachments. Hence, it cannot exist externally. Then an objection is raised: if the abstracted is not existent externally, perhaps it would also not be non-existent, because non-existence is also an attachment; but then it would be neither existent nor non-existent, and that would entail a violation of the law of non-contradiction (raising both contradictories and their conjunction). The answer clarifies that the freedom from attachments in the abstracted is by way of restriction to the absence of attachments, not restriction to the absence of non-existence. Restriction to the absence of non-existence would itself be a restriction to the existence of accidents, making it mixed (makhluṭa), not abstracted. Thus the objection fails. Furthermore, the argument actually shows that the abstracted quiddity, if taken in this way, would be impossible (mumtani'a) because the conflation of existence and non-existence is absurd. That is exactly what the proponent of the objection wanted to prove: that the abstracted as such is impossible. So the supposed objection backfires.

Key Terms
ماهية مجردة (mujarrada quiddity)
بشرط الخلو عن اللواحق (conditioned by freedom from attachments)
ارتفاع النقيضين (violation of two contradictories)
مخلوطة (mixed quiddity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the abstracted quiddity, because it is not existent externally, must be non-existent, and if it is also not non-existent, then it violates the law of non-contradiction.
Correction: The abstracted quiddity is defined with freedom only from positive attachments (like accidents), not from existence or non-existence. Therefore, it can be non-existent without contradiction. The objection incorrectly assumes freedom from non-existence is part of the definition.

Checkpoint
Why does the proof argue that the abstracted quiddity (mujarrada) cannot exist in external reality?
A) Because external existence is an attachment that would make it mixed
B) Because it cannot be conceived in the mind
C) Because it would require a composite in the mind
D) Because it would be non-existent and existent simultaneously

Key Takeaway
The abstracted quiddity is a mental concept defined by freedom from attachments, and its very definition entails its nonexistence in external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that the 'abstracted you'—your essence stripped of all attachments—cannot exist in reality. What attachments do you cling to in order to define yourself? If you removed them, would anything remain? Are you willing to live without that mental construct?
?
The proof concludes that the abstracted quiddity is impossible. Is there an ideal in your life—a perfect state, a pure concept—that you strive for, but which, upon reflection, is impossible to realize? How does holding onto that ideal affect your actions and contentment?
?
The objection attempted to salvage the abstracted quiddity but only proved its impossibility. When you face a belief that seems comforting but contradicts clear reasoning, do you have the courage to follow the argument where it leads, even if it shatters a cherished notion?

The Abstracted Quiddity and Mental Existence
Presents two opinions on mental existence of the abstracted and the commentator's solution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opinions on whether the abstracted quiddity has mental existence and how the commentator resolves the debate by distinguishing between the abstracted in itself and as considered by the mind.

Core Idea
The abstracted quiddity exists in the mind only as a mental concept, not as a real entity.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic logic and metaphysics: can the abstracted quiddity (mujarrada) exist in the mind? Two opposing views are given, followed by an objection and a nuanced solution. The discussion hinges on the distinction between what the mind can conceive and what exists independently.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Get a sense of the debate before analyzing details.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find the arguments for existence and non-existence. Note how each uses reasoning about accidents and the mind's capacity.
The first opinion denies existence; the second affirms it.
3
Examine the objection
The author of al-Maqāṣid objects that the second opinion only shows a non-corresponding conception. Understand why this is a problem.
4
Understand the commentator's solution
The commentator distinguishes between the abstracted in itself (impossible) and the abstracted as considered by the mind (possible). This resolves the debate.
The analogy of the absolutely non-existent helps clarify.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
Consider why this distinction matters for the overall project of understanding quiddities and mental existence.
This distinction avoids conflating mental conception with real existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهل توجد المجردة في الذهن عند القائل بالوجود الذهني؟ قيل: لا توجد؛ لأن وجودها في الذهن من العوارض واللواحق، فلا تكون مجردة عن جميعها كالموجود الخارجي. وقيل: توجد لأن الذهن يمكنه تصور كل شيء حتى عدم نفسه، ولا حجر في التصورات أصلاً، فلا يمتنع أن يبين أنه لا يحتاج في إثبات وجود الماهية المطلقة أيضاً إلى القول بالتركيب المذكور. قوله : ( وقيل : توجد لأن الذهن إلخ رد عليه صاحب المقاصد بأن هذا لا يقتضي كونها مجردة، بل غاية الأمر أن العقل تصورها كذلك تصوراً غير مطابق، فإن قيل: لا معنى للمأخوذ بشرط لا شيء سوى ما يعتبره العقل كذلك، قلنا : فحينئذ لا يمتنع وجوده في الخارج بأن يكون مقروناً بالعوارض والمشخصات ويعتبر العقل مجرداً عن ذلك، فصار الحاصل أنه إن أريد بالمجرد مالا يكون في نفسه مقروناً بشيء من العوارض امتنع وجوده في الخارج والذهن جميعاً، وإن أريد ما يعتبره العقل كذلك جاز وجوده فيهما. وقد أشار الشارح إلى جوابه بما حاصله أنه يعقل الذهن الماهية المجردة عن جميع اللواحق الخارجية والذهنية بأن يعتبر معراة عنها ويلاحظها كذلك، وإن كانت بحسب نفس الأمر متصفة ببعضها . ألا ترى أنه يمكنه الحكم على المجردة مطلقاً باستحالة الوجود في الخارج، ولا حكم على شيء إلا بعد تصوره ويقرب من هذا ما قيل من أن المعدوم مطلقاً أي خارجاً وذهناً قد يتصور، فيعرض له الوجود الذهني، فيكون قسماً من الموجود المطلق باعتبار وجوده في الذهن، وقسيماً له باعتبار ذاته ،ومفهومه فكذلك إذا تصورت المجردة مطلقاً كانت من حيث ذاتها ومفهومها مجردة ومقابلة للمخلوطة ومن حيث وجودها
And does the abstracted exist in the mind according to the proponent of mental existence? It is said: It does not exist, because its existence in the mind is from among the accidents and attachments, so it would not be abstracted from all of them like the externally existent. And it is said: It exists, because the mind can conceive everything, even its own non-existence, and there is no obstacle in conceptions at all, so there is no impediment to showing that in proving the existence of the absolute quiddity also one does not need to affirm the aforementioned composition. His statement: 'And it is said: It exists because the mind, etc.' The author of al-Maqāṣid responded that this does not entail it being abstracted; rather, the most that can be said is that the intellect conceives it as such, a conception that does not correspond to reality. If it is said: There is no meaning for 'taken with the condition of not having anything' other than what the intellect considers as such, we say: In that case, its existence in external reality is not impossible, as it could be accompanied by accidents and individuators and the intellect considers it abstracted from that. So the conclusion is that if by 'abstracted' is meant that which in itself is not accompanied by any accidents, its existence in external reality and the mind is impossible. But if what is meant is that which the intellect considers as such, its existence in both is possible. And the commentator (al-Shāriḥ) indicated his answer, the summary of which is that the intellect conceives the abstracted quiddity, free from all external and mental attachments, by considering it stripped of them and observing it thus, even if in itself it is qualified by some of them. Do you not see that it can judge about the abstracted in absolute terms that it is impossible to exist in external reality, and there is no judgment about something without conceiving it. And close to this is what was said: that the absolutely non-existent, i.e., externally and mentally, can be conceived, and then mental existence attaches to it, so it becomes a part of absolute existence by virtue of its existence in the mind, and a counterpart of it by virtue of its essence and concept. So also when you conceive the abstracted in absolute terms, it is, in terms of its essence and concept, abstracted and opposed to the mixed, and in terms of its existence...
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the debate on whether the abstracted quiddity (mujarrada) has mental existence. Two opinions are given: (1) It does not exist in the mind because mental existence is an accident, which would violate abstraction. (2) It does exist because the mind can conceive anything, even non-existence, so there is no barrier to conceiving the abstracted. The author of al-Maqāṣid (a reference) objects that the second opinion does not prove that the abstracted exists as a genuine entity; it only shows that the mind conceives it, but that conception is non-corresponding to reality. The commentator (al-Shāriḥ) then provides a solution: The intellect can conceive the abstracted quiddity by considering it stripped of all attachments, as a mental concept, even if in itself it has attachments. This is analogous to conceiving the absolutely non-existent, which, when conceived, gains mental existence. Hence, the abstracted exists in the mind as a considered concept (bi al-iʿtibār), but that does not imply it exists externally. The distinction is between the abstracted in itself (which is impossible externally) and the abstracted as considered by the mind (which can exist in the mind as an idea).

Key Terms
وجود ذهني (mental existence)
تصور غير مطابق (non-corresponding conception)
اعتبار (consideration, iʿtibār)
معدوم مطلق (absolutely non-existent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the abstracted can be conceived, it must have real mental existence corresponding to reality.
Correction: The text clarifies that the conception is by way of consideration (iʿtibār) and is non-corresponding; i.e., the quiddity is not actually in the mind as an independent entity.

Checkpoint

B) Distinction between the abstracted in itself and the abstracted as considered by the intellect
C) Distinction between genuine and imaginary existence
D) Distinction between necessary and contingent existence

Key Takeaway
The abstracted quiddity can be mentally conceived only as a considered concept, not as an independently existing entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How might confusing these two lead to errors in your worldview? Commit to examining one such concept today and clarifying whether it exists only in your mind or has real external instantiation.
?
The text shows that the mind can conceive even its own non-existence. Have you ever pondered the nature of your own existence and non-existence? What does this ability reveal about the limitations of your intellect? Will you take five minutes today to reflect on the act of thinking itself and what it presupposes?
?
Do you ever assume that your mental conceptions of Allah's attributes or other metaphysical ideas correspond exactly to reality? How does the distinction between 'abstracted in itself' and 'abstracted by consideration' challenge you to be more humble about your knowledge? The Abstracted Quiddity as a Considered Concept
Explains the abstracted quiddity as a considered concept and defends its role in judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the abstracted quiddity is a considered concept (iʿtibārī) that nonetheless allows true judgments, and how this defends against objections about its existence in the mind.

Core Idea
The abstracted quiddity is a concept that exists in the mind through consideration, not as an external entity, yet it grounds true judgments.

The commentator explains the nature of the abstracted quiddity as a concept that becomes established in the mind after being considered. This passage clarifies that such concepts are like hypothetical matters and can be subjects of true judgments, addressing a key objection from al-Maqasid. It also relates to the principle that no judgment is possible without conception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the definition, then the objection, then the response.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the difference between real concepts and hypotheticals
The passage states that real concepts are established without regard to consideration, while hypotheticals (like the abstracted) are established through consideration. This is key to understanding the status of the abstracted.
Think about why this distinction matters for truth.
3
Understand the response to the objection
The objection was that the abstracted would have only hypothetical existence not corresponding to reality. The commentator argues that the discussion is about existence according to reality (bi hasab nafs al-amr) and that the restriction to absence of accidents occurs only by mental consideration.
This shows the subtlety of mental existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( بأن يعتبرها معراة إلخ) ثم بعد اعتبارها كذلك تكون مفهوماً من المفهومات الثابتة في نفس الأمر، فتكون الماهية المجردة بعد اعتبارها مفهوماً ثابتاً في نفس الأمر، كسائر الأمور الفرضية بعد اعتبارها ولذا تجري عليها الأحكام الصادقة، ولا أقل من كونها مفهومات اعتبارية، إنما الفرق بينها وبين سائر المفهومات الثابتة في نفس الأمر أنها ثابتة من قطع النظر عن الاعتبار، والفرضيات ثابتة بتوسط الاعتبار، فاندفع ما قاله صاحب المقاصد من أن اللازم مما ذكره هذا القائل وجود المجردة في الذهن وجوداً فرضياً غير مطابق لنفس الأمر، والكلام في وجودها في الذهن بحسب نفس الأمر، ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن الكلام في وجودها في الذهن مع قطع النظر عن الاعتبار، لأن التقييد بعدم العوارض لا يكون إلا باعتبار الذهن. قوله : ( ولا حكم على شيء إلخ ) وهذا الحكم صادق، فلا بد من وجود المجردة في الذهن بحسب نفس الأمر. قوله : ( ويقرب من (هذا لاشتراكهما في أن المقابلة والقسمية باعتبار الجهتين، وافتراقهما بأن المانع في المعدوم المطلق من الوجود في نفس الأمر العدم المطلق وها هنا التجرد . قوله : ( أن المعدوم مطلقاً) أي مفهومه وذاته المتصف بمفهومه فرضاً بقرينة قوله : باعتبار ذاته ومفهومه . قوله : (قد يتصور إلخ) أما مفهومه فينفسه، وأما ذاته فباعتبار هذا المفهوم. قوله : ( وقسيماً له إلخ) أما ذاته فباعتبار صدق مفهومه، وأما مفهومه فبنفسه. قوله : (كانت من حيث ذاتها ومفهومها (مجردة أما من حيث ذاتها فظاهر، وأما من حيث لا معنى للموجود في الذهن إلا ما تصوره العقل أعم من أن يكون ذلك التصور مطابقاً للواقع أم لا، فنحن لا ندعي سوى أن المجردة قد تكون متصورة للعقل مفروضة له، وأما أن ذلك الفرض مطابق للواقع فنحن لا ندعيه بل نعترف بأنه خلاف الواقع. وهو قوله : ( ولا حكم على شيء إلا بعد تصوره فيه بحث أشرنا إليه في بحث الوجود الذهني، أنه يكفي في التصور للحكم حصول المحكوم عليه إجمالاً بواسطة أمر عارض له، وهو المرتسم والموجود في الذهن حقيقة، فلا يلزم من الحكم على الماهية المجردة وتصورها لأجل ذلك الحكم وجودها في الذهن، كما يدل عليه سياق كلامه، فليتأمل.
His statement: 'By considering it stripped etc.' Then, after considering it thus, it becomes a concept among the concepts established in themselves (fī nafs al-amr). Thus the abstracted quiddity, after being considered, is a concept established in themselves, like other hypothetical matters (al-umūr al-farḍiyya) after their consideration; therefore true judgments apply to it. It is at least a conceptual concept (mafhūm iʿtibārī). The only difference between it and other concepts established in themselves is that those are established without regard to consideration, while hypotheticals are established through consideration. So what the author of al-Maqāṣid said is repelled—that what follows from what this proponent mentioned is the existence of the abstracted in the mind a hypothetical existence not corresponding to reality, while the discussion is about its existence in the mind according to reality (bi ḥasab nafs al-amr). And it cannot be said that the discussion is about its existence in the mind without regard to consideration, because restriction to the absence of accidents occurs only by the mind's consideration. His statement: 'And there is no judgment about something except after conceiving it'—and this judgment is true, so there must be existence of the abstracted in the mind according to reality. His statement: 'And it is similar to this because both share in the opposition and division being in terms of two aspects, but they differ in that the impediment in the absolutely non-existent from existing in reality is absolute non-existence, while here it is abstraction.' His statement: 'The absolutely non-existent' means its concept and its essence qualified by its concept hypothetically, as indicated by his phrase 'considering its essence and its concept.' His statement: 'It can be conceived etc.' As for its concept, it is in itself; as for its essence, it is through considering this concept. His statement: 'And a counterpart of it etc.' As for its essence, it is by virtue of the truth of its concept; as for its concept, it is in itself. His statement: 'It is, in terms of its essence and its concept, abstracted'—as for its essence, it is clear; as for its concept, there is no meaning of 'existent in the mind' except what the intellect conceives, regardless of whether that conception corresponds to reality or not. We do not claim except that the abstracted may be conceived by the intellect and hypothesized for it, but we do not claim that that hypothesis corresponds to reality; rather we admit that it is contrary to reality. And his statement: 'And there is no judgment about something except after conceiving it' contains a discussion we have pointed to in the investigation of mental existence: that for the conception required for judgment, it is sufficient that the object of judgment be obtained in sum through some accidental thing, which is the mental image (al-murtasim) and what exists in the mind truly. Therefore, from the judgment about the abstracted quiddity and its conception for the sake of that judgment, it does not follow that it exists in the mind, as the context of his words indicates, so one should reflect.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary defending the status of the abstracted quiddity as a considered concept (iʿtibārī) that nevertheless allows true judgments. First, it explains that after being considered by the mind, the abstracted quiddity becomes a concept established in itself (fī nafs al-amr), similar to hypothetical matters (al-umūr al-farḍiyya). The difference is that real concepts are established without regard to consideration, while hypotheticals (which include the abstracted as a mental concept) are established through consideration. This refutes the objection of the author of al-Maqāṣid that the abstracted would have only a hypothetical existence non-corresponding to reality. The commentator argues that the discussion is precisely about existence according to reality, and that the restriction to absence of accidents occurs only by mental consideration; hence the abstracted's existence in the mind is real in the sense of being a considered concept. Then the commentator addresses the principle that 'there is no judgment about something without conceiving it,' which is used to argue that the abstracted must exist in the mind in order to be judged. He acknowledges this, but clarifies that the existence in the mind required for judgment is not the same as the quiddity itself existing as an independent entity; rather, it is sufficient that the object of judgment be represented through a mental image (murtasim) that truly exists in the mind. Thus, from the judgment about the abstracted, it does not follow that the abstracted quiddity itself exists in the mind as a real entity; only its concept does. The passage ends with a call for reflection, indicating the subtlety of the issue.

Key Terms
اعتبار
أمور فرضية
وجود ذهني
مرتسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the abstracted quiddity is a concept in the mind, it must exist as a mental entity corresponding to an external reality.
Correction: The text clarifies it is a considered concept that exists only in the mind as a product of consideration, not as a self-subsistent entity.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how does the abstracted quiddity have a status that allows true judgments?

B) It is a considered concept (iʿtibārī) that is established in itself through mental consideration
C) It is a real mental entity like physical objects
D) It is merely an illusion with no basis

Key Takeaway
The abstracted quiddity is a valid considered concept that exists in the mind through mental consideration, enabling true judgments without requiring external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says the abstracted quiddity is a considered concept that 'exists' in the mind only through consideration. Think of a belief you hold that is based on a concept you have never seen in reality (e.g., 'angels' or 'the afterlife'). Is your faith in that belief anchored in a mental image (murtasim) that truly exists in your mind, or in the object itself? Which one gives you confidence?

The passage ends with 'so one should reflect.' What in your own thinking about abstract theological concepts (like Allah's attributes) have you taken for granted as real, when they might only be considered concepts? Are you willing to examine your assumptions and ask whether your mental images are sufficient for true knowledge?

Plato's Proof from Receptivity to Opposites
Plato's proof and initial commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Plato's argument that man's receptivity to opposites proves his abstract nature, and how the commentator clarifies the reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
Receptivity to opposites shows that the self must be abstract and free from those opposites.

Before diving into objections, it is essential to grasp the proof itself. Plato argues that because man can receive opposite attributes, his essential self must be free from all such opposites. This lesson presents the proof and its foundational logic as explained by the commentator.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the opening sentence of the passage—the proof that man is receptive to opposites and thus abstract. Note the logical structure.
2
Understand the commentator's explanation
The commentator explains that the proof first establishes receptivity, then derives abstraction and individuality. Pay attention to how he unpacks each part.
3
Clarify key terms
Note the commentator's clarification: 'receptive' means in external existence, and 'abstraction' is from the whole of opposites.
4
Consider the preliminary critique
The commentator already hints at a flaw: if 'all opposites' is meant, it's false; if 'some', then abstraction from all is not proven. This foreshadows the objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج عليه : بأن الإنسان قابل للمتقابلات، وإلا لم تعرض له فيكون في نفسه مجرداً عن الكل. قوله : ( واحتج إلخ ) لما كان قبوله للمتقابلات أصلاً لجميع القيود المعتبرة في الدعوى تعرض أولاً لإثباته، ثم فرع عليه بأن تجرده وفرديته لازم منه لأن المجردة فرد للمطلقة وكذا الأزلية والأبدية. قوله : ( بأن الإنسان ) قابل أي في الخارج فثبت وجوده. قوله : ( وإلا لم يعرض له ) فيه أنه إن أراد عروض جميع المتقابلات فممنوع، وإن أراد بعضها فلا يثبت تجرده عن كلها. قوله : ( واحتج عليه بأن الإنسان إلخ ) فيه بحث : أما أولاً : فلان هذا الاحتجاج على تقدير تمامه إنما يدل على التجرد عن العوارض المفارقة لا عن لوازم الماهية وبهذا القدر لا يثبت التجرد الذي نحن بصدده، وأما ثانياً : فلان الفردية بعض المدعى فلا دليل عليه، وأما ثالثاً : فلان الكل؛ لأن ما يكون معروضاً لبعضها يستحيل أن يكون قابلاً لما يقابله.
And he argued against it: that man is receptive to opposites, otherwise opposites would not occur to him, so he is in himself abstracted from the whole. His statement: 'and he argued, etc.' – since his receptivity to opposites is the basis of all the conditions considered in the claim, he first proceeded to establish it, then he derived from it that his abstraction and individuality are necessary from it, because the abstract is an individual of the absolute, and likewise eternity and perpetuity. His statement: 'that man is receptive' meaning in external existence, so his existence is established. His statement: 'otherwise opposites would not occur to him' – in this there is a discussion: if he meant the occurrence of all opposites, it is denied; and if he meant some of them, then his abstraction from all of them is not established. His statement: 'he argued against it that man, etc.' – there is a discussion in it: firstly, because this argument, assuming its completeness, only indicates abstraction from accidental attributes, not from the essential accidents of the quiddity, and to this extent it does not establish the abstraction that we are concerned with; secondly, because individuality is part of the claim, and there is no proof for it; thirdly, because the whole: since what is receptive to some of them is impossible to be receptive to their opposite.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage contains Plato's proof and the commentator's critical analysis. Plato argues: man is receptive to opposites (e.g., hot and cold); if he were not receptive, opposites would not occur to him; therefore, man in himself must be abstracted from all opposites. The commentary first explains the structure of the proof: it establishes receptivity, then derives abstraction and individuality. Then the commentator raises three objections. The first objection: the proof only shows abstraction from accidental (مفارقة) attributes, not from essential (لازمة) attributes of the quiddity, so it does not prove the full abstraction required. The second objection: individuality is part of Plato's claim (that there exists an individual man), but the proof does not demonstrate that this abstract entity is an individual. The third objection: if something is receptive to some opposites, it cannot be receptive to the opposite of those, so the argument fails because the claim involves all opposites. The commentator thus systematically undermines Plato's reasoning.

Key Terms
قابل للمتقابلات
تجرد
فردية
مجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the proof primarily aim to establish?
A) Man's existence
B) Man's abstraction and individuality
C) Man's receptivity to opposites
D) Man's eternity

Key Takeaway
Plato’s proof hinges on the idea that receptivity to opposites necessitates an abstract self, but the commentator questions the scope of that receptivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence is ‘abstract from all opposites,’ as the proof suggests, then your true self is not defined by any of your changing moods or circumstances. What would need to change in how you live today if you truly believed that?
?
The proof relies on the idea that you are receptive to opposites—you can feel both joy and sorrow, both knowledge and ignorance. But do you live as though your worth depends on one side of these opposites rather than on the unchanging receptor itself? What practical step can you take today to realign your focus?
?
The commentator doubts that man is receptive to all opposites. Consider a quality you think you could never have (e.g., genuine humility if you are proud). If you are not receptive to it, does that mean you are not fully abstract? What does that reveal about the work still needed on your soul?

Three Objections to the Proof
Three objections to Plato's proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key objections raised by the commentator against Plato's proof from receptivity to opposites.

Core Idea
The proof only covers accidental attributes, fails to prove individuality, and self-destructs if taken universally.

After presenting the proof, the commentator turns to a critical examination. He identifies three fundamental flaws: the proof conflates different types of accidents, does not prove individuality, and is self-contradictory. This lesson will unpack each objection in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection
The first objection: the proof only indicates abstraction from accidental attributes, not essential attributes of the quiddity.
2
Read the second objection
The second objection: individuality is part of the claim but not proven by the argument.
3
Read the third objection
The third objection: if something is receptive to some opposites, it cannot be receptive to their opposites, making the claim of universal receptivity impossible.
4
Reflect on the cumulative critique
Consider how these three objections together show the proof is insufficient for establishing the desired abstraction.

Refutation of Plato's Claim: The Abstract and the Individual
The author refutes Plato's claim that the abstract exists externally and that an individual can be receptive to opposite individuations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Plato's claim about the abstract existing and an individual being receptive to opposite individuations is necessarily false.

Core Idea
The abstract cannot exist externally, and no individual can bear opposites simultaneously.

Plato argued that the abstract (المجرد) exists and that an individual man is receptive to opposites like Zayd and 'Amr. The author refutes both points, showing they lead to absurdities. This lesson examines the core refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand the author's argument against Plato.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Notice the author first denies the existence of the abstract externally, then denies that an individual can be receptive to opposite individuations.
3
Analyze the necessity of impossibility
Understand why the author says the claim is 'necessarily false' (ضروري البطلان). This is a logical impossibility.
4
Consider the commentator's elucidations
The commentary explains the quiddity's receptivity by alternation vs. combination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت قد علمت أن المجرد لا وجود له في الخارج، بل يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً فيه، فهذا المدعى باطل قطعاً. و علمت أيضاً أن القابل للمتقابلات الماهية من حيث هي هي فإنها في حد ذاتها قابلة للاتصاف بكل واحدة منها بدلاً عن الآخر، فالماهية المقارنة للتشخصات المتقابلة. وأما وجود فرد من الماهية الإنسانية يكون ذلك الفرد قابلاً لزيد وعمرو أي لتشخصهما كما يدل عليه كلامه فضروري البطلان؛ لاستحالة أن يكون الواحد المعين متصفاً بالصفات المتقابلة في زمان واحد. وكذا إن أراد بفرد منها الماهية المقيدة بقيد التجرد، فإن اقتران المجرد بالقيود التي اعتبر تجريده عنها ضروري البطلان أيضاً، فظهر أن دليله غير واف بما. قوله : ( لأن ما يكون معروضاً ) أي في نفسه. قوله : (فهذا المدعى باطل إلخ) يعني أن دعواه بديهي الاستحالة لا يليق أن يسمع، فقوله : علمت أن المجرد لا وجود له في الحقيقة معارضة رتب الشارح قدس سره عليها بطلان الدعوى للاستظهار. قوله : (فإنها في حد ذاتها إلخ) الماهية في حد ذاتها لما لم تكن إلا الماهية كان قبولها للمتقابلات بطريق البدلية، وأما في مرتبة الوجود فهي قابلة لها بطريق الاجتماع؛ لكونها مع الوجود موجودة، ومع العدم معدومة، ومع الوحدة واحدة، ومع الكثرة كثيرة. قوله : ( فالماهية الإنسانية إلخ) زاده الشارح قدس سره ليرتبط قوله : وأما وجود فرد إلخ. قوله : ( أي لتشخصهما ) فالكلام على حذف المضاف، وإنما قال : لتشخصهما مع أن قبوله لتشخص واحد أيضاً محال؛ لأن الكلام في قبول المتقابلات. قوله : ( وكذا إن أراد إلخ) أي ما ذكر من كونه ضروري البطلان على تقدير إرادته بالفرد معناه المتعارف أي معروض التشخص ؛ وإن كان ذلك خروجاً عما نحن فيه، وإن أراد به الماهية المجردة بناء على أنه فرد للمطلقة فهو أيضاً ضروري البطلان. نعم الإنسان قابل للعدم كما هو قابل لسائر عوارضه المتقابلة فيوجب الدليل على تقدير تمامه تجرده عن عوارض الوجود أيضاً، فكيف يحكم بمقارنته لهذا العارض أعني الوجود وتجرده عن العوارض البتة. وقد يقال : الظاهر من كلام أفلاطون أن مراده الحكم بوجود الكلي الطبيعي، فمعنى كلامه أن الماهية من حيث هي أزلية أبدية بقرينة دليله وقوله في المدعى : قابل للمتقابلات إلا أنه تمحل في إطلاق الفرد على الماهية على تقدير تحقق هذا الإطلاق في كلامه بمعنى أنها طبيعة واحدة، وفي التجرد بمعنى أن شيئاً من العوارض ليست نفس الماهية ولا جزءاً منها، وحينئذ يكون دليله وارداً على مدعاه غايته أنه يرد عليه ما ورد على القائلين بوجود الطبائع.
And you already know that the abstract has no existence in the external world, rather it is impossible for it to exist there, so this claim is absolutely false. And you also know that the receptive of opposites is the quiddity in itself, for it in its essence is receptive to being described with each one of them alternatively. So the quiddity accompanying opposing individuations. As for the existence of an individual of the human quiddity such that that individual is receptive to Zayd and 'Amr, i.e., to their individuations, as his statement indicates, its falsity is necessary, because it is impossible for one determinate thing to be qualified with opposite attributes at one time. Likewise, if he meant by an individual of it the quiddity restricted by the condition of abstraction, then the conjunction of the abstract with the conditions from which it was assumed to be abstracted is also necessarily false. So it appears that his proof is not sufficient for... His statement: 'because that which is receptive' i.e., in itself. His statement: 'so this claim is false, etc.' means that his claim is of obvious impossibility, not worthy to be heard. So his statement 'you know that the abstract has no existence in reality' is an objection that the commentator arranged upon it the falsity of the claim for emphasis. His statement: 'for it in its essence, etc.' – the quiddity in its essence, since it is nothing but the quiddity, its receptivity to opposites is by way of alternation; but in the realm of existence, it is receptive to them by way of combination, because with existence it exists, with non-existence it is non-existent, with unity it is one, with multiplicity it is many. His statement: 'so the human quiddity, etc.' – the commentator added it to connect his statement 'as for the existence of an individual, etc.'. His statement: 'i.e., to their individuations' – the expression involves deletion of the object; he said 'to their individuations' even though its receptivity to a single individuation is also impossible, because the discussion is about receptivity to opposites. His statement: 'and likewise if he meant, etc.' – i.e., what was mentioned of its necessary falsity assuming he meant by 'individual' its common meaning, i.e., the bearer of individuation; even if that is a departure from our context; and if he meant by it the abstract quiddity, based on it being an individual of the absolute, it is also necessarily false. Yes, man is receptive to non-existence just as he is receptive to the rest of his opposite accidents, so the proof, assuming its completeness, would require his abstraction from the accidents of existence as well. Then how can he judge that he is concomitant with this accident, namely existence, and yet abstracted from accidents entirely? And it may be said: The apparent meaning of Plato's speech is that his intention is to judge the existence of the natural universal. So the meaning of his speech is that the quiddity in itself is eternal and perpetual, by the evidence of his proof and his statement in the claim 'receptive to opposites', except that he engages in artificiality in applying 'individual' to the quiddity, on the assumption that this application occurs in his speech, meaning that it is a single nature, and 'abstracted' meaning that none of the accidents are the quiddity itself nor a part of it. Then his proof is valid for his claim, except that it is subject to the same objections as those who affirm the existence of the universals.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This is a refutational and reconciliatory passage. The author begins by asserting that the abstract (مجرد) cannot exist externally, so Plato's claim is false. He then clarifies that the true subject receptive to opposites is the quiddity in itself, which can receive opposites alternately, not a concrete individual. The impossibility of an individual being receptive to opposite individuations (like Zayd and 'Amr) is stressed. Further, if Plato meant the quiddity restricted by abstraction, that is self-contradictory because abstracting from conditions while retaining them is impossible. The commentator then explains each phrase: the quiddity in itself can receive opposites either alternately (in essence) or simultaneously (in existence, e.g., existence/non-existence, unity/multiplicity). The added comment about the human quiddity ties the argument. The discussion then addresses that if the proof were sound, it would also require abstraction from existence, contradicting the assumption of existence. Finally, a reconciliation is offered: Plato may have intended the natural universal (الكلي الطبيعي), meaning the quiddity in itself is eternal and receptive to opposites, but his terminology of 'individual' and 'abstract' is forced. His proof would then be valid but faces the standard objections against the existence of universals.

Key Terms
المجرد
الماهية من حيث هي هي
قابل للمتقابلات
الواحد المعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Plato's argument is plausible because the concept of a universal seems abstract and existing.
Correction: The author shows that the abstract has no external existence and that concrete individuals cannot possess opposite attributes simultaneously.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say it is impossible for one determinate thing to be receptive to opposite individuations?
A) Because opposites cannot exist together
B) Because one thing cannot have opposite attributes at the same time
C) Because quiddity is not receptive
D) Because Plato was mistaken

Key Takeaway
Plato's claim about the abstract and the individual is necessarily false because the abstract cannot exist externally and a concrete individual cannot simultaneously possess opposite attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your own life do you try to hold onto contradictory desires or identities — wanting to be both spiritually abstracted from the world yet fully engaged in its pleasures? How does this lesson's refutation of combining opposites expose the impossibility of such a stance?
?
The author argues that the abstract has no external existence. Where in your daily practice of faith do you treat 'abstract ideals' as if they exist independently, without grounding them in concrete action? What one concrete step can you take today to embody an ideal you believe in?
?
If it is impossible for a single individual to be receptive to opposite individuations, then you cannot be a true servant of Allah while simultaneously serving your ego. Which opposite 'individuation' — servitude to Allah or to your desires — do you find yourself trying to combine, and what must you eliminate to be a determinate believer?

Reconciling Plato's Claim with the Natural Universal
The author reconciles Plato's claim by interpreting it as a statement about the natural universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Plato's claim can be reinterpreted as referring to the natural universal (الكلي الطبيعي) and the problems that still remain.

Core Idea
Plato may have meant the quiddity in itself as the natural universal, but his terminology is forced.

The author offers a conciliatory interpretation: Plato might have intended the natural universal, which is the quiddity in itself considered as a universal. This lesson explores that interpretation and the objections it still faces.

Guided Path
1
Read the reconciliation passage
Read the Arabic passage where the author suggests Plato meant the natural universal.
2
Understand the reinterpretation
Plato's claim is taken as affirming the existence of the quiddity in itself as a universal, which is eternal and perpetual.
3
Identify the forced terminology
Note how 'individual' and 'abstract' are used artificially to mean quiddity and freedom from accidents.
4
Consider the remaining objections
Even if reinterpreted, the claim faces the same objections as those who affirm the existence of universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت قد علمت أن المجرد لا وجود له في الخارج، بل يمتنع أن يكون موجوداً فيه، فهذا المدعى باطل قطعاً. و علمت أيضاً أن القابل للمتقابلات الماهية من حيث هي هي فإنها في حد ذاتها قابلة للاتصاف بكل واحدة منها بدلاً عن الآخر، فالماهية المقارنة للتشخصات المتقابلة. وأما وجود فرد من الماهية الإنسانية يكون ذلك الفرد قابلاً لزيد وعمرو أي لتشخصهما كما يدل عليه كلامه فضروري البطلان؛ لاستحالة أن يكون الواحد المعين متصفاً بالصفات المتقابلة في زمان واحد. وكذا إن أراد بفرد منها الماهية المقيدة بقيد التجرد، فإن اقتران المجرد بالقيود التي اعتبر تجريده عنها ضروري البطلان أيضاً، فظهر أن دليله غير واف بما. قوله : ( لأن ما يكون معروضاً ) أي في نفسه. قوله : (فهذا المدعى باطل إلخ) يعني أن دعواه بديهي الاستحالة لا يليق أن يسمع، فقوله : علمت أن المجرد لا وجود له في الحقيقة معارضة رتب الشارح قدس سره عليها بطلان الدعوى للاستظهار. قوله : (فإنها في حد ذاتها إلخ) الماهية في حد ذاتها لما لم تكن إلا الماهية كان قبولها للمتقابلات بطريق البدلية، وأما في مرتبة الوجود فهي قابلة لها بطريق الاجتماع؛ لكونها مع الوجود موجودة، ومع العدم معدومة، ومع الوحدة واحدة، ومع الكثرة كثيرة. قوله : ( فالماهية الإنسانية إلخ) زاده الشارح قدس سره ليرتبط قوله : وأما وجود فرد إلخ. قوله : ( أي لتشخصهما ) فالكلام على حذف المضاف، وإنما قال : لتشخصهما مع أن قبوله لتشخص واحد أيضاً محال؛ لأن الكلام في قبول المتقابلات. قوله : ( وكذا إن أراد إلخ) أي ما ذكر من كونه ضروري البطلان على تقدير إرادته بالفرد معناه المتعارف أي معروض التشخص ؛ وإن كان ذلك خروجاً عما نحن فيه، وإن أراد به الماهية المجردة بناء على أنه فرد للمطلقة فهو أيضاً ضروري البطلان. نعم الإنسان قابل للعدم كما هو قابل لسائر عوارضه المتقابلة فيوجب الدليل على تقدير تمامه تجرده عن عوارض الوجود أيضاً، فكيف يحكم بمقارنته لهذا العارض أعني الوجود وتجرده عن العوارض البتة. وقد يقال : الظاهر من كلام أفلاطون أن مراده الحكم بوجود الكلي الطبيعي، فمعنى كلامه أن الماهية من حيث هي أزلية أبدية بقرينة دليله وقوله في المدعى : قابل للمتقابلات إلا أنه تمحل في إطلاق الفرد على الماهية على تقدير تحقق هذا الإطلاق في كلامه بمعنى أنها طبيعة واحدة، وفي التجرد بمعنى أن شيئاً من العوارض ليست نفس الماهية ولا جزءاً منها، وحينئذ يكون دليله وارداً على مدعاه غايته أنه يرد عليه ما ورد على القائلين بوجود الطبائع.
And you already know that the abstract has no existence in the external world, rather it is impossible for it to exist there, so this claim is absolutely false. And you also know that the receptive of opposites is the quiddity in itself, for it in its essence is receptive to being described with each one of them alternatively. So the quiddity accompanying opposing individuations. As for the existence of an individual of the human quiddity such that that individual is receptive to Zayd and 'Amr, i.e., to their individuations, as his statement indicates, its falsity is necessary, because it is impossible for one determinate thing to be qualified with opposite attributes at one time. Likewise, if he meant by an individual of it the quiddity restricted by the condition of abstraction, then the conjunction of the abstract with the conditions from which it was assumed to be abstracted is also necessarily false. So it appears that his proof is not sufficient for... His statement: 'because that which is receptive' i.e., in itself. His statement: 'so this claim is false, etc.' means that his claim is of obvious impossibility, not worthy to be heard. So his statement 'you know that the abstract has no existence in reality' is an objection that the commentator arranged upon it the falsity of the claim for emphasis. His statement: 'for it in its essence, etc.' – the quiddity in its essence, since it is nothing but the quiddity, its receptivity to opposites is by way of alternation; but in the realm of existence, it is receptive to them by way of combination, because with existence it exists, with non-existence it is non-existent, with unity it is one, with multiplicity it is many. His statement: 'so the human quiddity, etc.' – the commentator added it to connect his statement 'as for the existence of an individual, etc.'. His statement: 'i.e., to their individuations' – the expression involves deletion of the object; he said 'to their individuations' even though its receptivity to a single individuation is also impossible, because the discussion is about receptivity to opposites. His statement: 'and likewise if he meant, etc.' – i.e., what was mentioned of its necessary falsity assuming he meant by 'individual' its common meaning, i.e., the bearer of individuation; even if that is a departure from our context; and if he meant by it the abstract quiddity, based on it being an individual of the absolute, it is also necessarily false. Yes, man is receptive to non-existence just as he is receptive to the rest of his opposite accidents, so the proof, assuming its completeness, would require his abstraction from the accidents of existence as well. Then how can he judge that he is concomitant with this accident, namely existence, and yet abstracted from accidents entirely? And it may be said: The apparent meaning of Plato's speech is that his intention is to judge the existence of the natural universal. So the meaning of his speech is that the quiddity in itself is eternal and perpetual, by the evidence of his proof and his statement in the claim 'receptive to opposites', except that he engages in artificiality in applying 'individual' to the quiddity, on the assumption that this application occurs in his speech, meaning that it is a single nature, and 'abstracted' meaning that none of the accidents are the quiddity itself nor a part of it. Then his proof is valid for his claim, except that it is subject to the same objections as those who affirm the existence of the universals.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This is a refutational and reconciliatory passage. The author begins by asserting that the abstract (مجرد) cannot exist externally, so Plato's claim is false. He then clarifies that the true subject receptive to opposites is the quiddity in itself, which can receive opposites alternately, not a concrete individual. The impossibility of an individual being receptive to opposite individuations (like Zayd and 'Amr) is stressed. Further, if Plato meant the quiddity restricted by abstraction, that is self-contradictory because abstracting from conditions while retaining them is impossible. The commentator then explains each phrase: the quiddity in itself can receive opposites either alternately (in essence) or simultaneously (in existence, e.g., existence/non-existence, unity/multiplicity). The added comment about the human quiddity ties the argument. The discussion then addresses that if the proof were sound, it would also require abstraction from existence, contradicting the assumption of existence. Finally, a reconciliation is offered: Plato may have intended the natural universal (الكلي الطبيعي), meaning the quiddity in itself is eternal and receptive to opposites, but his terminology of 'individual' and 'abstract' is forced. His proof would then be valid but faces the standard objections against the existence of universals.

Key Terms
الكلي الطبيعي
الماهية من حيث هي
العوارض
فرد
مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Plato's claim is completely false and cannot be salvaged.
Correction: The author shows that with a proper interpretation, Plato's claim can be seen as a valid argument for the natural universal, though still controversial.

Checkpoint
According to the reconciliation, what did Plato really mean by 'individual'?
A) A concrete particular
B) The quiddity as a single nature
C) The abstract entity
D) The accident

Key Takeaway
Plato's claim can be reinterpreted as an affirmation of the natural universal, but the terminology is forced and the proof remains subject to objections against realism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have seen that Plato's claim can be reinterpreted charitably. Where in your own life do you hold onto a surface-level understanding of someone's words without seeking their deeper intended meaning? Will you commit to trying to understand before judging?
?
This lesson shows that even when a proof is valid, it may still face objections. Do you ever take your own valid reasoning as final, ignoring that others may have legitimate criticisms? How can you humble yourself to examine objections prayerfully?
?
The author forces terminology to reconcile a viewpoint. When have you forced your own words to defend a position, knowing deep down that your terminology was stretched? What would it take for you to be honest about the limits of your own arguments?

The Three Divisions of Accidents
The commentator enumerates the three divisions of accidents based on their attachment to mental and external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three divisions of accidents that attach to possible quiddities according to the commentator.

Core Idea
Accidents are divided into three: common, mental-only, and external-only.

The commentator begins by stating the division of existence into mental and external, and explains that possible quiddities are receptive to both. From this, the accidents that attach to quiddities are seen as three divisions. This lesson clarifies these divisions and the terminology used.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three divisions.
Start with the Arabic text and translation to grasp the initial classification.
The passage only explicitly mentions one division but implies the other two.
2
Identify the three accident categories.
From the text, deduce the three: accidents that attach to both existences, accidents specific to mental existence (for impossibles), and accidents specific to external existence (for the necessary).
The commentator uses the impossibles and necessary as examples.
3
Understand the meaning of 'accident' here.
The commentator clarifies that 'accident' here means what attaches and follows the quiddity, not the external predicate.
This is crucial to avoid misunderstanding.
4
Reflect on why this division matters.
This classification helps in analyzing the ontological status of different entities relative to existence.
It is foundational for further discussions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسموا الوجود إلى ذهني وخارجي، وجعلوا الماهية الممكنة قابلة لهما ولرفعهما، فرأوا العوارض ثلاثة أقسام: قسم لهما بمعنى أنه محمول عليهما خارج عن ماهيتهما وهو موضوع لهما بالطبع. قوله: (وجعلوا أي اعتقدوا كما في قوله تعالى: {وجعلوا لله شركاء الجن} [الأنعام: 100]. قوله: (الماهية الممكنة قابلة لهما وأما الممتنعات فلعدم قبولها الوجود الخارجي لا يكون لها إلا العوارض الذهنية، ولذا الواجب لاقتضائه الوجود الخارجي لا يكون له إلا العوارض الخارجية، وأما العوارض التي تلحقه في الذهن، فباعتبار أنه من حيث الوجود الذهني ممكن؛ إذ يجوز أن يحصل فيه وأن لا يحصل. قوله: (ولرفعهما) إنما اعتبر قبولها لرفع الوجودين ليظهر اختصاص بعض العوارض بالوجود الخارجي، وبعضها بالوجود الذهني. قوله: (أي الأمور التي تعرض إلخ) أي ليس المراد بالعارض الخارج المحمول بل ما يعرضه ويلحقه. الشارح قدس سره سواء وجدت الأربعة في الخارج أو في الذهن وصرح به في شرح التجريد وليس المراد به مفهوم الوجود ولا الوجود من غير اعتبار خصوصية معه، حتى لا تنحصر القسمة فتدبر. ثم اعلم أنه إن أريد بمدخلية الوجود المطلق أو الخارجي أو الذهني في العروض أن يكون ذلك شرطاً فيه، فالوجود المطلق وكذا الخارجي والذهني خارج من الأقسام الثلاثة؛ إذ قيام الوجود إنما هو بالماهية من حيث هي على ما نص عليه في التجريد وغيره لا بشرط الوجود، وإلا لزم تقدم الوجود على الوجود وإن أريد به أن يكون ظرفاً له ومصححاً لعروضه فالوجود داخل في القسم الثالث، لأن الاتصاف بالوجود وإن لم يستدع حينئذ تقدم المعروض بالوجود، لكنه يقتضي أن لا يكون المعروض مخلوطاً بذلك العارض في ذلك الظرف، وظاهر أن الماهية في الوجود الخارجي مخلوطة بالوجود الخارجي، وكذا في الوجود في نفس الأمر مخلوطة به بحسب نفس الأمر، وكذا في الوجود الذهني مخلوطة به بحسب نفس الأمر، لكن للعقل أن يأخذها غير مخلوطة بشيء من العوارض فهو في هذا الاعتبار معرى عن جميع العوارض حتى عن هذا الاعتبار، فهذا النحو من الوجود ظرف للاتصاف به، وهو نحو من أنحاء الوجود في نفس الأمر. كذا أفاده المحقق الدواني. وهذا على ما اختاره من أن ثبوت الشيء للشيء مستلزم لثبوت المثبت له وأما على ما هو المشهور من الفرعية فنقول: اتصاف الماهية بالوجود ليس اتصافاً حقيقياً، فإن زيادة الوجود خارجياً كان أو ذهنياً إنما هو في التصور، فهو انتزاعي محض، فإذا لاحظها العقل وانتزع منها الوجود ووصفها به كان ذلك فرعا لحصولها في الذهن بوجود هو نفسها، ثم إذا لاحظها مرة ثانية وانتزع منها وجوداً ذهنياً ووصفها به كان ذلك فرعاً لحصولها في الذهن مرة ثالثة بوجود هو نفسها وهكذا وليس هذه الملاحظة والالتفات لازمة للنفس فتنقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار والملاحظة، وهذا تحقيق ما ذكره صاحب التجريد من أن الوجود من المعقولات الثانية. وبما حررنا لك يندفع الشكوك التي عرضت للناظرين في هذا المقام لا نطول الكلام بذكرها ودفعها، فإنك بعد الاحاطة بما ذكرنا يظهر لك جلية الحال من غير حاجة إلى القيل والقال.
They divided existence into mental and external, and made the possible quiddity receptive to both and to the removal of both, so they saw the accidents as three divisions: a division for both in the sense that it is predicated on both, external to their quiddity, and it is subject for both by nature. His statement: (they made) meaning they believed, as in His statement exalted: {And they made for Allah partners from the jinn} [al-An’am: 100]. His statement: (the possible quiddity receptive to both) as for the impossibles, due to their lack of receptivity to external existence, they have only mental accidents; and therefore the necessary, due to its requiring external existence, has only external accidents. As for the accidents that attach to it in the mind, it is by virtue of it being, in terms of mental existence, possible; for it is possible that it occurs and that it does not occur. His statement: (and to the removal of both) he only considered its receptivity to the removal of the two existences to show that some accidents are specific to external existence and some to mental existence. His statement: (i.e., the matters that attach etc.) meaning the intended meaning is not the external accident that is predicated, but rather what attaches to it and follows it. The commentator, may his soul be sanctified, (said) whether the four [accidents] are found externally or mentally, and he stated this explicitly in Sharh al-Tajrid. The intended meaning is not the concept of existence nor existence without consideration of any particularity with it, so that the division is not restricted; so reflect. Then know that if what is meant by the entry of absolute existence, or external, or mental, into the occurrence (of the accident) is that it is a condition for it, then absolute existence, and likewise external and mental, are outside the three divisions; because the subsistence of existence is only with the quiddity in itself, as stated in al-Tajrid and elsewhere, not conditioned by existence; otherwise, the priority of existence over existence would be required. And if what is meant is that it is a container for it and a corrector for its occurrence, then existence is included in the third division, because the being described by existence, although it does not then require the priority of the described with existence, yet it requires that the described not be mixed with that accident in that container; and it is apparent that the quiddity in external existence is mixed with external existence, and likewise in existence in reality it is mixed with it according to reality, and likewise in mental existence it is mixed with it according to reality; but the intellect can take it as unmixed with any of the accidents, so it is in this consideration stripped of all accidents, even of this consideration. So this mode of existence is a container for being described by it, and it is one of the modes of existence in reality. Thus stated the investigator al-Dawani. And this according to his choice that the existence of a thing for a thing necessitates the existence of the thing for which it is established; but according to the well-known [view] of subordination, we say: the description of quiddity by existence is not a real description, for the increase of existence, whether external or mental, is only in the concept; it is a pure abstraction. When the intellect considers it and abstracts existence from it and describes it by that, that is subordinate to its occurrence in the mind with an existence that is itself; then when it considers it a second time and abstracts mental existence from it and describes it by that, that is subordinate to its occurrence in the mind a third time with an existence that is itself, and so on. And this observation and attention are not necessary for the soul; so they cease when consideration and attention cease. This is the verification of what the author of al-Tajrid mentioned, that existence is among the secondary intelligibles. By what we have written for you, the doubts that occurred to the viewers in this matter are repelled; we do not prolong speech by mentioning and repelling them, for after encompassing what we have mentioned, the state of affairs becomes clear to you without need for dispute.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defining the three divisions of accidents that attach to possible quiddities, and it discusses the role of existence in their attribution. The commentator begins by stating that the original text divided existence into mental and external, and that possible quiddities are receptive to both and to their removal. From this, the accidents are seen as three divisions: one division that attaches to both mental and external existence, being predicated on both and external to their quiddity, serving as a subject for both by nature. Then the commentary explains each term: 'they made' means they believed; the impossibles have only mental accidents because they cannot receive external existence; the necessary has only external accidents due to its requiring external existence, but mental accidents can be considered from its mental existence. The commentary then clarifies that the receptivity to removal of both existences is to show the specificity of some accidents to external existence and some to mental. The intended meaning of 'accidents' here is not the external accident that is predicated, but rather what attaches and follows the quiddity. The commentator then addresses the entry of existence into the accidents: if existence (absolute, external, or mental) is taken as a condition for the occurrence of an accident, then existence itself is outside the three divisions because the subsistence of existence accords with quiddity in itself, not conditioned by existence; otherwise, we would have a priority of existence over itself. If existence is taken as a container (container) and corrector for the accident, then existence falls into the third division, because while description by existence does not require the priority of the described with existence, it requires that the described not be mixed with that accident in that container. The quiddity in external existence is mixed with existence, but the intellect can consider it stripped of all accidents, making that mode of existence a container for description. The commentator then relates this to the views of al-Dawani and the author of al-Tajrid (al-Tusi), concluding that existence is a secondary intelligible. The passage ends with the assurance that this analysis repels doubts.

Key Terms
وجود ذهني
وجود خارجي
ماهية
عارض
ممتنعات
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three divisions of accidents are about the nature of existence itself.
Correction: The text clarifies that the accidents are not the concept of existence, but rather what attaches to the quiddity in relation to existence types.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of accidents do impossibles have?
A) External accidents only
B) Mental accidents only
C) Both mental and external accidents
D) No accidents

Key Takeaway
The three divisions of accidents are: common to both existences, specific to mental existence (for impossibles), and specific to external existence (for the necessary).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah's attributes, are you careful to remember that these are external-only accidents (i.e., they exist in reality as they are) and not mere mental constructions that might confuse your understanding? How can you train your mind to avoid mixing mental-only concepts with the reality of Allah?
?
Think of a time you held a belief about someone that existed only in your mind but had no external reality. How might the distinction between mental-only and common accidents help you check your assumptions and lead you to seek truth rather than cling to mental illusions?
?
The commentator warns that the division is not about existence itself but about how accidents attach. Are you treating the categories you learn in 'aqeedah as tools to understand reality, or as final boxes that limit your reflection? Commit to one way you will reflect deeper today.

Existence as Condition or Container for Accidents
The commentator analyzes the two interpretations of existence's role in accident occurrence: condition or container.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of how existence enters into the occurrence of accidents: as a condition or as a container.

Core Idea
Existence is either a condition for an accident's occurrence or a container that enables its attribution.

The commentator delves into a subtle point: when we say an accident occurs in existence, does existence serve as a condition that must be present for the accident to occur, or does it merely provide a container in which the accident can be attributed to the quiddity? This lesson unpacks the implications of each view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on condition vs container.
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the two interpretations: existence as a condition, and existence as a container.
The passage also discusses the consequences: if condition, existence is outside the three divisions; if container, existence falls into the third division.
2
Understand the first interpretation: condition.
If existence is a condition for the accident's occurrence, then existence itself cannot be an accident of the quiddity, because that would require priority of existence. Existence is then external to the three divisions.
The text says: 'فالوجود المطلق وكذا الخارجي والذهني خارج من الأقسام الثلاثة'.
3
Understand the second interpretation: container.
If existence is a container, then the quiddity in existence is mixed with existence, but the intellect can separate them, allowing existence to be an accident under the third division.
The text says: 'فالوجود داخل في القسم الثالث' in this interpretation.
4
Reflect on the significance.
This distinction affects whether existence is considered an accident of the quiddity or not, which is crucial for metaphysics.
The passage cites al-Dawani's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
