Objection and Answer on Mental Existence of Parts
Objection and detailed refutation on mental existence of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that mental images become external entities is refuted, and why the composite must have only one set of essential parts.

Core Idea
Two complete essences for one thing is impossible; the objection fails to avoid it.

This passage presents a critical objection to the earlier argument about predicate parts. The objector claims that if mental images become identical to external entities, the problem disappears. Sanusi's answer systematically shows that this leads to the same unacceptable conclusion: one thing would have two complete essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the objection begins with 'قيل' and the answer continues through several sub-points.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says the previous argument only works if external parts are distinct existences. But if mental images become those external entities when in the world, and vice versa, then the problem of two essences does not arise.
The key phrase: 'فالصورة العقلية إذا وجدت في الخارج صارت بعينها تلك الأعيان الخارجية'
3
Follow the answer's logical steps
The answer proceeds: (a) The complete definition of a composite is the mental image from the external parts. (b) If there were also predicate parts, they either include the external parts or not. If not, no match; if they do, with or without addition. (c) Either way, the external parts suffice as the complete reality; any different mental parts would create a second complete essence.
The answer is detailed; focus on the conclusion: 'يلزم أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان'
4
Understand the commentator's nuance
The commentator notes that predicate parts are essentially the same as external parts; the distinction is only in the mode of consideration (unconditional vs. conditional). This nuance does not weaken the argument.
The phrase 'الأجزاء المحمولة بعينها هي الخارجية' is key.
5
Grasp the final conclusion
Even accepting the objector's assumption, the composite cannot have two essentially different sets of parts. If it did, it would have two complete essences, which is impossible.
The argument ends with a nod to genus and species: they can correspond to one individual, but that is a different matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وذلك لأنه إذا حصلت إلخ) قيل : من يقول بأن الأجزاء موجودات متمايزة في الخارج بوجودات متمايزة بحسب نفس الأمر لم يرد عليه شيء مما ذكر؛ إذ الصورة العقلية إذا وجدت في الخارج صارت بعينها تلك الأعيان الخارجية، وتلك إذا وجدت في الذهن صارت صوراً عقلية، فمعنى كون المركب العقلي مركباً خارجياً ذا أجزاء خارجية أن يكون للأجزاء
الملتئمة من على مجموع تلك الأجزاء حداً تاماً لها، إذ لا معنى للتحديد التام إلا تصوير كنه الماهية، فلو كان لها أجزاء محمولة أيضا، فإن لم تشتمل على تلك الأجزاء لم تحصل منها صورة مطابقة للماهية المفروضة؛ لأن الصورة المطابقة لها هي تلك الأجزاء، وإن اشتملت عليها فحينئذ إن لم تشتمل على أمر زائد، كانت هي تلك الأجزاء بعينها، لا أجزاء محمولة، وإن اشتملت على أمر زائد فذلك الزائد إن دخل في الماهية كانت حقيقتها قابلة للزيادة والنقصان وإن لم تدخل فلا اعتبار به في الأجزاء. وبالجملة : مجموع الأجزاء الخارجية تمام حقيقة المركب في العقل كما أنه تمام حقيقته في الخارج، فلو كان له أجزاء عقلية مغايرة لتلك الأجزاء، لكان مجموعها أيضاً تمام ماهية المركب في العقل، فيلزم أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان من قوله : ( لأن الصورة المطابقة لها هي الملتئمة إلخ ) يعني أن المطابقة منحصرة في الملتئمة الأجزاء الغير المحمولة؛ إذ لا فرق بينها وبين الماهية إلا بالإجمال والتفصيل، والمفروض أن الصورة الملتئمة من الأجزاء المحمولة مخالفة للصورة المذكورة، فلا تكون تلك الصورة مطابقة للماهية لامتناع مطابقة أمرين متخالفين لأمر واحد، بأن يكون كل منهما صورة تمام الماهية. قوله : كانت هي تلك الأجزاء بعينها لا أجزاء محمولة فيه بحث؛ لأن الأجزاء المحمولة الأجزاء الخارجية ذاتاً، والفرق بينهما باعتبار أخذ المحمولة لا بشرط، والخارجية بشرط لا، وهو مناط الحمل وعدمه كما عرفت . عين قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ) أي نترك التفصيل المذكور ونقول مجملاً هكذا. قوله : ( مغايرة لتلك الأجزاء) بالذات إما كلاً أو بعضاً. قوله : ( فيلزم أن يكون لشيء واحد إلخ) قد عرفت أنه إنما يلزم ذلك لو لم تتحد الأجزاء المحمولة والخارجية بالذات . العينية وجودات متمايزة في الخارج، ومعنى كون المركب الخارجي مركباً عقلياً ذا أجزاء عقلية أن يكون للأجزاء العينية وجودات متمايزة ،فيه فيختار أن الأجزاء المحمولة، بعينها هي الخارجية بلا شامل ومشمول، وإنما التمايز بعارض الوجود وأنت خبير بأن الكلام في تركب المركب الخارجي من الأجزاء المحمولة وأن الصور العقلية على هذا التصوير لا تحمل على الكل . قوله : ( فيلزم أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان مختلفتان أي تماما حقيقتين مختلفين، كما ظهر من تقريره، فلايرد تجويزنا مطابقة كل من الجنس والفصل والنوع لزيد مثلاً .
His statement: (and that is because when the [external parts] are obtained, etc.) It was said: Whoever holds that the parts are distinct existents in the external world with distinct existences according to the thing itself is not affected by anything mentioned; for the mental image (al-sura al-`aqliyya) when it exists externally becomes identical to those external entities themselves, and those when they exist in the mind become mental images. So the meaning of the mental composite being an external composite having external parts is that the parts formed from the total of those parts have a complete definition; for there is no meaning to complete definition except depicting the true reality (kunn) of the essence. So if it also had predicate parts, then if those [predicate parts] do not include those [external] parts, no form corresponding to the assumed essence would be obtained from them, because the corresponding form is those [external] parts; but if they include them, then if they include no additional thing, they are those very parts, not predicate parts; and if they include an additional thing, then if that additional thing enters into the essence, its reality would be subject to increase and decrease, and if it does not enter, it is not considered in the parts. In summary: the total of the external parts is the complete reality of the composite in the mind just as it is its complete reality externally; so if it had mental parts different from those parts, their total would also be the complete essence of the composite in the mind, and thus it would follow that one thing has two essences. From his statement: (because the corresponding form is the one composed, etc.) Meaning that correspondence is confined to the one composed of non-predicate parts; for there is no difference between it and the essence except in terms of conciseness and detail, and it is assumed that the form composed of predicate parts is different from the mentioned form, so that form would not correspond to the essence because it is impossible for two divergent things to correspond to a single thing, such that each is a picture of the complete essence. His statement: (they are those very parts, not predicate parts) contains a discussion; because predicate parts are essentially the external parts; the difference between them is based on considering the predicate [part] as unconditional, and the external as conditional upon not being predicated, which is the basis of predication or its absence, as you know. [His statement] precisely: his statement: (and in summary, etc.) i.e., we leave the mentioned detail and say summarily thus. His statement: (different from those parts) essentially, either wholly or partially. His statement: (and thus it would follow that one thing has [two essences]) you have known that this only follows if the predicate parts and the external parts are not essentially identical. The external parts are distinct existences externally, and the meaning of the external composite being a mental composite having mental parts is that the external parts have distinct existences, so it can be chosen that the predicate parts are identical to the external parts without inclusion and being included; the distinction is only through the accident of existence. And you are well aware that the discussion is about the composition of the external composite from predicate parts, and that mental images on this depiction do not apply to the whole. His statement: (and thus it would follow that one thing has two different essences) i.e., two complete different essences, as appears from his exposition; thus our allowing the correspondence of each of genus, specific difference, and species to Zayd, for example, is not refuted.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the previous argument and its detailed answer. The objector says: the previous argument only works if one assumes that external parts are distinct existences in the external world. But if we accept that mental images are identical to external entities when external, and vice versa when mental, then the mental composite's being 'external' simply means the parts have distinct existences. So the problem of two essences does not arise. The answer is given in several steps: (1) The complete definition of a composite is the mental image formed from its external parts; this image is the entire essence. (2) If there were also predicate parts, either they do not include the external parts (so no match) or they do. If they include them but without addition, they are the same external parts, not predicate. If with addition, either the addition enters the essence (making it variable) or not (so irrelevant). (3) Hence the set of external parts alone is the complete reality in the mind. If there were different mental parts, that would be another complete reality, leading to two essences. (4) The commentator then explains the key: the corresponding form must be composed of non-predicate parts; any other form would be different and cannot correspond to the same essence. (5) He acknowledges a nuance: predicate parts are essentially the same as external parts; the distinction is due to the mode of consideration (with or without the condition of not being predicated). (6) He concludes that the necessary implication (two essences) only holds if the parts are essentially different. At the end, he notes that the discussion is about the external composite being composed of predicate parts, and mental forms in this depiction do not apply to the whole (i.e., they are not predicated of the composite).

أجزاء محمولة
أجزاء خارجية
حقيقة مركب
مطابقة

Correction: The answer demonstrates that even under that assumption, the composite cannot have two different sets of essential parts without implying multiple essences.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the consequence if a composite has both external parts and predicate parts that are not essentially identical?
A) The composite would have no definition.
B) The composite would have two complete essences.
C) The external parts would disappear.

Key Takeaway
The objector's attempt to avoid the argument fails because even if mental images become external, the composite still requires a single set of defining parts; any different mental parts imply two complete essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The argument shows that a thing cannot have two complete essences. Consider your own understanding of a complex issue in your faith or life—perhaps about Allah's attributes or your own purpose. Are you holding two incompatible definitions in your mind, thinking they are the same? How can you unify them, or must you discard one?
?
The objector tried to escape the problem by superficially identifying mental images with external reality. In your life, when you face a contradiction in your beliefs or actions, do you tend to patch it with a shallow excuse rather than examine the core? This lesson demands consistency in essence. What contradiction are you currently avoiding, and what deeper inconsistency would you uncover if you followed the argument to its end?

Second Objection: Distinguishing External and Mental Essences
Sanusi refutes the claim that one essence can have an external and a mental essence without absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an objector's attempt to allow two essences for one thing by distinguishing external and mental essences fails, because both turn out to be mental essences.

Core Idea
Two complete essences — whether external or mental — cannot belong to one thing.

This passage presents a second objection to the earlier argument: conceding that two essences follow, but one is external and the other mental. Sanusi shows this distinction collapses, since the mental representation of external parts is itself a complete essence, making both essences mental. Thus the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector concedes two essences but argues one is external and one mental, claiming this is not impossible.
Notice the concessive tone: 'نعم لزم... لكن'.
2
Identify Sanusi's counter
He states that the depiction actually leads to two mental essences, not one external.
3
Understand the reasoning
Because the total of external parts is the complete reality of the composite in the mind, just as in the external world. Thus the mental essence is identical to the external essence.
4
Examine the final phrase
The unclear phrase 'الأجزاء الخارجية لجذائها (هكذا) لا' may be corrupt but likely attempts to argue that external parts do not have a separate mental existence.
This part is uncertain; focus on the clear refutation.
5
Conclude: refutation stands
Since both would be mental, the distinction fails, and one thing cannot have two complete essences.
The core argument remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : نعم لزم أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان مختلفتان، لكن إحداهما حقيقية خارجية، والأخرى ذهنية، وقد لا نسلم امتناعه وأنت خبير بأنه لزم من التصوير المذكور أن يكون لشيء واحد حقيقتان مختلفتان ذهنيتان؛ لأن مجموع الأجزاء الخارجية تمام حقيقة المركب في العقل، كما أنه تمام حقيقته في الخارج على ما صرح به اللهم إلا أن يقال : الأجزاء الخارجية لجذائها ( هكذا) لا
And it may be said: Yes, it follows that one thing has two different essences, but one of them is external and the other mental, and we may not grant its impossibility. And you are well aware that what follows from the mentioned depiction is that one thing has two different mental essences; because the total of the external parts is the complete reality of the composite in the mind, just as it is its complete reality externally, as he explicitly stated. Unless it is said: the external parts [unclear] their [؟] no.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. The objector concedes that the earlier argument leads to two different essences for one thing but argues that one is external (in the outside world) and the other is mental (in the mind), and that this is not necessarily impossible. The author responds decisively: the depiction actually entails two mental essences, because the set of external parts constitutes the complete reality of the composite in the mind, just as it does externally. Therefore both essences are mental, not one external and one mental. The objector's attempted distinction fails. The final phrase 'الأجزاء الخارجية لجذائها ( هكذا) لا' is unclear and probably corrupt; it may be an unfinished attempt to argue that external parts do not have a separate mental existence, but the text breaks off. The core point is that the argument stands: a single thing cannot have two complete essences, whether both are mental or one external and one mental.

Correction: The mental essence derived from external parts is identical to the external essence; any other mental essence would be an additional complete essence, which is impossible.

Al-Razi's Proof that Ta'ayyun is Existential
Al-Razi's disjunctive proof for ta'ayyun's existential nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of al-Razi's proof that individuation (ta'ayyun) is an existential attribute, not a non-existential one.

Core Idea
Individuation must be existential; otherwise, it falls into contradiction or infinite regress.

Imam al-Razi presents a rigorous logical argument to settle the nature of individuation. Using a disjunction, he eliminates both absolute and relative non-existence as possibilities. This lesson walks through each step of the proof, showing how all routes lead to the conclusion that ta'ayyun is existential.

Guided Path
1
Understand the disjunction
Al-Razi starts by assuming the opponent's view: if ta'ayyun is non-existential, it must be either absolute non-existence or relative non-existence. Read the first line of the passage to see this assumption.
Absolute non-existence means no existence whatsoever; relative non-existence is the absence of something specific.
2
Reject absolute non-existence
The first branch is dismissed because absolute non-existence has no properties—it cannot serve as a principle of distinction. Also, if ta'ayyun were absolute non-existence, the individuated thing would be absolutely non-existent, which is false.
Note the two parallel arguments against absolute non-existence.
3
Analyze relative non-existence: first sub-branch
If ta'ayyun is relative non-existence, it could be non-existence of non-individuation (lā taʿayyun). This leads to an infinite regress because non-individuation itself would contain individuation, so the supposed non-existential individuation would actually be existential.
Focus on the regress argument: the chain of negations cannot go on infinitely.
4
Analyze relative non-existence: second sub-branch
Alternatively, ta'ayyun could be non-existence of another individuation. That other individuation must be either existential or non-existential. If non-existential, then this ta'ayyun is non-existence of non-existence = existence. If existential, then this ta'ayyun is like it, so also existential.
This branch uses the principle that a thing's attribute depends on the existence of the attribute itself.
5
Conclude the proof
All possibilities lead to the conclusion that ta'ayyun is existential. The commentary emphasizes that this formulation refutes all doubts raised by other scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد احتج الإمام الرازي) على كون التعين أمراً وجودياً بأنه لو كان عدمياً لكان إما عدماً مطلقاً، وأنه ظاهر البطلان )؛ لأن العدم المطلق لا تميز فيه، فكيف يميز غيره؟ ؛ وإما عدماً مضافاً، وحينئذ إما أن يكون عدماً للا تعين العدمي، فيكون هو وجودياً، وإما ( أن يكون ) (عدماً لتعين آخر ، فذلك ) التعين (الآخر إن كان عدماً فهذا التعين عدم العدم فهو وجود والتعين الآخر مثله، فيكون هو أيضاً وجوداً . ( وإن كان ذلك التعين الآخر ) وجوداً ، وهذا التعين الذي نحن فيه (مثله، قوله : ( بأنه لو كان إلخ) أي كل واحد من أفراد التعين وجودي، إذ لو كان فرد منه لكان إلخ . عدمياً قوله : ( لأن العدم المطلق إلخ ليس المراد به ما لا إضافة فيه فإنه ممتنع التعقل إذ الإضافة قوله : ( لأن العدم المطلق لا تميز فيه وأيضاً لو كان التعين عدماً لكان المتعين معدوماً مطلقاً؛ لأن المتصف بالعدم المطلق معدوم مطلق مع ظهور بطلانه . قوله : (وإما عدماً مضافاً ) أي إلى شيء مخصوص، ولا شك أنه يكون عدماً لشيء ينافيه وهو إما اللاتعين الذي هو نقيض ذلك التعين المخصوص، أو التعين الاخر إذ ما سواهما من المفهومات يمكن اجتماعه منه فإن اللاتعين المطلق يصدق على كل تعين مخصوص ضرورة سلب تعين آخر عنه وكل مفهوم ما سوى التعين يمكن عروض التعين له . قوله: (فيكون هو وجودياً) أي يكون التعين الذي هو عدم اللاتعين وجودياً لأن التعين الذي اعتبر في مفهوم اللاتعين وجودي؛ لأنه لو كان عدمياً لكان عدماً للاتعين؛ لأنه المفروض، وهذا اللاتعين أيضاً مشتمل على التعين الذي هو عدم اللاتعين، وهكذا فيلزم اشتمال اللاتعين الذي فرض التعين عدما له على أعدام غير متناهية فلا يكون التعين الذي اعتبر في عدمياً، وإذا كان هذا التعين وجودياً كان التعين الذي فرض أنه عدم اللاتعين وجودياً؛ لأنه عينه لما عرفت من أنه لو لم يكن عينه لم يكن اللاتعين منافياً له . قوله : ( فذلك التعين الآخر إن كان عدماً تقدير الكلام فذلك التعين الآخر إن كان عدمياً كان عدماً لشيء. قوله فهو (وجود) فيكون وجودياً بناء على مساواة الوجود الوجودي لمساواة العدمي بالعدم . قوله : ( والتعين الآخر مثله أي في كونه تعيناً سواء كان ذاتياً لهما أو عرضياً . قوله: (فيكون هو أيضاً وجودياً) بناء على ما تقرر من أن اتصاف شيء بصفة من شأنها الوجود في الخارج فرع وجود الصفة، وإلا لجاز اتصاف الجسم بالحركة المعدومة وهو سفسطة واعلم أن تقرير الاحتجاج المذكور على ما حررناه يدفع جميع الشكوك التي أوردها الناظرون في
And Imam al-Razi argued that individuation is an existential matter: if it were non-existential, it would be either absolute non-existence, which is obviously false—because absolute non-existence has no distinction, so how can it distinguish others?—or relative non-existence. In that case, it would either be non-existence of non-individuation, which would then be existential, or it would be non-existence of another individuation. Then that other individuation: if it is non-existential, then this individuation is non-existence of non-existence, so it is existence, and the other individuation is like it, so it too is existential. And if that other individuation is existential, then this individuation under discussion is like it, so it is also existential. His saying: 'if it were non-existential, etc.' means that each individual of individuation is existential; if a particular individual of it were non-existential, then it would be... His saying: 'absolute non-existence, etc.' — what is meant is not that which has no relation, for it is impossible to conceive, because relation... His saying: 'absolute non-existence has no distinction' and also if individuation were non-existence, then the individuated thing would be absolutely non-existent, because that which is qualified by absolute non-existence is absolutely non-existent, which is clearly false. His saying: 'or relative non-existence' i.e., to something specific, and there is no doubt that it would be non-existence of something that opposes it, either non-individuation which is the opposite of that specific individuation, or another individuation, since other concepts can coexist with it; for absolute non-individuation is true of every specific individuation necessarily by negating another individuation from it, and every concept other than individuation can receive individuation. His saying: 'so it would be existential' i.e., the individuation that is non-existence of non-individuation is existential, because the individuation considered in the concept of non-individuation is existential; for if it were non-existential, it would be non-existence of non-individuation (as supposed), and this non-individuation also includes the individuation that is non-existence of non-individuation, and so on, leading to the non-individuation that was supposed to have the individuation as its non-existence containing infinite non-existences, so the individuation considered would not be non-existential. If this individuation is existential, then the individuation that was supposed to be non-existence of non-individuation is existential, because it is the same as it, since you know that if it were not the same, non-individuation would not oppose it. His saying: 'then that other individuation if it were non-existential' — the implied phrase is: 'then that other individuation if it were non-existential, it would be non-existence of something.' His saying: 'so it is existence' — so it would be existential, based on the equivalence of existential existence to non-existential non-existence? His saying: 'and the other individuation is like it' i.e., in being individuation, whether it is essential or accidental to both. His saying: 'then it too is existential' based on what has been established: that the description of a thing by an attribute that by its nature exists externally is contingent upon the existence of the attribute; otherwise, it would be permissible to describe a body by non-existent motion, which is sophistry. And know that the formulation of the aforementioned argument according to what we have written repels all the doubts that the investigators have raised concerning...
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents a multi-branch proof from Imam al-Razi that ta'ayyun (individuation) is an existential (wujūdī) matter, not a non-existential (ʿadamī) one. The proof uses a disjunction: if ta'ayyun were non-existential, it would be either absolute non-existence or relative non-existence. Absolute non-existence cannot distinguish anything, so ta'ayyun cannot be that. Relative non-existence must be non-existence of something: either non-individuation (lā taʿayyun) or another individuation. The first branch: if it is non-existence of non-individuation, then since non-individuation is itself a concept that includes individuation (since its definition involves negation of individuation), this leads to an infinite regress, so the individuation considered must be existential. The second branch: if it is non-existence of another individuation, that other individuation is either existential or non-existential. If non-existential, then this individuation is non-existence of non-existence = existence. If existential, then this individuation is like it, so existential. Thus, all possibilities lead to ta'ayyun being existential. The commentary explains each step, emphasizing that absolute non-existence has no distinction, and that relative non-existence leads to either infinite regress or contradiction if the other individuation is non-existential.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyun)
عدمية (non-existential)
وجودية (existential)
عدم مطلق (absolute non-existence)
عدم مضاف (relative non-existence)
لاتعين (non-individuation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes what it tries to prove.
Correction: The proof is not circular; it assumes the opponent's position (ta'ayyun is non-existential) and derives a contradiction or infinite regress, thereby refuting it. The conclusion that ta'ayyun is existential follows from the elimination of all alternatives.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's proof, what happens if ta'ayyun is non-existence of non-individuation (lā taʿayyun)?
A) It is necessarily non-existential.
B) It leads to an infinite regress, so ta'ayyun must be existential.
C) It is impossible because non-individuation doesn't exist.
D) It becomes absolute non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's proof shows that all logical possibilities for ta'ayyun being non-existential lead to contradictions, so ta'ayyun must be existential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi's proof shows that your very individuality is not a mere absence but a positive reality from Allah. How does this truth affect your understanding of your own significance and accountability before Him?
?
If ta'ayyun is existential, then your distinctness as a person is a gift of existence from Allah. Does your life reflect gratitude for this gift? What specific actions can you take today to acknowledge that your identity is a creation of Allah, not a negation of others?
?
The proof uses logical necessity to show that non-existence cannot be a source of distinction. Are there areas in your life where you are defining yourself by what you are not (e.g., 'I am not like them') instead of by your positive relationship with Allah? How can you shift to a positive identity rooted in faith?

The First Objection: Denial of Validity Based on Impossibility
First objection to al-Razi's proof about the validity of the existential conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to al-Razi's proof and how the validity of a claim can be challenged on grounds of impossibility and non-impossibility.

Core Idea
A proof's validity can be denied if it depends on an unresolved issue of possibility.

Al-Razi's proof encountered several objections. The first objection targets a specific step where the conclusion 'then it is existential' is drawn. The objector denies the validity of this step, arguing that its foundation lies in the unresolved issue of impossibility and non-impossibility. This lesson examines this opening objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Note that the objection is short but points to a key logical issue.
The phrase 'قضية الامتناع واللا امتناع' refers to a contentious issue in kalam.
2
Understand the core of the objection
The objector says the conclusion 'then it is existential' lacks a valid basis because its support rests on the issue of impossibility and non-impossibility, which itself needs resolution.
This is a meta-level objection: the proof's step is only as strong as its presupposed principles.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection matters: if the validity of a proof depends on an unsettled question, the proof is not decisive. This is a common dialectical move in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فيكون هو وجودياً فيه منع سنده قضية الامتناع واللا امتناع. قوله : ( وإما أن يكون عدماً لتعين (آخر إن أريد بالتعين واللاتعين مفهوماهما فلا حصر؛ لجواز أن يكون التعين عدماً لمفهوم ،آخر وإن أريد ما صدقا عليه فلا نسلم أن ما صدق عليه اللاتعين فهو عدمي، فيكون نقيضه ثبوتياً . كيف واللاتعين صادق على ما سوى التعين من الحقائق ؟ . قوله : (فهذا) عدم العدم فهو (وجود فيه أن مفهوم العدم غير الوجود وكذا ما صدق هو عليه. وأيضاً إن كان المراد بالوجود والعدم في قوله إن كان عدماً وإن كان وجوداً مفهوم العدم والوجود، فالحصر ممنوع وسيورده المصنف على أصل الملازمة أيضاً. وإن كان المراد ما صدق
His saying: 'then it is existential' — there is a denial of its validity based on the issue of impossibility and non-impossibility. His saying: 'and it could be non-existence of another individuation' — if by individuation and non-individuation one means their concepts, then there is no exclusive division (ḥaṣr), because it is possible that individuation is non-existence of another concept; and if one means their referents, then we do not accept that the referent of non-individuation is non-existential, so its opposite would be positive. How can that be, when non-individuation is true of realities other than individuation? His saying: 'this is non-existence of non-existence, so it is existence' — there is in it that the concept of non-existence is other than existence, and likewise their referents. Also, if what is meant by existence and non-existence in his saying 'if it were non-existential' and 'if it were existential' are the concepts of non-existence and existence, then the exclusive division (ḥaṣr) is denied, and the author will mention it regarding the original implication as well. And if what is meant is the referent...

Commentary
This passage presents objections to al-Razi's proof and the beginnings of answers. The first objection challenges the step where the conclusion 'then it is existential' is claimed, saying that the validity of its ground depends on the issue of impossibility (imtināʿ) and non-impossibility (lā imtināʿ). The second objection concerns the division into non-existence of non-individuation or of another individuation: if we take the terms conceptually, the division is not exhaustive (lā ḥaṣr) because individuation could be non-existence of another concept; if we take them referentially, we do not accept that the referent of non-individuation is non-existential, so its opposite would be positive. It questions how non-individuation can be true of realities other than individuation. The third objection targets the idea that non-existence of non-existence is existence, arguing that the concept of non-existence is distinct from existence, so the inference is invalid. Additionally, if the proof uses the concepts of existence and non-existence, the exclusive division (ḥaṣr) is denied, and the author will address that later. The passage ends incompletely at the page break.

The Second Objection: Division of Individuation
Second objection criticizing the division of individuation in al-Razi's proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to al-Razi's proof, which critiques the division of individuation into existential and non-existential, and the distinction between conceptual and referential meaning.

Core Idea
A division is only exhaustive if terms are interpreted consistently as concepts or referents.

Al-Razi's proof relies on a division concerning individuation (taʿayyun). The second objection argues that this division is problematic. Whether we take 'individuation' and 'non-individuation' as concepts or as referents, the division fails to be exhaustive or acceptable. This lesson unpacks this nuanced objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its translation
Read the Arabic passage and the literal translation. Pay attention to the two scenarios: concept vs. referent.
The passage uses 'مفهوم' (concept) and 'ما صدق عليه' (referent).
2
Analyze the two interpretive angles
If 'individuation' and 'non-individuation' are taken as concepts, then the division is not exhaustive because individuation could be the non-existence of another concept. If taken as referents, the objector denies that the referent of non-individuation is non-existential.
In either case, the division fails to support the proof.
3
Consider the objection's conclusion
The objector asks: how can non-individuation be true of realities other than individuation? This undermines the entire division.
This shows the importance of clarity in terms when constructing arguments.

The Third Objection: Non-Existence of Non-Existence
Third objection attacking the inference that non-existence of non-existence is existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to al-Razi's proof, which argues that 'non-existence of non-existence is existence' is invalid because the concept of non-existence is distinct from existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence of non-existence does not entail existence because concepts of non-existence and existence are distinct.

The third objection targets a key step in al-Razi's proof that claims 'non-existence of non-existence is existence'. The objector points out that the concept of non-existence is different from the concept of existence, and similarly their referents are different. Additionally, the objection challenges the exclusive division if existence and non-existence are taken as concepts. This lesson explores these points.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and translation
Read the Arabic passage and note the two parts: first about concepts being distinct, second about the division if terms are concepts.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main idea is clear.
2
Understand the core argument
The objector says: even if we accept that something is non-existence of non-existence, that does not make it existence, because the concept of non-existence is other than the concept of existence. So the inference fails.
This is a semantic objection: the conclusion does not follow from the definition.
3
Analyze the additional point about ḥaṣr
If existence and non-existence in the proof are taken as concepts, then the exclusive division (ḥaṣr) is denied, as the author will mention later regarding the original implication.
This reinforces the pattern of objecting to divisions based on conceptual vs. referential interpretation.

Proof That Individuation is Existential and Its Refutation
Proof that ta'ayyun is wujudi and an answer denying the implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument for ta'ayyun being wujudi and the counter-argument that distinguishes 'adami from 'adam.

Core Idea
'Adami does not mean 'adam; a gap in meaning saves ta'ayyun from being wujudi.

This passage presents a key debate in kalam: whether individuation (ta'ayyun) is a positive attribute or a privation. The commentator lays out a proof that ta'ayyun must be existential, then offers a response that shows the proof's fallacy by clarifying the term 'adami. We'll trace the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Start by reading the proof that if ta'ayyun were 'adami it would entail 'adam, leading to a contradiction, so it must be wujudi.
Notice that the proof assumes 'adami = 'adam.
2
Understand the contradiction
The proof claims that the premises lead to a contradiction (khalaf), forcing the conclusion that ta'ayyun is wujudi.
3
Read the answer
Now read the answer, which denies the implication by distinguishing between 'adami and 'adam.
The answer says 'adami is opposite to wujudi, not equal to 'adam.
4
Grasp the key distinction
Understand that 'adami is a quality that does not necessitate non-existence; it is merely the opposite of existential.
This distinction is crucial for the correct understanding of ta'ayyun.
5
Reflect on the argument
Consider why this distinction matters for the broader theological discussion about the nature of individuation.
The debate affects whether ta'ayyun is a real or mental attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو أيضاً (وجود). فثبت أن كون التعين عدمياً يستلزم كونه وجودياً. هذا خلف فيكون وجودياً . ( والجواب : لا نسلم أنه لو كان ( التعين ( عدمياً لكان عدماً)، وإنما يلزم ذلك إذا كان العدمي بمعنى العدم أو مستلزماً له، وهو ممنوع؛ لأن العدمي يقابل الوجودي، كما أن العدم يقابل الوجود، فلو كان العدمي عدماً لكان الوجودي وجوداً، وليس كذلك بل المراد بالوجودي ما يكون ثبوته لموصوفه بوجوده له أي الوجودي هذا المقام، سوى ما ذكره المصنف من منع الملازمة من أن العدمي لا يلزم أن يكون عدماً كما لا يخفى على من تأمل وأجاد . قوله : (بمعنى العدم وعلى هذا التقدير تكون الملازمة بينهما بحسب التغاير الاعتباري. قوله : ( أو مستتلزماً له) بحيث يصدق عليه. قوله : ( لكان الوجودي وجوداً إذ لو كان غيره لم يصدق الوجود عليه، فيصدق العدم عليه مع عدم صدق العدمي عليه لصدق الوجودي عليه قوله : ( بل المراد إلخ) تقدير الكلام بل أعم منه لأن المراد بالوجودي إلخ . قوله : ( ما يكون ثبوته لموصوفه بوجوده (له) إن كان وجود العرض في نفسه هو وجوده في الموصوف، كما اختاره المحقق التفتازاني وصرح به الشيخ الرئيس فثبوت شيء لشيء أعم من عليه نفس الوجود والعدم فكذلك، وإن كان ما صدق عليه الوجود والعدم بالاشتقاق فنختار أن ذلك التعين معدوم، ولا يلزم أن يكون هذا التعين وجوداً؛ لأن عدم المعدوم ليس بوجود ولا موجود كما أشرنا إليه .
So it is also existence. Thus it is established that if individuation is non-existential, it entails that it is existential. This is a contradiction, so it is existential. (The answer: We do not accept that if individuation were non-existential, it would be non-existence. That would only follow if the non-existential meant non-existence or entailed it, and that is denied. Because the non-existential is opposite to the existential, just as non-existence is opposite to existence. For if the non-existential were non-existence, then the existential would be existence, but that is not the case. Rather, what is meant by the existential is that whose existence for its subject is by its existence for it, that is the existential in this context, aside from what the author mentioned of denying the entailment that the non-existential does not necessarily have to be non-existence, as is clear to anyone who reflects and excels. He said: (meaning non-existence) and on this estimation the entailment between them would be in terms of conceptual diversity. He said: (or that which entails it) such that it is predicated of it. He said: (then the existential would be existence) because if it were other than that, existence would not be predicated of it, so non-existence would be predicated of it while the non-existential is not predicated of it because the existential is predicated of it. He said: (But what is meant, etc.) The implied statement is rather more general than it, because what is meant by the existential, etc. He said: (that whose existence for its subject is by its existence for it). If the existence of the accident in itself is its existence in the subject, as chosen by al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani and stated by the Chief (Ibn Sina), then the existence of a thing for a thing is more general than existence and non-existence themselves, so it is likewise. And if it is that to which existence and non-existence are predicated derivationally, then we choose that that individuation is non-existent, and it does not follow that this individuation is existence, because the non-existence of a non-existent thing is neither existence nor existent, as we have indicated.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a proof and its refutation. The proof argues that if individuation (ta'ayyun) were non-existential ('adami), it would entail being non-existence ('adam), leading to a contradiction, so it must be existential (wujudi). The answer denies the implication: 'adami does not necessarily imply 'adam; the relationship is different. The commentator explains that 'adami is the opposite of wujudi, just as 'adam is opposite to wujud, but they are not the same. The objector's statement that a wujudi would be wujud is also denied. The commentator clarifies that wujudi means what is true of its subject by its existence for it. He considers two interpretations: if the accident's existence in itself is its existence in the subject, then predication is more general; if it is predicated derivationally, then we choose that individuation is non-existent, but it is not existence because the non-existence of a non-existent thing is not existent. The passage systematically defends the position that ta'ayyun can be 'adami without being 'adam.

Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes 'adami as a quality opposite to wujudi, not equivalent to 'adam. 'Adami does not entail non-existence.

The distinction between 'adami and 'adam is essential: being non-existential does not mean being non-existent, so ta'ayyun can be 'adami without being 'adam.

?

Objection of Similar Individuations Answered
The commentator answers an objection about similarity implying existentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the non-existential status of ta'ayyun by distinguishing different meanings of similarity and denying necessary entailment.

Core Idea
Similarity does not force existentiality; different meanings of 'similar' break the objection.

The commentator faces an objection: if one individuation (ta'ayyun) is existential, then all similar ones must be existential. He responds by analyzing what 'similar' could mean. This lesson unpacks his layered answer, showing how he protects the position that ta'ayyun is non-existential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the full English translation. Get a sense of the objection and the commentator's response.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the two meanings of similarity
Look for the two interpretations of 'similarity' that the commentator distinguishes: sharing in the concept of individuation vs. agreement in quiddity. Notice how each leads to a different outcome.
The commentator denies the objection under both interpretations.
3
Understand the Zayd and Amr example
Examine the example: Zayd and Amr are two similars, but one exists and the other does not. This shows that even if two things are similar in quiddity, existence of one does not entail existence of the other.
This counterexample undercuts the objection's inference.
4
Analyze the final entailment point
The passage ends with a clarification about the meaning of 'adami. The commentator insists that the objection's claim is not about characterization but about meaning or entailment, making the denial of entailment obstinate.
This reinforces that 'adami does not necessitate 'adam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والتعين الآخر مثله إن أراد بالمثلية المشاركة في التعينية فلا يتوهم من وجودية أحد المثلين بهذا المعنى وجودية الآخر، وإن أراد الاتفاق في الماهية فلا نسلم المثلية، لم لا يجوز أن تكون التعينات متخلفة في الماهية متشاركة في عارض هو مفهوم التعين، وعلى تقدير تسليمها لا يلزم من وجود أحد المتماثلات وجود جميعها، فإن زيداً وعمراً مثلان اتصاف أحدهما بالوجود والآخر بالعدم قوله : ( أو مستلزماً له بحيث يحمل عليه مواطأة، وإلا فلزوم ذلك ممنوع حينئذ. قوله : ( لكان الوجودي وجوداً) قد يمنع ذلك لجواز أن يتصف المتقابلان بشيء واحد، كالامتناع واللا امتناع المتصفين بالعدم وجوابه : أن ليس المراد بقوله : فلو كان العدمي عدماً أنه لو كان متصفاً بالعدم، بل أنه لو كان بمعنى العدم أو مستلزماً لحمله عليه مواطأة، فمنع الملازمة على هذا مكابرة، فتأمل.
He said: (And the other individuation is like it). If he means by similarity the sharing in individuation-ness, then do not imagine from the existentiality of one of the two similars in this sense the existentiality of the other. And if he means agreement in quiddity, then we do not grant the similarity, for why may the individuations not differ in quiddity while sharing in an accident which is the concept of individuation? And on the supposition of granting it, the existence of one of the similars does not entail the existence of all of them, for Zayd and Amr are two similars, one being characterized by existence and the other by non-existence. He said: (or that which entails it such that it is predicated of it in accordance, otherwise the entailment would be denied then). He said: (then the existential would be existence) – that might be denied because it is possible that two opposites are characterized by one thing, like impossibility and non-impossibility being characterized by non-existence. And its answer: that the meaning of his saying: 'for if the non-existential were non-existence' is not that it is characterized by non-existence, but rather that it is in the sense of non-existence or that which entails its predication in accordance. So the denial of the entailment on this is obstinacy, so reflect.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection concerning similar individuations. If one ta'ayyun is wujudi, does that imply all similar ones are? The answer distinguishes two interpretations of 'similarity'. If it means sharing in the concept of individuation, existentiality of one does not imply existentiality of another. If it means agreement in quiddity, similarity is denied because individuations may differ in quiddity while sharing only the accident of being individuated. Even if similarity is granted, existence of one similar does not entail existence of all, as with Zayd and Amr: one exists, the other does not. The passage then returns to the denial of the entailment that 'adami implies 'adam, reiterating that the objection's claim that if 'adami were 'adam then wujudi would be wujud is not about characterization but about meaning or entailment. Denying that entailment is obstinate. The overall effect is to reinforce the position that 'adami does not necessitate 'adam.

ماهية (mahiyya)
عارض (arid)
تعين (ta'ayyun)
عدمي ('adami)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that similarity in being an individuation implies existential nature.
Correction: Similarity may be only in the accident of individuation, not in quiddity, and even if quiddatively similar, existence of one does not entail existence of all.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator deny the objection that if one ta'ayyun is wujudi, all similar ta'ayyunat must be wujudi?
A) Because similarity is only in the accident of being individuated, not in quiddity
B) Because all ta'ayyunat are non-existential
C) Because Zayd and Amr are not similar
D) Because the objection is not about existence but about non-existence

Key Takeaway
The objection that one existential individuation forces all similar ones to be existential fails because 'similarity' can be only accidental, and even if quiddatively similar, existence is not entailed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a plausible objection to your belief, do you immediately accept it or do you scrutinize the words like the commentator does? This lesson shows that precision in meaning can dismantle a strong-sounding argument. Will you commit to examining the exact meanings of terms before being swayed by objections?
?
The commentator uses the example of Zayd and Amr—both similar in humanity, yet one exists and the other does not. Have you ever judged someone's spiritual state based on outward similarity to yourself, forgetting that existence and non-existence of faith can differ? Reflect on a relationship where you assumed another's faith was like yours, only to be surprised. What does this teach you about not presuming existential realities from similarity?
?
This entire refutation hinges on a careful distinction between accidental and essential similarity. In your own life, where do you confuse the accidental with the essential? For instance, do you equate outward religious practice (an accident) with true faith (the essence)? Ask Allah to give you the discernment to distinguish what truly defines a person from what merely accompanies them.

The Existential (Wujudi): Definition and Generality
The commentator defines wujudi and argues it is more general than wujud, refuting a key premise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'existential' (wujudi) and why it is more general than 'existent' (mawjud), and the implications for the non-existential (adami).

Core Idea
Existential is broader than existence: something can be existential without ever existing.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the term 'existential' (wujudi) as it applies to attributes. He distinguishes it from simply being existent (mawjud) and shows that wujudi is a wider category. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of non-existential attributes and for resolving the earlier argument about individuation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of wujudi
Read the passage from the beginning to where it defines wujudi as an attribute that does not subsist by itself but by another, and its subsistence is by its existence for it in the external.
The definition distinguishes wujudi from independent existents.
2
Understand the example of blackness
Notice the example of blackness, which is wujudi even if it never exists. This illustrates that wujudi does not require actual existence.
3
Grasp the generality of wujudi
The commentator explicitly states that wujudi is more general than mawjud (أعم من الموجود) in terms of actualization, because something can be wujudi without existing.
This is the key point that refutes the original argument.
4
See the implication for adami
From the generality, the commentator concludes that wujudi is not entailing existence, and similarly, adami (non-existential) is not entailing non-existence.
This implication is used to invalidate the previous objection.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وبالجملة : فلو كان العدمي هو العدم لكان الوجودي هو وجوده ،له فإن الأمور العدمية ثابتة لموصوفها وليس لها وجود فيها وإن كان مغايراً له كما اختاره الشارح قدس سره وسيجيء بيانه فثبوت شيء لشيء هو وجوده له، فلا بد أن يجعل الجار والمجرور، أعني له ظرفاً مستقراً، والمعنى بوجوده في نفسه حال كونه حاصلاً له. قوله : (لا) أن يكون إلخ) هذا العطف لبيان الفرق بين الوجودي وبين الأمور الاعتبارية بأن اتصاف الموصوف به في الخارج، بخلاف الأمور الاعتبارية فإن الاتصاف بها في العقل. قوله : (أعم) أي من حيث التحقق كما يدل عليه البيان . قوله : (عند قيامه) ظرف ليعرض لا ليصح، فلا يرد أن عند قيامه بموجود يجب له الوجود . قوله : ( وأما صدق إلخ) أي تحققه بقرينة قوله : ففي الموجودات حيث لم يقل فعلى . قوله : (وإذا كان كذلك أي إذا كان أعم منه في التحقق لم يكن الوجودي مستلزماً للوجود من حيث الحمل، وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( ويقرب ) لأنهما متلازمان في الصدق متغايران في المفهوم. قوله : (عرض) بالمعنى اللغوي، وإنه بالمعنى الاصطلاحي قسم الموجود. :قوله : ( وبالجملة إلخ ) هذا إبطال للملازمة المذكورة، فهو معارضة في المقدمة بجعل البداهة التي ادعاها المستدل بمنزلة الدليل، وإنما عبر عنه بقوله : بالجملة الشائع استعمالها في قوله : ( يصح أن يعرض له الوجود عند قيامه بموجود قيل عند القيام بموجود يجب عروض الوجود له لا أنه يصح . وأجيب بأن ليس المراد بالصحة الإمكان الخاص، بل مقابل الامتناع . قوله: (وبالجملة فلو كان العدمي هذا مناقضة ومنع للملازمة التي في قوله : لو كان عدمياً لكان إلخ.
He said: (But what is meant by the existential, etc.) He explained it so that its opposite, which is the intended subject of the explanation proper, i.e., the non-existential, is known. And the meaning is that the existential among attributes is what he mentioned by the indication of his saying: its subject, and the specification is based on the discourse being about the existentiality of individuation, which is among non-independent matters. That which does not subsist by itself, but rather subsists by another, and its subsistence by it is by its existence for it in the external, (like blackness) subsisting by the body, for its existence for it is only by its existence for it, (not that it) i.e., its existence for its subject, is by considering their existence in the intellect and its characterization by it therein, like genus subsisting by the body, for genus is not existent in the external subsisting by it therein, but rather its existence for it and its characterization by it is only in the mind. (And it) i.e., the existential in the mentioned sense, (is more general than the existent) not absolutely but in some respect, because of the possibility of an existential to which existence never accrues, like blackness that is always non-existent. For the summary of the meaning of the existential is that it is a concept that may be subject to existence when it subsists by an existent, so blackness, for example, is existential whether it exists or does not exist. As for the truth of the existent without the existential, that is in existents subsisting by themselves. And if that is so, then the existential is not entailing existence, and thus the non-existential is not entailing non-existence. (And close to this) which we mentioned in the interpretation of the existential (is what was said: that it) i.e., the existential (is an accident whose nature is external existence) whether it exists or does not exist. (And in summary: for if the non-existential were non-existence, then the existential would be... its existence for it. For non-existential things are established for their subject and they have no existence in it, even if it is different from it, as chosen by the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) and its explanation will come. So the existence of a thing for a thing is its existence for it, so the preposition and its object, i.e., 'for it', must be made a circumstantial adverb, and the meaning is 'by its existence in itself while being obtained for it'. He said: (not that it be, etc.) This conjunction is to explain the difference between the existential and the intentional matters, in that the characterization of the subject by it is in the external, unlike the intentional matters, for their characterization is in the intellect. He said: (more general) i.e., in terms of actualization, as the explanation indicates. He said: (when it subsists) is a temporal adverb for 'accrue', not for 'be valid', so it is not objected that when it subsists by an existent, existence must accrue to it. He said: (And as for the truth of the existent, etc.) i.e., its actualization, by the indication of his saying: 'for in existents' since he did not say 'upon'. He said: (And if that is so) i.e., if it is more general than it in actualization, then the existential is not entailing existence in terms of predication, and that is apparent. He said: (And it is close) because they are concomitant in truth and different in concept. He said: (An accident) in the linguistic sense, and in the technical sense it is a division of the existent. He said: (And in summary, etc.) This is an invalidation of the mentioned entailment, so it is a counter-argument in the premise by making the intuitive certainty claimed by the arguer into a proof. And he only expressed it by his saying: 'in summary', which is commonly used in... He said: (It may be subject to existence when it subsists by an existent) It was said: when subsisting by an existent, existence must accrue to it, not that it may. And it was answered that what is meant by 'may' is not specific possibility, but rather the opposite of impossibility. He said: (And in summary: for if the non-existential were...) This is a contradiction and denial of the entailment in his statement: 'if it were non-existential, it would be...' etc.

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'existential' (wujudi) in the context of the discussion about individuation. The commentator explains that wujudi does not mean 'existent' (mawjud) but rather a quality that can be subject to existence when it subsists by an existent. For example, blackness is wujudi even if it is eternally non-existent, because it can exist. This makes wujudi more general than mawjud (actual existence). Then the commentator draws the crucial implication: if wujudi is not necessarily existent, then 'adami (non-existential) is not necessarily non-existent ('adam). The passage also clarifies that non-existential things are established for their subject but have no existence in it, and that the expression 'existence for it' means a dependent attribute. The commentator contrasts this with intentional (mental) attributes. The summary section then uses this to invalidate the original entailment: if 'adami were 'adam, then wujudi would be wujud, but since wujudi is more general, the objection fails. The passage is pedagogical, defining a key term to resolve the earlier argument.

وجودي (wujudi)
موجود (mawjud)
عدم ('adam)
عدمي ('adami)
عرض (arad)
ثبوت (thubut)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That wujudi (existential) is synonymous with mawjud (existent).
Correction: The passage clarifies that wujudi is a broader category: something can be wujudi without actually existing, like blackness that is eternally non-existent.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say is the relationship between wujudi (existential) and mawjud (existent)?
A) Wujudi is identical to mawjud
B) Wujudi is more general than mawjud
C) Mawjud is more general than wujudi
D) They are unrelated

Key Takeaway
The existential (wujudi) is not the same as the existent (mawjud); it is a broader category that includes potential attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often describe yourself by qualities you believe you have—like 'patient' or 'generous'. But is that quality actually existent (mawjud) in your actions, or is it merely existential (wujudi) — a potential you assume but never manifest? Which one are you deceiving yourself about today?
?
The passage says blackness that never exists is still 'existential'. Think of a good deed you've never done but believe you are capable of. Does that belief (that you 'have' that potential) excuse you from actually doing it? What would it take to move that quality from wujudi to mawjud in your life?
?
If wujudi does not entail existence, then your flaws that you deny (like jealousy or pride) are still existential—they are possible attributes. Are you hiding from a potential sin by pretending it doesn't exist? What would change if you admitted, 'This is wujudi in me, even if I haven't acted on it'?

The Distinction Between 'Adami' and 'Ma'dum'
Clarifies the distinction between 'adami' and 'ma'dum' and refutes a potential objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the terms 'adami' (non-existential) and 'ma'dum' (non-existent) and why not everything that is not existence is necessarily non-existence.

Core Idea
In kalam, not being existence does not mean being non-existence.

In debates about determination (ta'ayyun), a key distinction arises: is something that is not existence automatically non-existence? The author clarifies that 'adami' and 'ma'dum' are not the same. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows how it prevents a false conclusion about the status of determination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from initial statement to objection to response.
2
Identify key terms and definitions
Find the terms 'wujudi', 'adami', 'mawjud', and 'ma'dum' in the passage. What do they mean? The author distinguishes between them.
The passage uses 'wujudi' in two senses: as 'not non-existence' and as 'existent'.
3
Follow the objection and response
An objection is raised: if 'wujudi' means existent, then ta'ayyun must be non-existent if not existent. The response shows this does not follow because 'ma'dum' can be a thing in itself.
4
Reflect on the logical implication
Why is it important that concepts other than existence and non-existence are infinite? How does this prevent a hasty conclusion about ta'ayyun?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجود، فلا حصر؛ إذ المفهومات المغايرة لمفهومي الوجود والعدم غير متناهية، فلا يلزم من أن لا يكون التعين وجوداً أن يكون عدماً. أو كان الوجودي ما ليس بعدم، الأمور الاعتبارية وجودية؛ إذ يصدق عليها أنها ليست نفس مفهوم العدم ولا قائل به. فإن قلت: الوجودي والعدمي قد يطلقان بمعنى الموجود والمعدوم أيضاً، وهو المناسب للمقام، وإذا لم يكن التعين موجوداً كان معدوماً قطعاً. قلت: فحينئذ يجاب بأن التعين إذا كان معدوماً لم يلزم أن يكون عدماً لشيء آخر، بل ربما كان شيئاً معدوماً في نفسه، وهو ظاهر.
Existence, so no restriction; because the concepts different from the two concepts of existence and non-existence are infinite. So it does not follow from the fact that determination (ta'ayyun) is not existence that it is non-existence. Or was the existential (al-wujudi) that which is not non-existence? The conceptual matters (al-umur al-i'tibariyya) are existential, because they are truly not the same concept as non-existence, and no one says so. For if you say: 'wujudi' and 'adami' are also used in the sense of existent (al-mawjud) and non-existent (al-ma'dum), and that is appropriate for the context, and if determination is not existent then it is definitely non-existent. I say: then the answer is that if determination is non-existent, it does not necessarily follow that it is a non-existence for something else; rather, perhaps it is a non-existent thing in itself, and that is apparent.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a terminological clarification and a refutation of a potential objection. It begins by stating that the concepts other than existence and non-existence are infinite, so one cannot simply infer that if determination (ta'ayyun) is not existence then it is non-existence. It then considers the possibility that 'wujudi' (existential) is defined as 'what is not non-existence', noting that mental constructs (al-umur al-i'tibariyya) are considered existential in that sense because they are not identical to the concept of non-existence, and no one claims they are. An objection is raised: 'wujudi' and 'adami' are also used synonymously with existent and non-existent, and in that sense if determination is not existent, it must be non-existent. The author replies that even if determination is non-existent (ma'dum), it does not entail that it is a non-existence ('adam) for something else; it may simply be a non-existent thing in itself. This distinction prevents a hasty conclusion that determination is a pure non-existence.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
العدم (al-'adam)
الوجودي (al-wujudi)
العدمي (al-'adami)
التعين (al-ta'ayyun)
الموجود (al-mawjud)
المعدوم (al-ma'dum)
الأمور الاعتبارية (al-umur al-i'tibariyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'adami' is synonymous with 'ma'dum' and that being non-existential implies being non-existent.
Correction: The passage shows that 'adami' (non-existential) refers to what is not existence, while 'ma'dum' (non-existent) refers to something that is not existent. Not everything that is not existence is non-existent; conceptual matters are existential in the first sense but may be non-existent in the second sense.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does it not follow that if ta'ayyun is not existence it must be non-existence?
A) Because the concepts other than existence and non-existence are infinite.
B) Because ta'ayyun is a mental construct.
C) Because the mutakallimun said so.
D) Because existence and non-existence are opposites.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, the term 'adami' does not imply non-existence; it refers to what is not existence, and non-existence ('adam) is different from being non-existent (ma'dum).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of 'non-existence', do you instinctively imagine a total void or absence? How does the lesson's distinction between ma'dum and 'adam challenge you to rethink what it means for something to be real or unreal?
?
You regularly deal with conceptual matters like intentions, meanings, or relationships. Are you quick to label them as 'just mental' and therefore less real? What would change if you saw them as wujudi – existent in a meaningful sense?
?
Are there aspects of your faith (like the attributes of Allah or the reality of the afterlife) that you treat as merely 'adami' (not existence) but not as ma'dum? How does this lesson call you to reassert their existential weight in your heart?

The Circularity Argument and the Failure of the Initial Response
The mutakallimun present the circularity argument and a first response that is found inadequate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's first proof that ta'ayyun is 'adami, and why the initial answer—that the essence is distinct by itself—fails to resolve the circularity.

Core Idea
The distinctness of instances requires ta'ayyun; self-distinctness of essence is irrelevant.

This lesson introduces the first of two arguments the theologians (mutakallimun) give for the non-existential (adami) nature of determination (ta'ayyun). The argument is based on circularity: if ta'ayyun were existential, its joining to the essence would require the essence to already be distinct, but distinctness itself depends on ta'ayyun. The initial answer—that the essence is distinct by itself—is examined and found wanting because it confuses the distinctness of the essence as a whole with the distinctness of its particular instances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation. Notice the structure: statement of proof, then a counter-answer, then a critique.
2
Identify the circularity argument
The mutakallimun claim that if ta'ayyun existed, its joining would depend on tamayyuz, and tamayyuz would depend on joining, causing a circle. What is tamayyuz here? The distinctness of the essence from other essences or of its instances?
The passage clarifies later that it is the distinctness of instances.
3
Examine the initial answer
The answer says the essence is distinct by itself. How does this attempt to break the circle? Why does the author say there is a problem (fīhi naẓar)?
The problem is that the distinctness of one instance from another is at issue, and that cannot be by the essence itself.
4
Understand the critique
The passage explains: what is meant is the distinctness of one instance (hissa) of the essence from another. Without such distinctness, ta'ayyun cannot specify one instance over another. This distinctness must come from ta'ayyun itself.
Thus the initial answer fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المتكلمون فقالوا: التعين أمر عدمي لوجهين: الأول لو كان التعين وجودياً لتوقف انضمامه إلى الماهية على تميزها وتميزها موقوف على انضمامه إليها. فيدور. وأجيب عنه: بأن الماهية ممتازة عن غيرها بذاتها لا بانضمام التعين إليها. وفيه نظر؛ إذ مرادهم امتياز حصة من الماهية عن حصة أخرى منها؛ إذ لولا امتياز إحداهما عن الأخرى لم يكن اختصاص التعين بإحداهما وانضمامه إليها أولى من العكس. وذلك أي امتياز الحصة عن الحصة إنما يكون بالتعين لا بذات الماهية، بل الجواب أن يقال: الانضمام مع الامتياز زماناً، وإن كان متقدماً عليه ذاتاً، ولا استحالة في ذلك، كما في اختصاص الفصول بحصص الأجناس. وتوضيحه أن التعين أو الفصل ينضم انتزاعياً، فلا انضمام في الخارج حتى يتوقف على تميزها، والانضمام في الذهن وإن توقف على تميزها وتصورها لكن تميزها الذهني لا يتوقف على انضمامه إليها، بل على انتزاعه منها. فما قيل: إنه جار على تقدير كونه عدمياً أيضاً. وهم.
As for the theologians (al-mutakallimun), they said: Determination (al-ta'ayyun) is a non-existential matter (amr 'adami) for two reasons: The first: If determination were existential, then its joining (indimam) to the essence (al-mahiyya) would depend on the essence's distinctness (tamayyuz), and its distinctness would depend on its joining to it, leading to circularity (dawr). It was answered: That the essence is distinct from other essences by itself, not by the joining of determination to it. But there is a problem in this answer: For what they mean is the distinctness of one instance (hissa) of the essence from another instance of it; for were there no distinctness of one from the other, the specification of determination to one of them and its joining to it would not be more appropriate than the opposite. And that, i.e., the distinctness of an instance from an instance, is only through determination, not by the essence itself. Rather, the answer is to say: The joining is simultaneous with the distinctness in time (zaman), even if it is prior to it in essence (dhatan), and there is no impossibility in that, as in the specification of differentiate (fusul) to the instances of genera. Its clarification is that determination or the differentia joins in an abstracted manner (intiza'i), so there is no joining in the external world that would depend on the distinctness of the essence, and joining in the mind, even if it depends on its distinctness and conception, yet its mental distinctness does not depend on its joining to it, but rather on its abstraction from it. So what has been said—that this reasoning applies also on the supposition that it is 'adami—is an illusion (wahm).
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the mutakallimun that determination (ta'ayyun) is a non-existential thing ('adami). The proof is based on circularity: if ta'ayyun were existential, its joining to the essence would require the essence to already be distinct, but the essence's distinctness (of its instances) depends on ta'ayyun, creating a circle. The initial answer—that the essence is distinct by itself, not by ta'ayyun—is then criticized: the relevant distinctness is of one instance of the essence from another, and that can only be through ta'ayyun. A better answer is then provided: the joining of ta'ayyun and the distinctness of the instance are simultaneous in time, though ta'ayyun is prior in essence (as cause). This is analogous to how differentiate are specified to instances of genera. Furthermore, ta'ayyun joins in an abstracted manner (intiza'i), meaning there is no real external joining that would require prior distinctness; mental distinctness depends on abstraction from ta'ayyun, not on its joining. Thus the circularity objection is refuted, and the claim that this reasoning also works if ta'ayyun is 'adami is dismissed as mistaken.

Key Terms
المتكلمون
التعين
العدم
الماهية
الانضمام
التميز
الدور
الحصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the distinctness of an essence from other essences is sufficient to avoid circularity in the joining of ta'ayyun.
Correction: The relevant distinctness is between instances of the same essence, which cannot be by the essence itself.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with the initial answer to the circularity argument?
A) The answer is logically incoherent.
B) The answer addresses the wrong type of distinctness (essence-as-a-whole vs. instances).
C) The answer assumes ta'ayyun is existential.
D) The answer claims the essence is not distinct at all.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's circularity argument relies on the distinctness of instances, which must come from ta'ayyun, so the initial self-distinctness answer does not resolve it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mutakallimun argue that your individuality (ta'ayyun) is not a real attribute added to your essence but a mere negation. If your 'self' is ultimately a non-existential distinction, what does that do to your pride in your unique qualities? Are you clinging to an illusion of independent identity?
?
If the distinctness of your very existence (as a particular instance of humanity) is not due to your essence but to something non-existential, how does that reshape your understanding of your dependence on Allah for even your individuality? Will you let go of the false sense of self-sufficiency and acknowledge that you are nothing but a contingent being?

The Proper Resolution: Temporal Simultaneity and Abstract Joining
Presents the correct answer to the circularity argument and clarifies the mode of joining.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct answer to the circularity argument, which involves temporal simultaneity and the abstract (intiza'i) mode of joining, and how this resolves the circularity without requiring ta'ayyun to be existential.

Core Idea
Ta'ayyun joins in abstraction, so no real external circularity arises.

Having seen that the initial answer fails, the author now presents the proper solution. The key insight is that the joining of ta'ayyun and the distinctness of the instance occur simultaneously in time, even if ta'ayyun is ontologically prior. Moreover, ta'ayyun joins in an abstracted manner (intiza'i), not in external reality. This means the circularity is avoided because there is no real external joining that would require prior distinctness; the mental distinctness of the instance depends on abstraction from ta'ayyun, not on a real joining. The passage also dismisses an objection that the same reasoning would apply even if ta'ayyun were 'adami.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note how the correct answer is introduced: 'بل الجواب أن يقال...'
2
Understand temporal simultaneity
The answer says that the joining and the distinctness are simultaneous in time, although the joining is prior in essence. This avoids the circularity because temporal dependence is not required.
This is similar to the case of differentiate and genera.
3
Grasp the concept of abstract joining (intiza')
Ta'ayyun (or the differentia) joins in an abstracted manner, meaning in the mind, not in external reality. In the mind, the distinctness of the essence does not depend on the joining but on the abstraction from the essence itself.
Thus, there is no vicious circle.
4
Examine the dismissal of the objection
The passage ends by rejecting the claim that this reasoning would also apply if ta'ayyun were 'adami. This is an illusion (wahm). Why?
Because the abstract joining and temporal simultaneity are consistent with ta'ayyun being 'adami as well.

Source Text

?

How will you avoid the error of thinking that the intention must come first in time for the action to be valid?
