The Objection to the Need for a Recipient and Its Refutation
Objection to the requirement of a recipient for individuation and its refutation via the distinct agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection against the necessity of a recipient for individuation and its refutation via the concept of the distinct agent.

Core Idea
Individuals can multiply through distinct agents, not just recipients.

This passage presents an objection to the earlier argument that required a recipient and matter to explain multiplicity. The objector asks: why allow essences to be determined by successive accidental attributes ad infinitum? This would eliminate the need for a recipient. The response introduces the concept of a distinct agent (al-mubayin) that can cause individuation through a particular relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection that proposes successive accidental attributes for essences, making a recipient unnecessary.
Notice the phrase 'على سبيل التعاقب إلى ما لا يتناهى'.
2
Understand the response
The response argues that a distinct agent (al-mubayin) with a specific relation can cause individuation, and multiple agents yield multiple individuals.
The term 'المباين' refers to something separate or distinct.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this response shifts the cause of individuation from the recipient to the agent, preserving the need for a cause.
This refutes the objection without eliminating causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا نهاية له اتجه لنا أن نقول : (فلم) لا يجوز تعين الماهيات بصفاتها العارضة لها كذلك ) أي على سبيل التعاقب إلى ما لا يتناهى فلا حاجة حينئذ في تعدد أفراد الماهية النوعية إلى القابل والمادة. هذا وقد يجاب عن أصل الدليل أيضا بجواز أن يكون للمباين نسبة مخصوصة بها تقتضي تشخصاً معيناً، وإذا تعدد الفاعل المباين تعدد أفراد الماهية أيضاً. (ومنهم من جعل هذا الاعتراض ( دليلاً على أن التعين ليس وجودياً) فقال : لو كان تعين الشخص الذي له ما يشاركه في نوعه وجودياً لكان له علة، فعلته إن كانت الماهية انحصر نوعها في شخصها، وإن كانت القابل، فتعين القابل إن كان بماهيته انحصر نوعه في شخص، وإن كان بقابل آخر لزم التسلسل، وإن كان بالمقبول لزم الدور، والكل باطل ولا يجوز أن تكون العلة أمراً مبايناً، فلا يكون التعين أمراً وجودياً. وقد يقال في إثبات كون التعين عدمياً: (التعين معناه أنه ليس غيره، وهو سلب لا وجود له في الخارج، ومنع بأن هذا السلب الذي ذكرتموه ليس هو التعين بل هو (لازم) له وليس يلزم من كون اللازم عدمياً كون الملزوم كذلك.
Ad infinitum, it is appropriate for us to say: (then why) is it not permissible for the determinations of essences by their accidental attributes to be likewise?) i.e., in the manner of succession ad infinitum, so then there is no need in the multiplicity of individuals of the specific essence for a recipient and matter. This, and also the original proof may be answered by the permissibility of the distinct (al-mubayin) having a particular relation that necessitates a specific individuation, and if the distinct agent multiplies, the individuals of the essence also multiply. (And some of them made this objection a proof that determination is not existent). He said: if the determination of a person that shares with others its type were existent, it would have a cause. Its cause: if it is the essence, then its type would be confined to its individual; if it is the recipient, then the determination of the recipient: if by its essence, its type is confined to one individual; if by another recipient, an infinite regress follows; if by the accepted (al-maqbul), circularity follows. All are false, and it is not permissible for the cause to be a distinct thing. Thus determination is not an existent thing. And it may be said in proving that determination is non-existent: (determination means that it is not other than it, which is a negation with no existence in the external world. And it is refuted by saying that this negation you mentioned is not determination but is a necessary property (lazim) of it, and it is not necessary that if the necessary property is non-existent, the thing qualified by it is likewise.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative objection and a further proof that determination (al-ta'ayyun) is non-existent ('adami). It begins with an objection: if the earlier response allowed successive accidents for the user's recipient, why not allow successive accidental attributes for the essences themselves? This would obviate the need for a recipient or matter to explain multiplicity. Additionally, another answer is mentioned: a distinct agent (al-mubayin) can have a particular relation that causes individuation, and its multiplicity explains the multiplicity of individuals. Then, some scholars use this very objection as a proof that determination is not an existent entity. They argue: if determination were existent, it would need a cause. That cause would be either the essence (leading to limitation to one individual), the recipient (again leading to limitation or regress), or the accepted form (leading to circularity). Since all these options fail, and the cause cannot be a distinct thing, determination must not be existent. Finally, another argument for the non-existence of determination is proposed: 'determination means that it is not other than it'—a negation with no external existence. However, this is refuted: the negation is merely a necessary property (lazim) of determination, not the determination itself. The fact that a necessary property is non-existent does not imply that the thing it characterizes is non-existent.

Key Terms
التعين
الماهية
القابل
المادة
المباين
النسبة

Checkpoint
What is the main idea of the refutation to the objection about successive accidental attributes?
A) The objection is valid and the need for a recipient is eliminated
B) The distinct agent's relation can cause individuation, so the need for a cause remains
C) The essences themselves are the cause of determination
D) Infinite regress is impossible

Key Takeaway
The objection that essences can be determined by successive accidental attributes is refuted by pointing to the distinct agent as the true cause of individuation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your unique identity and personality are merely the product of your environment or biology, without attributing them to the direct creative act of Allah? How would your self-perception change if you truly believed that your individuation is caused by the distinct agency of your Creator?
?
When you consider the countless distinct individuals around you, do you recognize each as a deliberate act of divine specification, or do you take their uniqueness for granted? Pause and thank Allah for making you distinct from others, and ask yourself: in what ways have I failed to live up to the purpose for which He specifically designated me?
?
The passage teaches that a cause of individuation is necessary and is found in the agent. What in your life have you attributed to mere chance or circumstance that actually points directly to Allah's specific will for you? Commit today to consciously acknowledge His role in the details of your existence, even the smallest ones.

The Proof that Determination is Non-Existent and Its Refutation
Proof that determination is non-existent via causal analysis, and its refutation by distinguishing determination from its necessary property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that determination is non-existent based on its cause, and why it is refuted by distinguishing determination from its necessary property.

Core Idea
Determination is not mere negation; its definition is a necessary property, not its essence.

This passage presents a proof that determination (al-ta'ayyun) is not an existent entity. It argues that if determination were existent, it would require a cause, leading to absurdities like infinite regress or circularity. Then another argument adds that determination means 'not being other,' a negation. However, the refutation clarifies that this negation is merely a necessary property of determination, not determination itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the causal proof
Read the argument that if determination were existent, it would need a cause, and examine the possible causes.
The proof considers essence, recipient, and other recipient as causes.
2
Analyze the failures
Each candidate cause leads to an absurdity: confinement to one individual, infinite regress, or circularity.
The conclusion is that determination cannot be existent.
3
Read the definition argument
Another argument defines determination as 'not being other', a negation, implying non-existence.
This is a separate attempt to prove non-existence.
4
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that 'not being other' is a necessary property (lazim), not determination itself. Non-existence of a property does not negate the subject.
This refutes both proofs by showing a confusion between essence and necessary property.

The Self-Evidence of Necessity, Contingency, and Impossibility
Establishing the self-evidence of the three rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concepts of necessity, contingency, and impossibility are considered self-evident in Islamic theology and how they are known to all rational people without need for proof.

Core Idea
These three concepts are the basic building blocks of rational thought, known to everyone without education.

In this lesson, we examine the opening of the Third Observatory in al-Iji's work, where he establishes that necessity, contingency, and impossibility are self-evident. The text argues that even those incapable of theoretical reasoning possess these concepts, as shown by everyday language and usage. We will explore the primary text and its commentary to understand the argument for their self-evidence and the implications for theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic primary text and its translation. Notice that the author states these concepts are 'daruriyya' (self-evident) and provides an everyday example.
Pay attention to the example of man, writer, and stone.
2
Understand the everyday example
The example shows that every rational person knows that man must be an animal, may be a writer, and cannot be a stone. This illustrates that specific instances of necessity, possibility, and impossibility are known to everyone.
3
Analyze the definitional circularity
The passage demonstrates that any attempt to define these terms inevitably uses one of them or their negations, indicating they are primitive and not definable in simpler terms.
For example, the necessary is defined as that whose non-existence is impossible.
4
Explore the commentary on derivatives
The commentator explains that derivatives (necessary, possible, impossible) are also self-evident because the absolute relation is known and the root concept is self-evident.
This reinforces the self-evidence of the entire family of concepts.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why establishing self-evidence is important for kalam: it grounds all theological arguments on universally accessible foundations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث في الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع ] والقدم والحدوث ) وفيه مقاصد ) ستة : والمقصد الأول : تصورات في الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع ] وكذا تصورات ما يشتق منها أعني الواجب والممكن والممتنع ( ضرورية ) فإن من لا يقدر على الاكتساب أصلاً يعرف هذه المفهومات، ألا ترى أن كل عاقل يعلم أن الإنسان يجب كونه حيواناً، ويمكن كونه كاتباً، ويمتنع كونه حجراً، إلى غير ذلك من موارد الاستعمال. (ومن رام تعريفها فقد عرف كل واحد من الثلاثة إما بأحد الآخرين، أو بسلبه إذ لم يزد على أن يقول : الواجب ما يمتنع عدمه، أو ما لا يمكن عدمه، فإذا قيل :له وما الممتنع قال : ما يجب عدمه، أو ما لا يمكن وجوده، وإذا قيل. قوله : ( والقدم والحدوث زادهما إشارة إلى أنهما داخلان في عوارض الماهية، وليس البحث عنهما بحثاً عن الوجوب والإمكان إلا أنه ترك ذكرهما في العنوان اختصاراً . قوله وكذا تصورات إلخ) لأن النسبة المطلقة إلى شيء ما معلومة، فليس جهالة المشتقات إلا باعتبار المشتق منه فإذا كان بديهياً كان المشتق بديهياً . قوله : ( ألا ترى إلخ) يعني أن كل عاقل سواء كان قادراً على النظر أو لا، كالبله والصبيان يعلم أن بعض المفهومات ضروري الثبوت وبعضها ضروري السلب وبعضها ليس ضروري الثبوت والسلب، فالوجوب الخاص والامتناع الخاص والإمكان الخاص التي تعرض لبعض المفهومات بالقياس إلى بعض آخر حاصلة له من غير كسب فإذا جرد هذه الأمور الجزئية عن خصوصياتها الحاصلة لها بالقياس إلى الطرفين حصل المفهومات الكلية لها بنفسها لا بأمور صادقة عليها، فتكون معلومة بالكنه الإجمالي، وهذه الأمور التي هي كيفيات نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع بعينها المبحوث عنها هاهنا لا فرق إلا باعتبار خصوصية المحمول أعني الوجود، وبما
The Third Observatory: On Necessity, Contingency, and Impossibility, and Eternity and Temporal Origination. It contains six aims. The First Aim: Concepts of necessity, contingency, and impossibility, and also concepts derived from them, namely the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, are self-evident. For one who is unable to acquire knowledge at all knows these concepts. Do you not see that every rational person knows that man must be an animal, may be a writer, and cannot be a stone, and other such instances of usage. Whoever seeks to define them, defines each of the three either by one of the others or by its negation, for he says no more than: the necessary is that whose non-existence is impossible, or that whose non-existence is not possible. If it is said to him: what is the impossible? he says: what must not exist, or what cannot exist. And if it is said... His statement: (And eternity and temporal origination) he added them to indicate that they are included among the accidents of the essence, and the investigation about them is not an investigation about necessity and contingency, but he omitted them from the title for brevity. His statement: (And also concepts, etc.) because the absolute relation to something is known, so the ignorance of derivatives is only due to the derivative from; if it is self-evident, the derivative becomes self-evident. His statement: (Do you not see, etc.) means that every rational person, whether capable of theoretical reasoning or not, like the imbecile and children, knows that some concepts are necessary in affirmation, some necessary in negation, and some are not necessary in affirmation or negation. So the specific necessity, specific impossibility, and specific contingency that occur to some concepts in relation to others are acquired by him without effort. When these particular items are stripped of their particularities obtained in relation to the two sides, the universal concepts of them are obtained by themselves, not by things true of them. Thus they are known by an overall essential knowledge. These things that are the qualities of the relation of the predicate to the subject are exactly what are investigated here, with no difference except considering the specificity of the predicate, namely existence. And by what...
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the self-evidence (badaha) of the concepts of necessity (wujub), contingency (imkan), and impossibility (imtina'), along with their derivatives (necessary, possible, impossible). The primary text states that these concepts are self-evident (daruriyya) because even those incapable of theoretical reasoning know them, as shown by everyday usage (e.g., man, writer, stone). Attempts to define them inevitably use one of the others or their negation, indicating they are primitive. The commentator then elaborates on several points: First, the inclusion of eternity (qidam) and temporal origination (huduth) in the title is noted; these are also accidents of the essence but not essential to this discussion. Second, the derivatives are self-evident because the absolute relation (al-nisba al-mutlaqa) is known, and if the root is self-evident, the derivative is too. Third, the example shows that all rational people, including the mentally deficient, have specific notions of necessity, impossibility, and contingency in particular cases; by abstracting from particularities, universal concepts are obtained. These concepts are the very qualities of the relation of predicate to subject, but here the predicate is specifically existence. The passage ends with 'وبما' (and by what) which is incomplete.

إمكان
امتناع
ضروري
بديهي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these concepts are purely metaphysical and not known to ordinary people.
Correction: The text argues they are self-evident through everyday usage, known even to the mentally deficient and children.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are defined by the same words.
B) Because the absolute relation is known and the root is self-evident.
C) Because everyone uses them in daily life.
D) Because they are created by God.

Key Takeaway
The concepts of necessity, contingency, and impossibility are self-evident, known to all rational beings, and form the foundation of theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe God is necessary (Wajib al-Wujud). Then why do you live as if your own existence is the center of reality? What would change if you truly felt His necessity in your daily choices?
?
The passage says even a child knows what is necessary and impossible. In what area of your life are you suppressing that innate knowledge—perhaps pretending that a sin is impossible to avoid or that a good deed is unnecessary?
?
If contingency means your existence is not necessary, then everything you have is a gift. Does your attitude toward wealth, health, or relationships reflect that, or do you act as if they are necessary?

The Three Concepts Are Intuitive
The author states that the concepts of necessity, possibility, and impossibility are intuitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the concepts of necessity, possibility, and impossibility are self-evident and universally recognized by rational persons.

Core Idea
These three rational judgments are innate, not acquired.

In this opening of the first chapter, the author establishes a foundational claim: the three concepts that underpin all theological reasoning are not learned but intuitive. He uses everyday examples to show that even the simplest mind grasps them. Let's read the passage and see how he proves this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the plain meaning.
The passage is short but dense; pay attention to the examples.
2
Identify the three concepts and their derivatives
The passage lists necessity (wujub), possibility (imkan), and impossibility (imtina'), and their derived terms: necessary, possible, impossible. Notice how they are paired.
These six terms are foundational for kalam.
3
Analyze the author's argument
The author claims these concepts are daruriyya (self-evident). He supports this by saying even someone incapable of acquisition knows them, and gives a concrete example: man must be animal, may be writer, cannot be stone.
The example shows the three judgments in action.
4
Reflect on why this matters
If these concepts are intuitive, then all rational discourse about Allah's attributes and actions is built on common ground. This is the starting point for kalam reasoning.
Connect to the learning objective.

Commentary
This passage identifies the first maqṣad of the third marṣad, which concerns the concepts of necessity (al-wujūb), possibility (al-imkān), and impossibility (al-imtināʿ), as well as their derived terms: the necessary (al-wājib), the possible (al-mumkin), and the impossible (al-mumtaniʿ). Circular Definitions of the Three Judgments
The author critiques circular definitions of necessary, impossible, possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conventional definitions of necessary, impossible, and possible are circular and what this implies about their nature.

Core Idea
The three judgments cannot be defined without each other—they are cyclical.

In this passage, the author examines attempts to define the three rational judgments—necessary, impossible, possible. He shows that every definition ultimately refers back to one of the other terms, creating a clear circularity. This reveals that these concepts are primitive and cannot be genuinely defined.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Notice the structure of definitions.
The author gives examples of how each concept is defined.
2
Identify the circular pattern
Observe how each definition of wājib, mumtaniʿ, and mumkin uses one of the other two terms. This creates a circle.
For instance, wājib is defined by mumtaniʿ or mumkin.
3
Extend to abstract nouns
The author shows that the same circularity applies to the derived nouns wujūb, imtināʿ, and imkān.
4
Understand the conclusion
Because of the circularity, these definitions cannot be real or indicative—only verbal. Grasp why this matters for kalam.
Real definitions explain unknown meanings; indicative definitions remove ambiguity. These do neither.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن رام تعريفها فقد عرف كل واحد من الثلاثة إما بأحد الآخرين، أو بسلبه إذ لم يزد على أن يقول: الواجب ما يمتنع عدمه، أو ما لا يمكن عدمه، فإذا قيل له وما الممتنع قال: ما يجب عدمه، أو ما لا يمكن وجوده، وإذا قيل له ما الممكن؟ قال: ما لا يجب وجوده ولا عدمه أو ما لا يمتنع وجوده ولا عدمه، فيأخذ كلاً الثلاثة من في تعريف الآخر ألا ترى أنه عرف الواجب الوجود تارة بالممتنع المنسوب إلى العدم، وأخرى بسلب الممكن المنسوب إلى العدم أيضاً وعرف الممتنع الوجود تارة بالواجب المنسوب إلى العدم، وأخرى بسلب الممكن المنسوب إلى الوجود، وعرف الممكن أولاً بسلب الواجب المنسوب إلى الوجود والعدم معاً، وثانياً بسلب الممتنع المنسوب إليهما أيضاً. (وأنه دور ظاهر). وقس على ذلك تعريفات ما اشتق منه هذه الأمور فيقال: الوجوب امتناع العدم، أولا إمكان العدم والامتناع وجوب العدم أولا إمكان الوجود، والإمكان: لا الوجود والعدم أولا امتناعهما فلا يجوز أن تكون هذه التعريفات حقيقية، ولا تنبيهية بالقياس إلى شخص واحد.
And whoever attempts to define them has defined each of the three either by one of the other two, or by its negation, since he does no more than say: The necessary is that whose non-existence is impossible, or that whose non-existence is not possible. So if it is said to him: And what is the impossible? He says: That whose existence is necessary, or that whose existence is not possible. And if it is said: What is the possible? He says: That whose existence and non-existence are not necessary, or that whose existence and non-existence are not impossible. So he takes each of the three in defining the other. Do you not see that he defined necessary existence sometimes by the impossible relative to non-existence, and sometimes by the negation of the possible relative to non-existence; and he defined impossible existence sometimes by the necessary relative to non-existence, and sometimes by the negation of the possible relative to existence; and he defined the possible first by the negation of the necessary relative to existence and non-existence together, and second by the negation of the impossible relative to both. (And it is a clear circle). And analogously for the definitions of what is derived from these things, so it is said: Necessity is impossibility of non-existence, or non-possibility of non-existence; impossibility is necessity of non-existence, or non-possibility of existence; and possibility is non-necessity of existence and non-existence, or non-impossibility of both. So these definitions cannot be real (ḥaqīqiyya) nor indicative (tanbīhiyya) for a single person.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the three concepts by examining how people define them. The author observes that typical definitions of the necessary (al-wājib), the impossible (al-mumtaniʿ), and the possible (al-mumkin) invariably refer back to one another. For example, the necessary is defined as 'that whose non-existence is impossible' or 'that whose non-existence is not possible'; the impossible is defined as 'that whose non-existence is necessary' or 'that whose existence is not possible'; the possible is defined as 'that whose existence and non-existence are not necessary' or 'that whose existence and non-existence are not impossible'. This shows that each term is defined by the other two, resulting in a clear circularity (dawr ẓāhir). The author then extends this to the abstract nouns (al-wujūb, al-imtināʿ, al-imkān), showing similar circular definitions. Because of this circularity, these definitions cannot be real (ḥaqīqiyya) definitions that would be used to convey unknown meanings, nor indicative (tanbīhiyya) definitions that would clear away ambiguity from an already known meaning. Instead, they can only be verbal (lafẓiyya) definitions that simply label the known concepts. The author implies that since the concepts are intuitive, the circularity is not harmful for identifying the terms, but it does show that the definitions are not genuine explanations.

الممتنع

الإمكان
الامتناع
حقيقي
تنبيهي
لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these definitions are meaningful explanations of the concepts.
Correction: The author shows they are circular and therefore only verbal, not real or indicative definitions.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the given definitions of the three concepts to be circular?
A) Because each definition uses one of the other two concepts
B) Because they are all synonyms
C) Because they rely on Islamic revelation
D) Because they are too long

Key Takeaway
The conventional definitions of necessary, impossible, and possible are circular and therefore only verbal, not real explanations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you explain basic Islamic beliefs, do you ever catch yourself using words that you cannot define without falling into a circle? How does this lesson push you to seek deeper, more rigorous understanding?
?
What would it mean for you to admit that some of your most cherished theological concepts are known intuitively, not through airtight definitions? Are you willing to trust that intuition while still striving for clarity?
?
This lesson shows that even scholars fall into circularity when defining fundamental terms. Where in your own thinking might you be relying on circular reasoning without realizing it? What will you do to break the cycle?

Necessity as the Most Evident Judgment
The commentator argues that necessity is the most evident rational judgment and defends against objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity is considered the most evident rational judgment in kalam and how the author defends it against objections.

Core Idea
Necessity is most evident because it is closest to existence.

The passage begins by asserting that the three rational judgments are all necessary but differ in clarity. Necessity stands out as the most evident. However, this claim raises objections: does it imply that all concepts are intuitive? And is it confusing kalam with logic? The commentator addresses these to clarify the subject matter of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Focus on the claim that necessity is most evident and the reason given.
The passage has a dialectical structure: claim, objection, response.
2
Identify the objection
Notice that some theorists object that if necessity is intuitive, then many other concepts would also be intuitive, and that what is discussed are merely logical modalities.
Think about why this would be a problem for the author's position.
3
Understand the response
The commentator argues that the objection is based on a confusion between kalam and logic. The author will later clarify that the subject of kalam is different from the modal propositions discussed in logic.
This shows the importance of distinguishing between disciplines.
4
Reflect on the final note
The word 'سيبصرح' indicates a future clarification. This prepares the student to expect a distinction between the two fields.
Consolidate: necessity is most evident, and the objection is deflected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ( لكن استدراك من قوله تصوراتها ضرورية يعني ) أنها متشاركة في كونها ضرورية، ومع ذلك متفاوتة ( أظهرها الوجوب ) إذ لا استحالة في كون بعض الضروريات أجلى من بعض، وعلى هذا فالتنبيه على معنى الإمكان والامتناع بالوجوب أولى من العكس، وإنما كان الوجوب أظهر ( لأنه أقرب إلى الوجود) حررنا لك اندفع ما أورده الناظرون من أن اللازم منه أن يكون تصورها بوجه بديهياً، ولو استلزم التصديق المذكور لتصورها بالكنه لاستلزم أن يكون تصور الإنسان والحجر والحيوان والكاتب أيضاً بديهياً، وأن ما ذكره إنما هي جهات القضايا التي يبحث عنها في المنطق. وسيصرح المصنف بأن المبحوث عنها في الكلام غير ما هو جهات القضايا . قوله : ( ما لا يمكن عدمه بالإمكان العام فيكون معناه ما يسلب عنه سلب ضرورة الوجود فلا يشمل الممتنع على ما وهم، وكذا فيما بعده قوله (حقيقية أراد به ما يقابل اللفظية أي لا تكون هذه التعريفات لتحصيل ما ليس بحاصل لاستلزامها امتناع التحصيل ولا تعريفات تنبيهية يقصد بها إزالة الخفاء عما هو حاصل لأنه يستلزم إزالة خفاء الشيء بنفسه، بل تعريفات لفظية قصد بها التصديق بوضع هذه الألفاظ للمعاني المعلومة فلا يضر كونها دورية. قوله : ( وأنه دور (ظاهر قد يناقش بأن الإمكان المأخوذ في تعريفه أحد الأمرين هو الإمكان الخاص، والواقع في تعريفهما هو الإمكان العام فلا دور في صورة أخذ الإمكان، واندفاعهما يظهر مما قررنا في الجهات، نعم يمكن أن يناقش بأن الممكن إذا عرف بما لا يجب وجوده ولا عدمه مثلاً، وعرف الواجب بما يمتنع عدمه والممتنع بما يجب عدمه لم يلزم دور في تعريف الإمكان، بل اللازم هو التعريف بالمجهول كما لا يخفى وجواب هذا أيضاً ظاهر إذ المدعى لزوم الدور مطلقاً، ولقد لزم وإن لم يكن بين المعرف والمعرف الذي هو الممكن فتأمل. قوله : ( لأنه أقرب إلى الوجود قد يعارض بأن الضد أقرب خطوراً بالبال الضد، كما صرح به في بحث الوجود فينبغي أن يكون الامتناع أظهرها، فتأمل.
His statement: (But it is an addition from his statement: 'their concepts are necessary' means) that they share in being necessary, yet they differ (the most evident of them is necessity) because there is no impossibility in some necessary things being clearer than others. Accordingly, indicating the meaning of possibility and impossibility by necessity is better than the reverse. Necessity is most evident only (because it is closest to existence). We have explained to you that the objection raised by the theorists is repelled: that the necessary consequence would be that their concept is intuitive in one respect, and if that affirmation required their concept by essence, it would require that the concept of human, stone, animal, and writer also be intuitive, and that what he mentioned are only modalities of propositions (jihāt al-qadāyā) which are discussed in logic. The author will explicitly state that what is discussed in kalām is other than the modalities of propositions. His statement: (What cannot be non-existent with general possibility) means that it is that from which the negation of the necessity of existence is negated, so it does not include the impossible as was mistakenly thought. Similarly after that. His statement: (real) he means by it what is opposite to verbal, that these definitions are not for acquiring what is not acquired because they entail impossibility of acquisition, nor are they indicative definitions intended to remove obscurity from what is acquired because it would entail removing obscurity of the thing by itself; rather they are verbal definitions intended to affirm the placement of these words for the known meanings, so their circularity does not harm. His statement: (And it is a clear circle) may be objected to by saying that the possibility taken in defining one of the two is specific possibility, while that which occurs in their definitions is general possibility, so there is no circle in the case of taking possibility. The rebuttal appears from what we established concerning modalities. Yes, it can be objected that if the possible is defined as that whose existence and non-existence are not necessary, and the necessary is defined as that whose non-existence is impossible, and the impossible as that whose non-existence is necessary, there is no circle in the definition of possibility; rather what is necessary is definition by the unknown, as is obvious. The answer to this is also apparent because the claim is the occurrence of a circle absolutely, and it does occur even if not between the definer and the defined which is the possible. So reflect. His statement: (because it is closest to existence) may be objected to by saying that the opposite is more readily thought, as stated in the discussion of existence, so impossibility should be the most evident. So reflect.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) that explains an insertion in the original text and addresses several objections. It begins by clarifying that the three concepts are all necessary (ḍarūriyya) but differ in clarity: necessity (al-wujūb) is the most evident because it is closest to existence. The author then explains that pointing to possibility and impossibility by way of necessity is more appropriate. Next, the commentator responds to an objection: some argue that if these concepts are intuitive, then concepts like 'human' and 'stone' would also be intuitive, and that what is being discussed are merely the modalities of propositions (jihāt al-qadāyā) studied in logic. The commentator counters by stating that the author will later clarify that the subject of kalām is different from those modalities. The commentary then delves into the nature of the definition, noting that the intended 'real' definition is opposite to 'verbal,' and that the circular definitions here are only verbal, serving to affirm the assignment of words to meanings, hence harmless. The passage then examines a potential counter-argument: perhaps the possibility used in one definition is specific (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) while in others it is general (al-imkān al-ʿāmm), breaking the circle. The commentator dismisses this based on earlier discussion of modalities. Another objection states that defining the possible as 'that whose existence and non-existence are not necessary' does not involve a circle because it uses negation of necessity, but the commentator insists the circle is absolute. Finally, the passage addresses a challenge to the claim that necessity is most evident: opponents argue that the opposite (impossibility) is more readily imagined, citing discussions on existence. The commentator leaves this with 'ta'ammal' (reflect), implying it remains unsettled.

Key Terms
أظهر
الوجوب
الامتناع
جهات القضايا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all rational judgments are equally clear and that kalam's study of modalities is the same as that in logic.
Correction: The passage shows that there is a hierarchy (necessity is most evident) and that kalam's focus is distinct from logical modalities.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator consider necessity (al-wujūb) to be the most evident of the three?
A) Because it is the most commonly used term
B) Because it is closest to existence
C) Because it is mentioned first in the definition
D) Because it is the only one that is intuitive

Key Takeaway
Necessity is the clearest rational judgment, and kalam's discussion of modalities differs from logic's.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you experience the immediacy of necessity—that you are utterly dependent on Allah? Or do you live as if your existence is merely possible, with no pressing sense of dependence? What would change in your daily life if you truly felt the weight of necessity?
?
The commentator says necessity is most evident because it is closest to existence. In your heart, what is the one thing you are most certain of? Is your certainty in Allah's existence as immediate as the awareness that you exist? If not, what is blocking that clarity, and what step will you take today to remove that barrier?
?
This lesson warns against confusing the study of kalam with logic. Have you ever treated your faith as a set of intellectual propositions to be analyzed, rather than as a necessary truth that demands your whole being? What would it look like to let the concept of necessity transform your worship, not just your understanding?

Verbal Definitions and Circularity in Kalam Terms
The commentator explains that the definitions are verbal, not real, and addresses circularity and a final objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between real and verbal definitions and how circular definitions can be acceptable in kalam.

Core Idea
Circular definitions in kalam are verbal, not real, so they do not harm.

After establishing which judgment is most evident, the commentator turns to the definitions of these judgments. He clarifies that the definitions given in the text are not intended to reveal new concepts but to assign words to known meanings. This leads to an important distinction between real and verbal definitions and addresses concerns about circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the classification of definitions as real or verbal.
Notice how the commentator uses the term 'حقيقية' and 'لفظية'.
2
Understand the definition types
Identify what distinguishes a real definition from a verbal one. The passage explains that real definitions seek to acquire new knowledge, while verbal definitions merely assign words to known meanings.
This is a key distinction in Islamic logic.
3
Analyze the circularity objection
The objector claims the definitions are circular. The commentator explains that because these are verbal definitions, circularity is not harmful.
Then a more specific objection arises about the different types of possibility.
4
Examine the final challenge
The passage ends with a challenge: some argue that impossibility is more readily thought, so it should be most evident. The commentator leaves this with 'ta'ammal' (reflect).
Think about why this objection is not fully resolved.

Why Wujub is the Clearest of the Three Concepts
Al-Jurjani explains why necessity is the most evident concept among the three.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity (wujub) is considered the clearest of the three rational judgments, based on the number of conceptual mediations to existence.

Core Idea
Wujub is the clearest because it relates to existence directly, without mediation.

This passage delves into a subtle objection: if impossibility is more striking to the mind, why is necessity considered clearer? Al-Jurjani resolves this by analyzing the conceptual mediations involved. You'll see that clarity in rational concepts depends not on strength but on directness of relation to existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the objection is raised early, and the response unfolds in stages.
2
Identify the objection
The passage begins with an objection: if impossibility is more striking to the mind, shouldn't it be clearer? Understand why someone might think this.
The commentator invites reflection ('tأمل') before proceeding.
3
Follow the initial answer
The matn states that wujub is clearest because it affirms existence, while imtina' opposes it and imkan does not approach it without necessity.
This initial answer lays the groundwork for a deeper analysis.
4
Understand the deeper analysis
The commentary distinguishes between nearness in actuality and nearness in intellection, then offers the preferred explanation based on mediations.
Count the mediations: wujub (0), imtina' (1), imkan (2).
5
Appreciate the resolution
Conclude that wujub is the clearest because its concept has no intermediary to existence, making it more evident to the intellect.
This resolves the objection and clarifies the hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لأنه أقرب إلى الوجود قد يعارض بأن الضد أقرب خطوراً بالبال الضد، كما مع صرح به في بحث الوجود فينبغي أن يكون الامتناع أظهرها، فتأمل.

الذي هو أظهر المفهومات وأجلاها، وذلك لأنه يؤكد الوجود، وأما الامتناع فهو مناف للوجود ،والإمكان ما لم يصل إلى حد الوجوب لم يقرب إلى الوجود، وما هو أقرب إلى أجلى التصورات كان أظهر من غيره ) واعلم أن الوجوب يقال على الواجب باعتبار ما له من الخواص...

قوله : (فهو مناف للوجود وليس نقيضاً له حتى يكون تعقله بالقياس إليه، فيكون جلاؤه مستلزماً لجلائه كالعدم، فإنه لكونه نقيض الوجود أجلى من سائر المفهومات عند العقل. قوله: (وما هو أقرب إلخ لا يخفى أن ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره إنما يدل على قرب الوجود في التحقق بالقياس إلى الامتناع والإمكان دون القرب في التعقل، فهذا مبني على أن ما هو أكثر تحققاً في الخارج أكثر تحققاً في الذهن، بناء على أن العلوم مأخوذة من الحسيات، فإن تم تم وإلا فلا. والأظهر أن يقال : الوجوب تأكد الوجود ففي مفهومه النسبة إلى الوجود بلا واسطة، فيكون أجلى بخلاف الامتناع فإن مفهومه تأكد العدم، ففيه النسبة إلى الوجود بواسطة أن العدم سلب الوجود وكذا الإمكان، فإن مفهومه سلب ضرورة الوجود والعدم ففيه النسبة بواسطتين.
His saying: (because it is closer to existence). It may be objected that the opposite is more striking to the mind, as was stated in the discussion of existence, so impossibility should be the clearest of them. So reflect.

It is the clearest and most evident of concepts, and that is because it affirms existence. As for impossibility, it is contrary to existence, and contingency, as long as it does not reach the level of necessity, does not approach existence. And what is closer to the most evident of concepts is more manifest than others. And know that necessity is said of the necessary in consideration of its properties...

His saying: (it is contrary to existence and not a contradiction of it such that its intellection is by reference to it, so that its clarity would entail its clarity like non-existence, for because it is the contradictory of existence, it is clearer than other concepts to the intellect. His saying: (and what is closer, etc.) It is not hidden that what the commentator (may God sanctify his spirit) mentioned only indicates the nearness of existence in actuality relative to impossibility and contingency, not nearness in intellection. This is based on the principle that what is more actualized in the external is more actualized in the mind, based on the idea that knowledge is derived from sense-perceptions. If that holds, then it holds; otherwise not. The more evident thing to say is: necessity is the affirmation of existence, so in its concept there is a relation to existence without mediation, thus it is clearer. Contrary to impossibility, for its concept is the affirmation of non-existence, so in it there is a relation to existence through the mediation that non-existence is the negation of existence. Likewise contingency, for its concept is the negation of the necessity of existence and non-existence, so in it there is a relation through two mediations.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage explains why wujub (necessity) is the clearest of the three concepts. It begins with an objection: the opposite (imtina') is more striking to the mind, as mentioned in the discussion of existence, so imtina' should be clearest. The author says 'reflect', implying the answer is forthcoming. Then the matn states that wujub is the clearest because it asserts existence, while imtina' opposes existence and imkan does not reach existence without necessity. The commentator then delves deeper: imtina' is not a contradiction (naqid) of existence but its contrary, so its intellection does not require the clarity of existence. Another point: the commentator's previous remark only proves wujub's nearness in actuality, not in intellection, unless we accept that what is more real in externals is more real in the mind (a view based on sense-perception). The preferred explanation is that wujub is the affirmation of existence, so its concept relates to existence directly, making it clearer. Imtina' relates to existence through the mediation that non-existence is a negation of existence, and imkan relates through two mediations (negation of both necessities). Thus wujub is clearest because it has the fewest conceptual intermediaries.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think impossibility is clearer than necessity because it is a more striking concept.
Correction: Clarity in rational concepts depends on the number of conceptual mediations to existence, not on subjective strikingness. Wujub has zero mediations, making it the clearest.

How can you shift your mental focus to what is directly affirmed?
?
The passage teaches that clarity is about mediation, not intensity. Think of a belief you hold intensely (e.g., about the afterlife) but which might actually be mediated and less clear. Are you building your life on a concept that is less clear than you think? What would it take to ground it in something closer to direct necessity?

Why Wujub is the Clearest Rational Judgment
Analysis of why wujub is the clearest concept among the three rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of wujub (necessity) is clearer than imkan (possibility) and imtina' (impossibility) based on its direct relation to existence.

Core Idea
Wujub is clearest because it relates to existence without any mental mediation.

In this passage, the commentator analyzes why wujub (necessity) stands out as the clearest among the three rational judgments. He argues that existence itself is the most manifest concept, and wujub directly emphasizes existence, while imtina' and imkan relate to existence only through negation or mediation. Let's explore the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial claim
Read the Arabic sentence that states wujub is the most manifest because it emphasizes existence. Compare it with imtina' and imkan.
Notice the key phrase: 'يؤكد الوجود' (emphasizes existence).
2
Examine the objection
The objection points out that the reasoning based on external existence does not automatically imply cognitive clarity. Reflect on why sense-perception may not be enough.
This objection is a subtle philosophical point.
3
Learn the preferred explanation
The preferred view explains clarity in terms of directness of relation: wujub relates to existence without mediation, imtina' with one mediation (through adam), and imkan with two mediations.
Count the mediations for each concept.
4
Apply the concept
Try to rephrase the clarity hierarchy in your own words, using the idea of 'distance' from existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذي هو أظهر المفهومات وأجلاها، وذلك لأنه يؤكد الوجود، وأما الامتناع فهو مناف للوجود، والإمكان ما لم يصل إلى حد الوجوب لم يقرب إلى الوجود، وما هو أقرب إلى أجلى التصورات كان أظهر من غيره).
قوله : (فهو مناف للوجود وليس نقيضاً له حتى يكون تعقله بالقياس إليه، فيكون جلاؤه مستلزماً لجلائه كالعدم، فإنه لكونه نقيض الوجود أجلى من سائر المفهومات عند العقل.
قوله: (وما هو أقرب إلخ لا يخفى أن ما ذكره الشارح قدس سره إنما يدل على قرب الوجود في التحقق بالقياس إلى الامتناع والإمكان دون القرب في التعقل، فهذا مبني على أن ما هو أكثر تحققاً في الخارج أكثر تحققاً في الذهن، بناء على أن العلوم مأخوذة من الحسيات، فإن تم تم وإلا فلا.
والأظهر أن يقال : الوجوب تأكد الوجود ففي مفهومه النسبة إلى الوجود بلا واسطة، فيكون أجلى بخلاف الامتناع فإن مفهومه تأكد العدم، ففيه النسبة إلى الوجود بواسطة أن العدم سلب الوجود وكذا الإمكان، فإن مفهومه سلب ضرورة الوجود والعدم ففيه النسبة بواسطتين.
Which is the most manifest and clearest of concepts, and that is because it emphasizes existence. As for impossibility, it is contrary to existence, and possibility, as long as it does not reach the level of necessity, does not approach existence. And what is closer to the clearest perceptions is more manifest than other things.).
He said: (It is contrary to existence, not its opposite, such that its understanding is by reference to it, so its clarity entails its clarity like non-existence, for because it is the opposite of existence, it is clearer than other concepts to the intellect.
He said: (And what is closer etc.) It is not hidden that what the commentator (may his soul be sanctified) mentioned only indicates the nearness of existence in actuality relative to impossibility and possibility, not nearness in intellection. This is based on the idea that what is more realized externally is more realized in the mind, built on the premise that knowledges are taken from sense-perceptions. If it holds, it holds; otherwise not.
And the more apparent is to say: Necessity is the strengthening of existence, so in its concept there is a relation to existence without mediation, thus it is clearer. In contrast, impossibility: its concept is the strengthening of non-existence, so in it there is a relation to existence mediated by the fact that non-existence is the negation of existence. Likewise possibility: its concept is the negation of the necessity of existence and the necessity of non-existence, so in it there is a relation through two mediations.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a conceptual analysis. It begins by stating that existence (al-wujud) is the clearest and most manifest concept because it emphasizes existence, while impossibility (imtina') is opposed to existence, and possibility (imkan) does not approach existence unless it reaches the level of necessity. The commentator then examines two aspects: first, the statement that impossibility and possibility are less clear; second, the objection that the reasoning based on external existence may not imply cognitive clarity. The preferred view is that necessity (wujub) is the strengthening of existence, so in its concept there is a direct relation to existence without mediation, making it clearer. Impossibility involves strengthening of non-existence, thus its relation to existence is mediated by the fact that non-existence is the negation of existence. Possibility involves negation of both necessity of existence and necessity of non-existence, so it has two mediations. Thus wujub is the clearest.

الإمكان (imkan)
الامتناع (imtina')
الوجود (wujud)
العدم (adam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that imtina' is equally clear because it is the opposite of existence, just as 'adam is the opposite of wujud.
Correction: The passage shows that imtina' is not a simple opposite but involves strengthening of non-existence, requiring mediation through the concept of 'adam, which is the negation of existence. Thus it is less clear than wujub.

Key Takeaway
Wujub is the clearest rational judgment because it has a direct, unmediated relation to existence, while imtina' and imkan require one or two mental mediations respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Name one and describe a concrete step you can take to remove it.
?
When you consider your own death (an inevitable wujub), is your awareness of it as immediate and clear as the concept of wujub, or do you often live as if it is only a possibility (imkan)? What would change in your daily actions if you truly internalized the necessity of your meeting with Allah?
?
The passage argues that imtina' is actually clearer than imkan. Think of a sin you consider 'possible' to commit again. Are you letting its possibility cloud your resolve? How would your behavior shift if you treated that sin as 'impossible' (imtina') for you, by strengthening your reliance on Allah?

The Three Meanings of Necessity (Wujub) and Their Logical Relationship
Author defines three meanings of wujub and explains their conceptual distinctness and real entailment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct meanings of wujub (necessity) in kalam, how they differ in concept yet entail each other in reality, and how this distinction impacts later rulings about existence and essence.

Core Idea
The three meanings of necessity are distinct concepts but inseparable realities.

Sanusi introduces the foundational classification of wujub into three conceptually distinct properties that nonetheless always coexist. Understanding their exact definitions and interrelations is essential for grasping subsequent discussions on whether these attributes are existential or non-existential, and whether they are identical to God's essence or additional to it. This passage lays the groundwork for a precise theological vocabulary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full English translation to get the overall flow. Note the author's compact style.
The passage is from the 'Third Observation, First Purpose' of al-Mawaqif.
2
Identify the three meanings of wujub
The passage presents three properties (khususiyyat) that are mutually entailing but different in concept. Identify each: the first is a negative relation, the second is a relation of essence and existence, the third is the essence itself.
The author explains their difference in the first half and then their entailment in the second.
3
Analyze the difference between the three meanings
Focus on how the author explains the conceptual distinction: the third property is 'the essence itself', the second is 'a relation of essence and existence', and the first is 'a negative relation consequent upon the positive relation'.
4
Understand the entailment argument
The author argues that if the essence is sufficient for existence, then no external cause is needed (first meaning), and whenever either the first or second meaning is found, the third (distinction) is also found. Work through the mutual entailment logic.
This shows why the three always coexist despite being conceptually distinct.
5
Connect to the final ruling
The author concludes by noting which meanings are existential vs. non-existential and which are identical to the essence. This prepares for later discussions on the nature of divine attributes.
The first meaning is non-existential (a negation), while the last two are existential (positive concepts). The third is the essence itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الأول : تصورات في الوجوب والإمكان والامتناع ( وهي ) أي هذه الخواص (أمورٌ متلازمة لكنها متغايرة في المفهوم) أما تغايرها فلان الخاصة الثالثة عين الذات فإنه تعالى بذاته متميز عن جميع ما عداه، والثانية نسبة الذات والوجود والأولى نسبة سلبية مترتبة على النسبة الثبوتية، وأما بين تلازمها فلانه كان ذاته كافيا في اقتضاء وجوده لم يحتج في وجوده إلى غيره، وبالعكس. ومتى وجد أحد هذين الأمرين وجد ما به يتميز الذات عن الغير وبالعكس. ( فافهم هذا الذي ذكرناه من معاني الوجوب، وليكن هذا على ذكر منك ) فإنه ينفعك ( فيما يرد عليك من أحكامه ) أي أحكام الوجوب من كونه وجودياً أو عدمياً وكونه عين الذات أو زائدا عليها فالمعنى الأول عدمي والأخيران وجوديان بمعنى أنه لا سلب في مفهومهما والثالث عين الذات بخلاف الأولين
The Third Observation - First Purpose: Conceptualizations concerning Necessity, Possibility, and Impossibility. (And they) i.e. these properties (are mutually entailing matters but they are different in concept). As for their difference, it is because the third property is the essence itself, for He, exalted, is distinguished by His essence from all others, and the second is a relation of essence and existence, and the first is a negative relation consequent upon the positive relation. As for their entailment, it is because if His essence was sufficient for necessitating His existence, He did not need another in His existence, and vice versa. And whenever one of these two matters is found, what distinguishes the essence from others is found, and vice versa. (So understand what we have mentioned of the meanings of necessity, and let this be on your mind) for it will benefit you (concerning what comes to you of its rulings) i.e. the rulings of necessity: whether it is existential or non-existential, and whether it is the essence itself or additional to it. So the first meaning is non-existential, and the last two are existential, meaning that there is no negation in their concept, and the third is the essence itself unlike the first two.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the three meanings of wujub (necessity) as applied to God. It begins by stating that the three properties (khususiyyat) are mutually entailing but conceptually distinct. The author then explains the difference: the third property is the essence itself (God is distinct by His essence), the second is the relation of essence to existence (His existence is identical to His essence), and the first is a negative relation (not needing a cause). The entailment is argued: if the essence is sufficient for existence, no cause is needed, and this absence of need is the first meaning. The author then emphasizes understanding these meanings to handle later rulings about whether each meaning is existential or non-existential, and whether it is the essence or additional. The first meaning is non-existential (a negation), while the last two are existential (positive concepts), with the third being the essence itself.

Key Terms
وجوب
خاصية
متلازمة
متغايرة في المفهوم
عدمي
وجودي
عين الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because the three properties always coexist, they are identical and interchangeable in meaning.
Correction: The passage explicitly states they are 'different in concept' (mutaghāyira fī al-mafhūm). Each property has a distinct definition: one is a negation, one is a relation, one is the essence itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the three meanings of wujub is described as 'non-existential' (عدمي)?
A) The first meaning (the negative relation)
B) The second meaning (relation of essence and existence)
C) The third meaning (the essence itself)
D) None of them

Key Takeaway
The three meanings of wujub are conceptually distinct but inseparable in reality, with the first being non-existential and the last two existential, and the third identical to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first meaning of necessity is that God's existence has no need of a cause. Does your heart truly find rest in this, or do you sometimes secretly believe that God needs your worship to be complete? How would your worship change if you fully believed that God is absolutely independent and you are the one in need?
?
The author emphasizes that the third property is identical to God's essence—His being distinct from all else is not an added attribute. Do you think of God's greatness as something He 'has' rather than something He 'is'? What would it mean to relate to God not as a collection of attributes but as a single, simple reality?
?
The lesson ends with a direct command: 'So understand this.' Are you willing to wrestle with these difficult concepts until they become part of your understanding of God, or will you let them remain as abstract theological trivia? What might be the spiritual cost of not understanding them?

The Three Properties of the Possible and the Impossible
Author enumerates the three properties of the possible and impossible by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three properties of the possible (al-mumkin) and the impossible (al-mumtaniʿ) and how they mirror the properties of the necessary.

Core Idea
Possibility and impossibility each have three parallel properties, just like necessity.

In the previous lesson, we saw that the necessary (wājib) has three properties. Now, the author extends the same threefold analysis to the possible (mumkin) and the impossible (mumtaniʿ). By seeing how each category is defined through its essential properties, we deepen our understanding of the rational judgments that form the foundation of kalām. This passage completes the picture by symmetry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice that the author uses the same formula for both categories: 'concerning the possible... the first..., the second..., the third...' and similarly for the impossible.
The last sentence explains why the impossible was not explicitly mentioned earlier.
2
Identify the three properties for each
As you read, list the three properties for the possible and three for the impossible. For the possible, the first is need for a cause, the second is neutrality, the third is distinction. For the impossible, the first is independence from a cause for non-existence, the second is self-necessitating non-existence, the third is distinction.
The properties are parallel but opposite in some respects.
3
Reflect on the analogy and mutual entailment
The author says these three are 'different but mutually entailing' just like the necessary. Think about why: if something is possible, it must have all three properties; if it has one, it has the others. Also note that the author relied on analogy for the impossible, so we can derive its properties from the possible by contrast.
This shows the systematic nature of kalām classifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على الممكن باعتبار ما له من الخواص : فالأولى احتياجه في وجوده إلى غيره، والثانية عدم اقتضاء ذاته وجوده أو عدمه، والثالثة ما به يمتاز ذات الممكن عن الغير وهذه الثلاث أيضاً متغايرة متلازمة على ما مر في الواجب . على الممتنع باعتبار ما له من الخواص : فالأولى استغناؤه في العدم عن الغير، والثانية اقتضاء ذاته عدمه والثالثة ما به يمتاز ذات الممتنع عن غيره، وإنما لم يذكره اكتفاء .
Concerning the possible (al-mumkin) with respect to its properties: the first is its need in its existence from another, the second is its essence not necessitating existence or non-existence, and the third is that by which the essence of the possible is distinguished from others. And these three are also different but mutually entailing, as was mentioned concerning the necessary. Concerning the impossible (al-mumtaniʿ) with respect to its properties: the first is its independence from another in non-existence, the second is its essence necessitating its non-existence, and the third is that by which the essence of the impossible is distinguished from others. And he did not mention it [the impossible] because he relied [on analogy].
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage completes the analysis by applying the same threefold scheme to possibility (imkan) and impossibility (imtinaʿ). For the possible: (1) it needs a cause for its existence, (2) its essence does not necessitate either existence or non-existence (it is neutral), and (3) it is distinguished from others by its essence (essence-based individuation). These three are mutually entailing, just as with necessity. For the impossible: (1) its non-existence does not depend on a cause (it is intrinsically nonexistent), (2) its essence necessitates its non-existence, and (3) it is distinguished from others by its essence. The author notes that the impossible was not explicitly mentioned earlier, relying on the analogy.

إمكان
امتناع
ممكن
ممتنع
خاصية
متلازمة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think possibility and impossibility have only a single property (e.g., possibility just means 'needs a cause').
Correction: The passage shows that each has three distinct yet interconnected properties, mirroring the analysis of necessity.

Checkpoint
What is the second property of the possible (al-mumkin) according to the passage?
A) It needs a cause for its existence
B) Its essence does not necessitate existence or non-existence
C) It is distinguished from others by its essence
D) Its essence necessitates its non-existence

Key Takeaway
Both possibility and impossibility are defined by three parallel properties: a causal relation, an essential disposition, and an individuating essence, all mutually entailing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that the possible needs a cause for its existence. Do you truly live with the constant awareness that you need Allah for every moment of your existence, or do you feel self-sufficient? What would change if you internalized that your very being is dependent on Him every second?
?
The impossible is defined by its essence necessitating non-existence. Is there something in your life that you consider impossible for you to achieve? According to this lesson, its impossibility is in its essence, not in Allah's power. How does this encourage you to trust in Allah's ability to change your situation?
?
The third property says each essence is distinct from others. In a world that pressures you to imitate others, in what specific area are you trying to be distinguished by your own essence (your unique God-given nature) rather than by external labels? How can you honor that distinctiveness today?

Impossibility is a Mental Concept
Proof that impossibility is a mental concept

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why impossibility (imtina') cannot be an external existent and is purely a mental concept.

Core Idea
Impossibility is a quality of the impossible, so it cannot itself exist externally.

The author begins by asserting that the three rational judgments are mental considerations. He then provides a concise argument specifically for impossibility: since it describes something that cannot exist externally, the description itself does not have external existence. This lesson examines that simple but profound argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the direct claim that impossibility is a quality of what is impossible in the external world.
2
Understand the logic
Impossibility is a 'description' of something that cannot exist. Since the described thing has no external existence, its description cannot have external existence either.
This is a straightforward metaphysical principle: qualities of non-entities are not real.
3
Reflect on the implication
If impossibility were an external existent, it would need to inhere in something that is impossible, but that thing is non-existent. Thus impossibility must be merely mental.

Source Text

المقصد الثاني : أمور اعتبارية لا وجود لها في الخارج ( إن هذه أمور اعتبارية لا وجود لها في الخارج أما الامتناع فلأنه صفة لما يستحيل وجوده في الخارج، فلا يتصور لصفته وجود خارجي. The Second Purpose: Matters that are mental considerations having no existence externally. (Indeed these are mental considerations having no existence externally. As for impossibility, it is because it is a quality of that whose existence is impossible externally, so its quality cannot be conceived to have external existence. As for necessity, it is for two reasons: first, if necessity existed externally, it would be either possible or necessary, because external existents are confined to these two. If it were possible, and the necessary only becomes necessary by it, since if it were not subsisting in it it would not be necessary, then the necessary would need another, which is absurd. And if it were necessary, then that necessary would also become necessary by it, leading to an infinite regress. Both are false. As for possibility, its explanation is that if possibility existed externally, it would be either necessary or possible. If it were necessary, then the possible would be necessary, and if it were possible, it would need another, both absurd. So it is established that these matters are mental considerations having no existence externally.

Commentary
This passage presents the argument that the three categories (wujub, imkan, imtinaʿ) are iʿtibārī (mental/relational) and not real external existents. The author first notes that impossibility (imtinaʿ) is a quality of something impossible, so its quality cannot be external. Necessity Cannot Be External
Two proofs that necessity is a mental concept

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments proving that necessity (wujub) cannot exist as an external entity.

Core Idea
If necessity were external, it would create an absurd dependence or infinite regress.

The author presents two powerful arguments against the external existence of necessity (wujub). If wujub existed externally, it would have to be either possible or necessary. Each possibility leads to an absurdity: either God would depend on it, or an infinite regress would follow. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the wujub argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage for wujub and its translation. Notice the dilemma: external wujub must be either possible or necessary.
2
Analyze the first branch
If wujub were possible (mumkin), then the Necessary Being (God) would depend on it to be necessary. This makes God dependent on something else, which is absurd.
This argument relies on the principle that the Necessary Being cannot depend on anything.
3
Analyze the second branch
If wujub were necessary (wajib), then that necessary entity would also need wujub to be necessary, leading to an infinite regress of necessities.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is considered invalid in kalam.
4
Conclude
Both possibilities are impossible, so wujub cannot be external. It must be a mental consideration.

Possibility Is a Mental Concept
Proof that possibility is a mental concept

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument proving that possibility (imkan) is a mental consideration and not an external existent.

Core Idea
If possibility were external, it would either make the possible necessary or require another cause.

The author applies a similar dilemma to possibility (imkan). If imkan were an external entity, it would have to be either necessary or possible. Both alternatives lead to contradictions. This lesson unpacks that argument, completing the proof that all three rational judgments are mental considerations.

Guided Path
1
Read the imkan argument
Read the Arabic passage for imkan and its translation. Notice the parallel structure to the wujub argument.
2
First branch: imkan as necessary
If imkan were necessary, then the possible entity (which has imkan) would itself be necessary, contradicting its nature.
This is a direct contradiction: the possible cannot be necessary.
3
Second branch: imkan as possible
If imkan were possible, it would need an external cause to exist, leading to dependence on another – similar to the wujub argument.
4
Conclusion
Both branches are absurd, so imkan cannot be external. It is a mental consideration.
This final passage also reaffirms the overall conclusion for all three.

Two Arguments that Necessity is Entitative
Two arguments proving necessity is entitative via a dilemma branching from hypothetical external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs that necessity (al-wujūb) cannot exist externally and must be an entitative matter, as argued through the impossibility of both possibilities (possible and necessary).

Core Idea
Necessity cannot exist externally without leading to absurdity or infinite regress.

In this passage, Mir Sayyid Sharif presents a powerful argument that necessity is not a real attribute existing outside the mind. He assumes necessity exists externally and then shows that it leads to an impossible dilemma: either it would be possible (contradicting the nature of necessity) or it would be necessary (leading to an infinite regress). By eliminating both options, he forces the conclusion that necessity is merely a conceptual consideration. Let's walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Assumption
The argument begins with the assumption that necessity (al-wujūb) exists in the external world as a real entity. This is a hypothetical premise to be disproven.
2
Examine the First Branch
If necessity were possible (mumkin), then the Necessary Being (God) would owe His necessity to something possible, which would make the Necessary Being itself possible—a contradiction.
This relies on the principle that the cause of a thing's necessity must itself be necessary in some respect.
3
Examine the Second Branch
If necessity were necessary, then it would require another necessity to make it necessary, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible in the chain of existence.
Infinite regress is rejected in kalam arguments about primary principles.
4
Draw the Conclusion
Since both possibilities lead to absurdities, the original assumption that necessity exists externally must be false. Therefore, necessity is an entitative (iʿtibārī) concept, not a real existent.
This does not mean necessity is unreal, but that it is a mental consideration that has no external instantiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجوب فلوجهين: الأول أنه لو وجد الوجوب في الخارج لكان إما ممكناً أو واجباً لانحصار الموجودات الخارجية فيهما. فإن كان ممكناً والواجب إنما يجب به إذ لولا قيام الوجوب به لم يكن واجباً أصلاً، فبالأولى أن يكون الواجب ممكناً، هذا خلف. وإن كان واجباً كان له وجوب آخر وتسلسل.
As for necessity, it has two aspects: First, if necessity existed in the external world, it would be either possible or necessary, because external existents are limited to these two. If it were possible, and the necessary is necessary only by it, since without its subsistence by it it would not be necessary at all, then the necessary would have to be possible, which is absurd. And if it were necessary, it would have another necessity and an infinite regress.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs that necessity (al-wujūb) is an entitative matter (amr iʿtibārī) with no external existence. The argument is structured as a conditional proof: assume necessity exists externally. Since all external existents are either possible (mumkin) or necessary (wājib), necessity must be one of the two. If it is possible, then the Necessary (God) is necessary by something possible, which would imply that the Necessary is only possible (since the ground of its necessity is itself possible), a contradiction. If it is necessary, then it would require another necessity ad infinitum (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, the assumption that necessity exists externally leads to absurdity, so it must be entitative. The commentary would explain that this argument forces the opponent to accept that the necessity is entitative, or the infinite regress is avoided by other means. The next lesson will address the answer to the infinite regress objection.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الممكن
الواجب
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if necessity is entitative, then God is necessary by something external to Himself.
Correction: The argument shows that the alternative (external existence) leads to worse problems, so the entitative interpretation is the only coherent one.

Checkpoint
What happens if we assume necessity (الوجوب) exists externally and is necessary?
A) It leads to a contradiction with God's self-sufficiency.
B) It leads to an infinite regress of necessities.
C) It implies God is possible.
D) It proves necessity exists externally.

Key Takeaway
Necessity cannot be a real external attribute; it must be a mental consideration (amr iʿtibārī) because assuming external existence leads to contradiction or infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God’s attributes — like His necessity or power — as if they were independent entities you could mentally isolate from Him, forgetting they are identical with His essence? How can you guard your heart from breaking His oneness into parts?
?
If necessity is only a mental consideration, everything besides God is utterly contingent. Are there areas of your life — your security, identity, or relationships — where you secretly rely on something other than God as if it were necessary for you? What would it take to surrender that today?
?
The argument shows that God’s necessity cannot come from outside. Is there any ‘external’ thing you have been attributing ultimate significance to — like people’s approval, personal achievements, or material wealth — that you must now recognize as not truly necessary? Will you trust God alone to be your sufficiency?
