Two Solutions to the Regress of Necessity
The author answers the tasalsul objection by offering two solutions: identity or entitativity of further ranks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two solutions to the infinite regress objection regarding the necessity of divine attributes: identity of necessity and entitativity of further ranks.

Core Idea
Necessity of necessity may be identical or entitative, halting the regress.

The author now addresses the tasalsul (infinite regress) objection that arose from the previous discussion. He argues that even if necessity itself is necessary, it does not lead to an endless chain. Two distinct solutions are proposed: the necessity of necessity might be identical to it (like existence of existence), or the higher ranks of necessity could be merely mental considerations (iʿtibārī). Let's examine these solutions carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall argument of the author.
The passage is short but dense; take note of the two solutions.
2
Identify the two solutions
Find the two distinct approaches: one based on identity, the other on entitativity (iʿtibārī).
The text uses 'أيضا' to introduce the second solution.
3
Understand the analogy
The first solution is compared to the claim that 'existence of existence is identical to existence'. Reflect on how this prevents regress.
This analogy suggests that the necessity of necessity is not a separate entity.
4
Grasp the entitativity principle
The second solution relies on the principle that one individual of a nature does not necessitate all individuals. Understand how this blocks infinite regress.
The author leans toward this second solution as preferred (al-mukhtār).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوابه: أنا نختار الشق الثاني ونمنع لزوم التسلسل إذ قد يكون وجوب الوجوب نفسه على قياس ما قيل من أن وجود الوجود عينه، وأيضا جاز أن يكون وجوب الوجوب أو ما بعده من المراتب أمراً اعتبارياً، فإن وجود فرد من أفراد طبيعة لا يستلزم وجود جميعها، ولعل هذا هو المختار.
Its answer: We choose the second branch and deny the necessity of infinite regress, for the necessity of necessity itself may be similar to what was said that existence of existence is identical to it, and also it is permissible that the necessity of necessity or what follows it of ranks be an entitative matter, for the existence of one individual of a nature does not necessitate the existence of all of them. And perhaps this is the preferred view.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage provides the answer to the tasalsul (infinite regress) objection raised in the previous lesson. The author adopts the second branch of the alternative (that necessity is necessary) but denies that it leads to infinite regress. Two solutions are offered: First, the necessity of necessity (wujūb al-wujūb) could be identical to itself (ʿaynuhu), analogous to the claim that existence of existence is the same as existence, so no separate necessity is needed. Second, even if we grant a chain of necessities, the later ranks (e.g., the necessity of the necessity of necessity) could be entitative (iʿtibārī), not external. This is supported by the principle that the existence of one individual of a universal nature does not necessitate the existence of all its individuals. Thus, the regress is halted because the subsequent necessities have no external existence. The author suggests this latter view is the preferred one (al-mukhtār).

Key Terms
تسلسل
وجوب الوجوب
اعتباري
وجود الوجود عينه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if necessity is necessary, it must be an external existent, leading to an infinite regress of distinct necessary entities.
Correction: The author shows that the necessity of necessity can be identical to the original necessity (so no new entity) or merely entitative (not externally existent), stopping the regress.

Checkpoint
What is the second solution to the infinite regress problem for necessity?
A) The necessity of necessity is identical to its object.
B) The necessity of necessity or higher ranks are entitative matters, not external existents.
C) The existence of one individual of a nature necessitates all individuals.
D) We accept the regress as infinite.

Key Takeaway
The infinite regress objection is answered either through identity of necessity or by treating higher ranks as merely entitative, thus preserving the doctrine without paradox.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the infinite chain of reasons you might give for your own existence or for God's attributes, does the idea that 'necessity of necessity' could be identical to 'necessity' stop the search for a further cause? How does that affect your personal reliance on logical proofs versus direct acceptance of God's necessity?
?
The passage suggests that higher ranks of necessity are merely mental considerations. Have you ever caught yourself creating intellectual abstractions about God that have no real external basis in revelation? What concrete steps can you take to ensure your understanding of divine attributes remains rooted in what is taught rather than your own rational extrapolations?
?
The author presents two solutions and expresses a preference for the second (entitativity). Do you find yourself leaning toward one solution over the other? What does that reveal about your tendency either to simplify complex matters (identity) or to accept detailed mental distinctions (entitativity)? Are you willing to submit your intellect to the preferred view, even if it feels less intuitive?

The Argument that Wujūb Cannot Be an Existing Attribute and the Verbal Dispute
Argument that wujūb cannot exist and resolution of verbal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why wujūb (necessity of existence) cannot be an existing attribute and how the dispute over its nature is resolved by distinguishing different meanings of the term.

Core Idea
Wujūb is a relation, not an attribute; the real dispute is only verbal.

This passage tackles a subtle but critical question in the theology of divine attributes: is the 'necessity of existence' (wujūb) itself an existent attribute of God? The author presents a dilemma that shows the impossibility of wujūb being an existent thing, and then resolves an objection by showing that the disagreement is merely terminological. By the end, you'll see how careful definition prevents theological confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the hypothetical 'if wujūb existed' framing.
2
Identify the dilemma and its two horns
The passage presents a dilemma: if wujūb existed, it must be either identical to the essence or additional to it. Follow how the first horn is refuted and the second is deferred.
The refutation relies on wujūb being a relation (nisba).
3
Understand the verbal dispute resolution
An objector chooses the first horn and denies wujūb is a relation. The commentator suggests a third meaning of wujūb, making the dispute merely verbal.
Focus on the two different definitions of wujūb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان الوجوب موجوداً فإما نفس الماهية، ويبطله أنه نسبة بل كيفية عارضة لنسبة بين الماهية والوجود فيكون متأخراً عن الماهية بمرتبة واحدة بل بمرتبتين فكيف يكون نفسها؟ وإما زائداً على الماهية، وسنبطله حيث نبين أن الوجوب على تقدير كونه موجوداً لم يجز أن يكون زائداً على ماهية الواجب، ولم يتعرض لكونه جزءاً منها لأنه ظاهر البطلان وأيضاً كونه نسبة ينافيه. ومن أجاب عن هذا الوجه الثاني بأن منع كونه نسبة فقال: نختار أنه على تقدير وجوده عين الذات، ولا يمكن حينئذ كونه نسبة فلعله أراد بالوجوب المعنى الثالث أعني ما تتميز به الذات، فإنه تعالى متميز بذاته عن جميع ما عداه، لا بصفة تسمى الوجوب فيكون النزاع لفظياً، لأن المستدل أراد بالوجوب اقتضاء الذات للوجود، والمانع أراد به ما تتميز به الذات عن الغير.
If wujūb existed, then either [it is] the essence itself, and it is invalidated by it being a relation (nisba) – rather a quality (kayfiyya) that is accidental to a relation between the essence and existence, so it would be posterior to the essence by one degree, rather two degrees, so how could it be the essence itself? – or [it is] additional to the essence, and we will invalidate it where we explain that wujūb, on the assumption of it being existent, cannot be additional to the essence of the Necessary [Existent], and it did not address it being a part of it because its falsity is apparent, and also its being a relation conflicts with it. And someone answered this second aspect by denying it being a relation and said: we choose that on the assumption of its existence it is the essence itself, and it is impossible then for it to be a relation. Perhaps he meant by wujūb the third meaning, namely that by which the essence is distinguished, for He, exalted, is distinguished by His essence from all others, not by an attribute called wujūb, so the dispute becomes verbal, because the proponent meant by wujūb the essence's requisiteness for existence, and the objector meant by it that by which the essence is distinguished from others.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument and a verbal dispute resolution. It opens by stating that if wujūb were an existent thing, it would have to be either identical to the essence or additional to it. The first option is invalidated because wujūb is described as a relation (nisba) or a quality of a relation between the essence and existence, making it posterior to the essence by at least two degrees, so it cannot be identical to the essence. The second option is set aside to be refuted later, and it is noted that even considering it as a part of the essence is obviously false, and its being a relation contradicts it being an essential part. An objector then denies that wujūb is a relation and claims it is the essence itself. The commentator suggests that this objector might be using a third meaning of wujūb: that by which the essence is distinguished from others, not a real attribute. Thus the dispute is verbal: the proponent means by wujūb the essence's requisiteness for existence, while the objector means self-distinctiveness.

Key Terms
وجوب (wujūb)
نسبة (nisba)
كيفية عارضة (kayfiyya ʿārida)
ماهية (māhiyya)
النزاع لفظي (al-nizāʿ lafẓī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute over whether wujūb is an attribute is a real, substantive disagreement.
Correction: The passage shows it is a verbal dispute: the proponent and objector mean different things by wujūb.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the dispute over wujūb is verbal?
A) Because both sides agree that wujūb is not existent.
B) Because the proponent and objector define wujūb differently.
C) Because the objector denies that wujūb is a relation.
D) Because wujūb is identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of existence (wujūb) is not an existent attribute; it is a relation, and apparent disagreements about it can be resolved by clarifying the term's meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author resolves the dispute by distinguishing two meanings of wujūb: 'the essence's requisiteness for existence' and 'that by which the essence is distinguished from others'. Which of these two meanings strikes you as more central to your own conviction that God must exist? How does that priority shape your worship?
?
Think of a theological debate you've had or witnessed that might have been a verbal dispute. How could the approach in this lesson—clarifying definitions—change the way you engage in such discussions from now on? Will you commit to asking for definitions before arguing?
?
The passage argues that wujūb is a relation, not an attribute. If God's necessity is not an attribute of His, what does that imply about how you understand His other attributes? Does this make your concept of God more unified or more complex? Examine your heart: do you prefer a God with many attributes or a God who is simple and self-identified?

The Argument That Imkān Cannot Exist
The initial proof that imkān cannot be existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that imkān (contingency) cannot be an existent attribute and why assuming its existence leads to contradiction or infinite regress.

Core Idea
If imkān existed, it would make a contingent necessary or lead to endless regress.

The commentator applies the same logical structure used for wujūb to the case of imkān. This lesson presents the core argument: if imkān were existent, it would be either necessary or contingent, both leading to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the core argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the logical division: if imkān exists, it must be necessary or contingent.
2
Identify the two absurd outcomes
Notice the two branches: if necessary, the subject becomes necessary (contradiction); if contingent, we regress infinitely.
These are called khulf and tasalsul.
3
Understand the answer given
The commentator answers that the imkān of imkān is analogous to the case of wujūb, hinting at a deeper discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإمكان فلهذا الوجه بعينه أشار به إلى الوجه الأول فيقال: لو كان الإمكان موجوداً لكان إما واجباً أو ممكناً فإن كان واجباً مع كونه صفة للممكن كان موصوفه أولى منه بالوجوب، فكان الممكن واجباً، هذا خلف، وإن كان ممكناً نقلنا الكلام إلى إمكانه ويتسلسل. ويجاب: أن إمكان الإمكان نفسه على قياس ما مر في الوجوب، ولم يشر به إلى الوجه الثاني كما توهمه العبارة؛ إذ لا دليل على استحالة كونه صفة قائمة بالممكن. قوله : ( بعينه) ليس المراد به أنه بخصوصه جار فيه إذ لا يمكن إثبات مطلبين بدليل واحد بخصوصه من غير تغيير بوجه ما بل المراد أن ملخصه جار فيه فإن خلاصة الوجه الأول ترديد الوجود بين كونه ممكناً وواجباً واستلزامه على تقدير اتصافه بمقابله الانقلاب وعلى تقدير اتصافه بنفسه التسلسل ولا شك في جريانه في الامكان فإنه على تقدير اتصافه بالوجوب يلزم الانقلاب وعلى تقدير اتصافه بالإمكان يتسلسل، فلا يرد ما توهم أن اللازم في الوجه الأول على تقدير كون الوجوب ممكناً انقلاب الواجب ممكناً، وعلى ما قرره الشارح قدس سره يلزم على كون الإمكان واجباً انقلاب الممكن واجباً، فلا يكون الوجه الأول بعينه جارياً فيه. قوله : ( كان موصوفه أولى إلخ) أما وجوبه فلانه لو كان ممكناً يلزم من إمكانه إمكان الصفة، وأما الأولوية فلاستغنائه واحتياج الصفة إليه. قوله : ( كما توهمه العبارة) حيث أورد لفظ هذا الموضوع للقريب. قوله : (إذ لا دليل إلخ) أي لم يقم دليل على أنه على تقدير كونه موجوداً يمتنع زيادته على الماهية ولذا لم يجعلوا من أحكامه أنه على تقدير كونه موجوداً يكون نفس الماهية بخلاف الوجوب فإنه قام الدليل على عدم زيادته على تقدير وجوده، كما سيجيء في الحكم الثالث، والدليل الآتي لا يجري في الإمكان لأن الوجوب على تقدير كونه معلولاً لغيره تعالى يستلزم الانقلاب أعني إمكان الواجب واحتياج الممكن في إمكانه إلى غيره لا يستلزم الانقلاب، نعم قام الدلائل على عدميته، والناظرون لم يطلعوا على الفرق فاعترض البعض بأن الأدلة الآتية. قوله : ( إذ لا دليل على استحالة كونه صفة قائمة بالممكن أي على استحالة نفس كونه صفة قائمة به، فلا يرد أن يقال : فيه دليل لأنه إذا زاد الإمكان الموجود فإما واجب فهو باطل، أو ممكن فيتسلسل، ألا يرى أنه حينئذ يرجع إلى الوجه الأول
And as for imkān (contingency), [the same reasoning] applies by this very face (bi-ʿaynihi) – he indicated the first face – so it is said: If imkān were existent, it would be either necessary or contingent. If it were necessary while being an attribute of the contingent, then its subject would be more deserving of necessity, so the contingent would be necessary – this is a contradiction (khulf). And if it were contingent, we would transfer the discussion to its own imkān, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). And it is answered: that the imkān of imkān itself is analogous to what passed regarding wujūb, and he did not indicate the second face as the expression might suggest, since there is no proof for the impossibility of it being an attribute subsisting in the contingent. His statement: “(by this very face)” – it does not mean that it applies in its particularity, for it is impossible to establish two claims with a single proof in its particularity without any change; rather it means that its summary applies to it. For the summary of the first face is the division of existence into being contingent or necessary and its implication, on the assumption of it being qualified by its opposite, of conversion (inqilāb), and on the assumption of it being qualified by itself, of regress. There is no doubt that it applies to imkān, for on the assumption of it being qualified by necessity, conversion follows, and on the assumption of it being qualified by imkān, regress follows. So the objection that what is required in the first face on the assumption of wujūb being contingent is the conversion of the necessary into contingent, and according to what the commentator (quddisa sirruhu) established, the conversion of the contingent into necessary follows on the assumption of imkān being necessary – this objection does not apply, for the first face does not apply in its particularity to it. His statement: “(its subject would be more deserving etc.)” – as for its necessity, it is because if it were contingent, its imkān would necessitate the imkān of the attribute; and as for priority (awlawiyya), it is due to the attribute's need for it and its self-sufficiency. His statement: “(as the expression might suggest)” – because he used the word “this” for the near [referent]. His statement: “(since there is no proof etc.)” – i.e., no proof has been established that on the assumption of it being existent, its addition to the essence is impossible, and therefore they did not make it among its rulings that on the assumption of it being existent it is identical to the essence, unlike wujūb, for proof has been established that it is not additional on the assumption of its existence, as will come in the third ruling; and the following proof does not apply to imkān because wujūb on the assumption of it being an effect of another than Him, exalted, necessitates conversion, i.e., the contingency of the Necessary, whereas the need of the contingent in its imkān for another does not necessitate conversion. Indeed, proofs have been established for its non-existence, but the thinkers did not learn the difference, so some objected that the following proofs... His statement: “(since there is no proof for the impossibility of it being an attribute subsisting in the contingent)” – i.e., for the impossibility of it itself being an attribute subsisting in it, so it cannot be said that there is a proof for it, because if existent imkān were added, it would be either necessary – which is false – or contingent – leading to regress. Do you not see that then it reverts to the first face?
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a proof and commentary on the non-existence of imkān (contingency). It first presents the argument: if imkān were existent, it would be either necessary or contingent. If necessary, then its subject (the contingent thing) would be more deserving of necessity, implying the impossible contradiction that a contingent becomes necessary. If contingent, then its own imkān would be questioned, leading to infinite regress. The answer given is that the imkān of imkān itself is analogous to the case of wujūb, but the commentator clarifies that the argument is not exactly the same as the first face for wujūb, only its summary applies. He then explains that 'by this very face' means the summary or general form, not the specific argument for wujūb. He shows that in wujūb the assumption of it being contingent leads to the necessary becoming contingent (conversion), while for imkān the assumption of it being necessary leads to the contingent becoming necessary – so the specific conversion is different, but the logical form (division into opposites leading to absurdity) is the same. He then discusses the 'more deserving' point: if imkān were necessary, its subject would be more deserving of necessity because the subject is self-sufficient and the attribute depends on it. He further explains that there is no proof that imkān cannot be an additional attribute, unlike wujūb where such a proof exists (coming in the third ruling). He concludes by noting that some have confused the issue and mistakenly thought the same proof applies, but it does not; indeed, proofs for imkān's non-existence are separate.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān)
وجوب (wujūb)
خلف (khulf)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imkān is a positive attribute that can exist.
Correction: The argument shows assuming its existence leads to absurdities, implying it is not a real attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what happens if imkān is assumed to be necessary?
A) It leads to infinite regress.
B) Its subject becomes necessary – a contradiction.
C) It becomes identical to wujūb.
D) No problem arises.

Key Takeaway
The assumption that imkān exists leads to either making a contingent necessary or an infinite regress, so it cannot be an existent attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on something created (a person, a possession, or a skill), do you treat it as if it is necessarily existent and self-sufficient? How does the argument that imkān cannot be an attribute challenge your tendency to place absolute trust in contingent things?
?
If all contingent things are dependent on the Necessary Being, why do you still act as if your own plans and efforts are the decisive factor? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalised that your very existence is not self-sustaining?
?
The argument shows that assuming imkān exists leads to absurdity. In your own thinking, where have you assumed that something temporary or contingent has a fixed, independent reality? Will you commit to re-evaluating one such assumption today?

Clarifying the Distinction From Wujūb
Commentary clarifying distinctions between imkān and wujūb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced difference between the argument against imkān and that for wujūb, including why 'by this very face' refers to logical structure, not identical content.

Core Idea
The argument mirrors wujūb in form but swaps necessity and contingency.

After presenting the core argument, the commentator clarifies that 'by this very face' means the logical summary, not the exact same proof. This lesson explores the difference in conversion direction and why imkān, unlike wujūb, lacks proof against being an attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification about 'by this very face'
Understand that the commentator distinguishes between the specific argument (for wujūb) and its summary applied to imkān.
2
Note the difference in conversion direction
For wujūb, if it were contingent, the necessary becomes contingent. For imkān, if it were necessary, the contingent becomes necessary – opposite directions.
3
Grasp the 'more deserving' point
The subject is more deserving of necessity because it is self-sufficient, while the attribute depends on it.
4
Understand why no proof exists for impossibility
Unlike wujūb, no proof shows that imkān cannot be an additional attribute; the argument only shows it cannot exist independently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإمكان فلهذا الوجه بعينه أشار به إلى الوجه الأول فيقال: لو كان الإمكان موجوداً لكان إما واجباً أو ممكناً فإن كان واجباً مع كونه صفة للممكن كان موصوفه أولى منه بالوجوب، فكان الممكن واجباً، هذا خلف، وإن كان ممكناً نقلنا الكلام إلى إمكانه ويتسلسل. ويجاب: أن إمكان الإمكان نفسه على قياس ما مر في الوجوب، ولم يشر به إلى الوجه الثاني كما توهمه العبارة؛ إذ لا دليل على استحالة كونه صفة قائمة بالممكن. قوله : ( بعينه) ليس المراد به أنه بخصوصه جار فيه إذ لا يمكن إثبات مطلبين بدليل واحد بخصوصه من غير تغيير بوجه ما بل المراد أن ملخصه جار فيه فإن خلاصة الوجه الأول ترديد الوجود بين كونه ممكناً وواجباً واستلزامه على تقدير اتصافه بمقابله الانقلاب وعلى تقدير اتصافه بنفسه التسلسل ولا شك في جريانه في الامكان فإنه على تقدير اتصافه بالوجوب يلزم الانقلاب وعلى تقدير اتصافه بالإمكان يتسلسل، فلا يرد ما توهم أن اللازم في الوجه الأول على تقدير كون الوجوب ممكناً انقلاب الواجب ممكناً، وعلى ما قرره الشارح قدس سره يلزم على كون الإمكان واجباً انقلاب الممكن واجباً، فلا يكون الوجه الأول بعينه جارياً فيه. قوله : ( كان موصوفه أولى إلخ) أما وجوبه فلانه لو كان ممكناً يلزم من إمكانه إمكان الصفة، وأما الأولوية فلاستغنائه واحتياج الصفة إليه. قوله : ( كما توهمه العبارة) حيث أورد لفظ هذا الموضوع للقريب. قوله : (إذ لا دليل إلخ) أي لم يقم دليل على أنه على تقدير كونه موجوداً يمتنع زيادته على الماهية ولذا لم يجعلوا من أحكامه أنه على تقدير كونه موجوداً يكون نفس الماهية بخلاف الوجوب فإنه قام الدليل على عدم زيادته على تقدير وجوده، كما سيجيء في الحكم الثالث، والدليل الآتي لا يجري في الإمكان لأن الوجوب على تقدير كونه معلولاً لغيره تعالى يستلزم الانقلاب أعني إمكان الواجب واحتياج الممكن في إمكانه إلى غيره لا يستلزم الانقلاب، نعم قام الدلائل على عدميته، والناظرون لم يطلعوا على الفرق فاعترض البعض بأن الأدلة الآتية. قوله : ( إذ لا دليل على استحالة كونه صفة قائمة بالممكن أي على استحالة نفس كونه صفة قائمة به، فلا يرد أن يقال : فيه دليل لأنه إذا زاد الإمكان الموجود فإما واجب فهو باطل، أو ممكن فيتسلسل، ألا يرى أنه حينئذ يرجع إلى الوجه الأول
And as for imkān (contingency), [the same reasoning] applies by this very face (bi-ʿaynihi) – he indicated the first face – so it is said: If imkān were existent, it would be either necessary or contingent. If it were necessary while being an attribute of the contingent, then its subject would be more deserving of necessity, so the contingent would be necessary – this is a contradiction (khulf). And if it were contingent, we would transfer the discussion to its own imkān, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). For the summary of the first face is the division of existence into being contingent or necessary and its implication, on the assumption of it being qualified by its opposite, of conversion (inqilāb), and on the assumption of it being qualified by itself, of regress. There is no doubt that it applies to imkān, for on the assumption of it being qualified by necessity, conversion follows, and on the assumption of it being qualified by imkān, regress follows. So the objection that what is required in the first face on the assumption of wujūb being contingent is the conversion of the necessary into contingent, and according to what the commentator (quddisa sirruhu) established, the conversion of the contingent into necessary follows on the assumption of imkān being necessary – this objection does not apply, for the first face does not apply in its particularity to it. His statement: “(its subject would be more deserving etc.)” – as for its necessity, it is because if it were contingent, its imkān would necessitate the imkān of the attribute; and as for priority (awlawiyya), it is due to the attribute's need for it and its self-sufficiency. His statement: “(as the expression might suggest)” – because he used the word “this” for the near [referent]. His statement: “(since there is no proof etc.)” – i.e., no proof has been established that on the assumption of it being existent, its addition to the essence is impossible, and therefore they did not make it among its rulings that on the assumption of it being existent it is identical to the essence, unlike wujūb, for proof has been established that it is not additional on the assumption of its existence, as will come in the third ruling; and the following proof does not apply to imkān because wujūb on the assumption of it being an effect of another than Him, exalted, necessitates conversion, i.e., the contingency of the Necessary, whereas the need of the contingent in its imkān for another does not necessitate conversion. Indeed, proofs have been established for its non-existence, but the thinkers did not learn the difference, so some objected that the following proofs... Commentary
This passage is a proof and commentary on the non-existence of imkān (contingency). It first presents the argument: if imkān were existent, it would be either necessary or contingent. If necessary, then its subject (the contingent thing) would be more deserving of necessity, implying the impossible contradiction that a contingent becomes necessary. If contingent, then its own imkān would be questioned, leading to infinite regress. The answer given is that the imkān of imkān itself is analogous to the case of wujūb, but the commentator clarifies that the argument is not exactly the same as the first face for wujūb, only its summary applies. He then explains that 'by this very face' means the summary or general form, not the specific argument for wujūb. He shows that in wujūb the assumption of it being contingent leads to the necessary becoming contingent (conversion), while for imkān the assumption of it being necessary leads to the contingent becoming necessary – so the specific conversion is different, but the logical form (division into opposites leading to absurdity) is the same. He then discusses the 'more deserving' point: if imkān were necessary, its subject would be more deserving of necessity because the subject is self-sufficient and the attribute depends on it. He further explains that there is no proof that imkān cannot be an additional attribute, unlike wujūb where such a proof exists (coming in the third ruling). He concludes by noting that some have confused the issue and mistakenly thought the same proof applies, but it does not; indeed, proofs for imkān's non-existence are separate.

Key Terms
الوجه الأول (al-wajh al-awwal)
انقلاب (inqilāb)
أولوية (awlawiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument against imkān is identical to that against wujūb in all respects.
Correction: The logical form is the same, but the specific conversion direction is opposite.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the argument for wujūb and that for imkān?
A) The argument for wujūb leads to regress while imkān leads to contradiction.
B) The conversion direction is opposite: wujūb converts necessary to contingent, imkān converts contingent to necessary.
C) Imkān has no argument at all.
D) Wujūb is shown to be non-existent, while imkān is shown to be existent.

Key Takeaway
The argument against imkān shares its logical structure with that for wujūb but differs in the direction of conversion, and it does not prove imkān cannot be an attribute.

?

?
The passage reveals how a subtle shift in an argument (from wujūb to imkān) changes the conclusion. The First Argument Against the Existence of Possibility and Its Answer
Presents the dilemma that possibility cannot be existent and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma argument that possibility (imkan) cannot be an existent attribute and how the Ash'ari answer resolves it through analogy with necessity.

Core Idea
Possibility's existence is defended by parity with necessity's possibility.

The mutakallimun debated whether possibility (imkan) is a real attribute or merely a conceptual relation. This lesson presents one objection against its reality: a dilemma that forces contradiction or infinite regress. The answer shows that the same logic would apply to necessity, which is accepted, so the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the full translation. Let the structure of the dilemma and answer become clear.
The passage is divided into the objection (dilemma) and the answer.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection claims possibility, if existent, must be either necessary (wajib) or possible (mumkin). Trace each horn: why does necessary lead to contradiction? Why does possible lead to infinite regress?
3
Understand the answer's key move
The answer says the possibility of possibility itself is analogous to what was said about necessity. That means the same dilemma would apply to necessity, but necessity is accepted as an attribute, so the dilemma is not decisive.
This shows the dilemma proves too much.
4
Notice the author's clarification
The author clarifies that the argument targets only the first aspect (possibility as attribute), not the second (as relation). He emphasizes there is no proof that possibility cannot be an attribute.
This limits the scope of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإمكان فلهذا الوجه بعينه ) أشار به إلى الوجه الأول فيقال : لو كان الإمكان موجوداً لكان إما واجباً أو ممكناً فإن كان واجباً مع كونه صفة للممكن كان موصوفه أولى منه بالوجوب، فكان الممكن واجباً، هذا خلف، وإن كان ممكناً نقلنا الكلام إلى إمكانه ويتسلسل . ويجاب : أن إمكان الإمكان نفسه على قياس ما مر في الوجوب، ولم يشر به إلى الوجه الثاني كما توهمه العبارة؛ إذ لا دليل على استحالة كونه صفة قائمة بالممكن
As for possibility (al-imkan), it is by this very aspect—he indicated the first aspect—so it is said: if possibility were existent, it would either be necessary (wajib) or possible (mumkin). If it were necessary while being an attribute of the possible, then its subject would be more deserving of necessity, so the possible would be necessary; this is a contradiction. And if it were possible, we would transfer the discussion to its own possibility, leading to infinite regress. It is answered: the possibility of possibility itself is analogous to what was mentioned regarding necessity, and he did not indicate the second aspect as the phrase might suggest, for there is no proof that it is impossible for it to be an attribute subsisting in the possible.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a proof-answer unit. It begins by presenting the first argument against the existence of possibility as a real attribute. The argument sets up a dilemma: if possibility (imkan) were an existent thing, it would either be necessary (wajib) or possible (mumkin). If it were necessary, then because it is an attribute of a possible entity, that possible entity would be more deserving of necessity, turning the possible into a necessary being—a contradiction. If it were possible, then its own possibility would require another possibility, leading to an infinite regress. The answer responds that the possibility of possibility itself is analogous to the earlier discussion about necessity (where the same dilemma was handled). The author notes that this argument does not target the second aspect (where possibility is considered as a relation), and there is no proof that possibility cannot be an attribute. Thus, the argument does not definitively disprove the existence of possibility as a real attribute.

ماهية
انقلاب
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this dilemma proves that possibility cannot be existent at all.
Correction: The answer shows that the dilemma only works under a specific interpretation (first aspect) and does not rule out other views; the same logic would apply to necessity, which is accepted.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma presented against the existence of imkan (possibility) as an existent attribute?
A) If imkan is wajib, then the possible becomes wajib; if imkan is mumkin, regress.
B) If imkan is wajib, regress; if mumkin, the possible becomes necessary.
C) If imkan is existent, it must be either the quiddity or part of it.
D) If imkan is non-existent, it cannot be a predicate.

Key Takeaway
The dilemma against possibility fails because the same logic would apply to necessity, which is accepted as an attribute.

Why the First Argument Does Not Disprove Possibility as an Attribute
Clarifies that the first argument does not disprove imkan's reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first argument (the division of existence into possible and necessary) does not rule out the possibility of imkan being a real attribute, and why no proof has been established for its impossibility.

Core Idea
No proof disallows imkan from being an additional attribute.

This lesson dives into a nuanced debate about the nature of imkan (possibility). The commentators address a potential misunderstanding: the first argument seems to disprove the existence of imkan as a real attribute, but they clarify that it only works if we assume a specific interpretation. The key is that no definitive proof establishes its impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the flow of the commentary come through.
The passage is dense; focus on the main claim.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that the passage argues the first argument does not disprove imkan's existence. Find the phrase 'for there is no proof' and understand its significance.
3
Understand the reasoning about the 'first aspect'
The commentary explains that the gist of the first argument applies to imkan (leading to conversion or regress), but not the exact same proof without modification. This shows the argument only works under a specific interpretation.
4
Note the distinction between imkan and wujub
Observe that unlike necessity, no proof exists that imkan cannot be additional to the quiddity. This is the central point: the impossibility is not proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وفي الملخص إلخ) نقل كلامي الملخص وشرحه لبيان أن النزاع في وجوديته على تقدیر كونه نسبة فالنزاع معنوي . قوله : ( يطلق) أي إطلاقاً مشهوراً ، فلا ينافي إطلاقه على المعنى الثالث قوله : ( بعينه) ليس المراد به أنه بخصوصه جار فيه إذ لا يمكن إثبات مطلبين بدليل واحد بخصوصه من غير تغيير بوجه ما بل المراد أن ملخصه جار فيه فإن خلاصة الوجه الأول ترديد الوجود بين كونه ممكناً وواجباً واستلزامه على تقدير اتصافه بمقابله الانقلاب وعلى تقدير اتصافه بنفسه التسلسل ولا شك في جريانه في الامكان فإنه على تقدير اتصافه بالوجوب يلزم الانقلاب وعلى تقدير اتصافه بالإمكان يتسلسل، فلا يرد ما توهم أن اللازم في الوجه الأول على تقدير كون الوجوب ممكناً انقلاب الواجب ممكناً، وعلى ما قرره الشارح قدس سره يلزم على كون الإمكان واجباً انقلاب الممكن واجباً، فلا يكون الوجه الأول بعينه جارياً فيه. قوله : ( كان موصوفه أولى إلخ) أما وجوبه فلانه لو كان ممكناً يلزم من إمكانه إمكان الصفة، وأما الأولوية فلاستغنائه واحتياج الصفة إليه . قوله : ( كما توهمه العبارة) حيث أورد لفظ هذا الموضوع للقريب . قوله : (إذ لا دليل إلخ) أي لم يقم دليل على أنه على تقدير كونه موجوداً يمتنع زيادته على الماهية ولذا لم يجعلوا من أحكامه أنه على تقدير كونه موجوداً يكون نفس الماهية بخلاف الوجوب فإنه قام الدليل على عدم زيادته على تقدير وجوده، كما سيجيء في الحكم الثالث، والدليل الآتي لا يجري في الإمكان لأن الوجوب على تقدير كونه معلولاً لغيره تعالى يستلزم الانقلاب أعني إمكان الواجب واحتياج الممكن في إمكانه إلى غيره لا يستلزم الانقلاب، نعم قام الدلائل على عدميته، والناظرون لم يطلعوا على الفرق فاعترض البعض بأن الأدلة الآتية بخلاف الوجوب إذ يلزم منه كون الماهية واجبة قبل وجوبها، كما سيأتي. قوله : ( إذ لا دليل على استحالة كونه صفة قائمة بالممكن أي على استحالة نفس كونه صفة قائمة به، فلا يرد أن يقال : فيه دليل لأنه إذا زاد الإمكان الموجود فإما واجب فهو باطل، أو ممكن فيتسلسل، ألا يرى أنه حينئذ يرجع إلى الوجه الأول
His statement: 'And in al-Mulakhkhas, etc.'—he quotes the words of al-Mulakhkhas and its commentary to clarify that the dispute is about its existence on the assumption that it is a relation, so the dispute is conceptual. His statement: 'it is used'—meaning a well-known usage, so it does not contradict its usage for the third meaning. His statement: 'by this very aspect'—it is not meant that it applies specifically without any change, for it is impossible to prove two claims with one specific proof without any modification; rather, the meaning is that its gist applies, for the essence of the first aspect is the division of existence into possible and necessary and the implication, on the assumption of its being described by its opposite, of conversion, and on the assumption of its being described by itself, of regress. There is no doubt that this applies to possibility, because on the assumption of its being described by necessity, conversion follows, and on the assumption of its being described by possibility, regress follows. So the objection does not stand that what is necessary in the first aspect on the assumption of necessity being possible is the conversion of the necessary into possible, and according to what the commentator (may God sanctify his spirit) has established, it follows on the assumption of possibility being necessary the conversion of the possible into necessary, so the first aspect does not apply specifically to it. His statement: 'its subject would be more deserving, etc.'—as for its necessity, because if it were possible, its possibility would entail the possibility of the attribute; as for the priority, because of its self-sufficiency and the attribute's need of it. His statement: 'as the phrase might suggest'—where it used the word 'this' for the near object. His statement: 'for there is no proof, etc.'—meaning no proof has been established that on the assumption of its existence, its being additional to the quiddity is impossible, and therefore they did not make it among its rulings that on the assumption of its existence it would be identical to the quiddity, unlike necessity, for proof has been established that it is not additional on the assumption of its existence, as will come in the third ruling. The following proof does not apply to possibility, because necessity on the assumption of its being an effect of another, exalted, entails conversion, meaning the possibility of the necessary, but the need of the possible in its possibility for another does not entail conversion. Yes, proofs have been established for its non-existence, and the investigators were not aware of the difference, so some objected that the following proofs... unlike necessity, for it entails that the quiddity would be necessary before its necessity, as will come. His statement: 'for there is no proof that it is impossible for it to be an attribute subsisting in the possible'—meaning regarding the impossibility of its being itself an attribute subsisting in it, so it cannot be said that there is a proof because if existent possibility is additional, then either it is necessary—which is false—or possible leading to regress. Do you not see that it would then revert to the first aspect?
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary. It begins by clarifying that the dispute about the existence of possibility is on the assumption that it is a relation, so it is conceptual. Then it explains that the phrase 'by this very aspect' does not mean the exact same proof applies without modification; rather the gist of the first argument applies. The essence of that argument is the division of existence into possible and necessary, leading to conversion or regress. This gist applies to possibility: if possibility were existent and described by necessity, it would lead to conversion (the possible becoming necessary); if described by possibility, it would lead to regress. The commentator then addresses a misunderstanding: in the first argument, if necessity were possible, the necessary would become possible; but here, if possibility were necessary, the possible would become necessary. Thus the first aspect does not apply identically. He explains the rationale for the priority: the subject is self-sufficient while the attribute needs it. Next, he emphasizes that there is no proof that possibility cannot be additional to the quiddity, unlike necessity where proof exists for its non-addition. The following proof that works for necessity does not apply to possibility because necessity as an effect entails conversion, but the possible's need of another for its imkan does not. Finally, he reiterates that there is no proof that imkan cannot be an attribute, and an attempted objection that reverts to the first aspect is unsuccessful.

صفة
وجود
عدم
انقلاب
ماهية

إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first argument proves that imkan cannot exist as a real attribute.
Correction: The argument only shows a problem if imkan is real and described by necessity or possibility, but no proof establishes its impossibility as an attribute.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentary say the first argument does not disprove the existence of imkan as a real attribute?
A) Because imkan is necessarily non-existent.
B) Because there is no proof that imkan cannot be an additional attribute.
C) Because the argument leads to regress.
D) Because imkan is a relation.

Key Takeaway
The first argument does not prove that imkan cannot be a real attribute; it only shows a contradiction under a specific assumption, and no proof for its impossibility exists.

The Second Argument Against the Existence of Possibility and Its Critique
Presents a second argument against possibility and critiques it as forced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument against the existence of possibility as an attribute and the reasons why it is considered a forced argument (takalluf).

Core Idea
The second argument fails because it requires extra premises, making it a forced effort rather than a sound proof.

This lesson presents a more elaborate argument that possibility cannot be a real attribute, along with a detailed critique that exposes its weaknesses. You will see how the argument attempts to show that possibility, if existent, would lead to an infinite regress or contradiction, and how the commentator labels it as 'takalluf' (forced exertion).

Guided Path
1
Read the argument's main claim
Start by reading the first part of the passage where the argument considers three scenarios for possibility if it were existent: identical to the quiddity, part of it, or additional. Notice how it dismisses the first two quickly.
The argument is trying to reduce possibility to an impossibility.
2
Follow the analysis of the 'additional' scenario
The passage argues that if possibility is additional, it would be an effect of the quiddity, leading to a regress because the quiddity would need another possibility. Also, the cause must precede by necessity, leading to another possibility before possibility.
This is the core of the argument against additional possibility.
3
Understand the second sub-argument about priority
The argument also claims possibility must be prior to existence, but positive attributes are posterior, so possibility cannot be a positive attribute. Read that part carefully.
4
Read the critique labeling it 'takalluf'
Now read the second half of the passage where the commentator points out that this argument requires extra premises not needed in the first argument, thus it is a forced effort. Pay attention to the specific points about necessity and concomitants.
The commentator distinguishes two meanings of 'necessity' to clarify the critique.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider why the second argument is weaker than the first and what it teaches us about proper theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يتكلف إجراء الثاني في الإمكان فيقال : لو كان موجوداً لكان إما نفس ماهية الممكن أو جزءها، ويبطل كلاً منهما كونه نسبة بين الماهية والوجود، أو كان زائداً عليها قائماً بها فيكون معلولاً لها إذ يستحيل استفادتها إمكانها الذاتي من غيرها، وإلا لم تكن ممكنة في حد ذاتها، والعلة متقدمة على المعلول ،بالوجوب، فذلك الوجوب إما بالذات وهو محال في الممكن وإما بالغير والوجوب بالغير فرع الإمكان الذاتي فللممكن قبل إمكانه إمكان آخر ) ووجه آخر وهو أنه أي الإمكان (سابق على الوجود ) لأن الشيء يمكن وجوده في نفسه فيوجد من غيره ) والصفة الثبوتية متأخرة . قوله : ( وقد يتكلف وجه التكلف احتياجه في إبطال الزيادة إلى مقدمات غير مذكورة فيما سيأتي في إبطال زيادة الوجوب فلا يلائمه الحوالة المذكورة بقوله : وسنبطله . قوله : ( وإلا لم تكن ممكنة في حد ذاتها لا يخفى أن هذا إنما يقتضي أن تكون الماهية في نفسها مقتضية له بحيث لا يتصور انفكاكه عنها، فأينما وجدت كانت متصفة به كما هو حكم لوازم الماهية وهذا لا ينافي كونه معلولاً لغيرها؛ لجواز أن تكون الماهية مع لوازمها معلولة له بحيث لا يتصور الانفكاك بينهما أصلاً كما قالوا إن جعل الماهية جعل للوازمها . نعم لا يمكن استفادتها الإمكان من غيرها بأن يكون متأخراً عنها حاصلاً بعدها، فإنه يستلزم الانقلاب. قوله: (يمكن وجوده في نفسه إلخ) فالإمكان مقدم على الوجود بالذات بمراتب؛ لتقدمه على الاحتياج المتقدم على الإيجاد المتقدم على الوجود، وقد يتقدم زماناً أيضاً كما في الممكنات الحادثة، ولظهور التقدم في الإمكان قال : وربما يستعمل في الوجوب . قوله : ( وقد يتكلف إلخ وجه التكلف أنه يحتاج في إجراء ذلك إلى مقدمات زائدة ليست بصريحة في الوجه الثاني ولا هي مما يمكن اعتبارها بالقياس إلى الوجوب، وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( والعلة متقدمة على المعلول (بالوجوب) أي بوجوب الوجود، لأن الشيء ما لم يوجد لم يجب وما لم يجب لم يوجد فبطل ما يتوهم من أن هذا الوجوب كيفية نسبة التقدم إلى العلة لا كيفية نسبة الوجوب إلى الممكن والوجوب الذاتي المستحيل في الممكن إنما هو الكيفية الثانية لا الأولى
And one might exert effort to apply the second aspect to possibility, so it is said: if it were existent, it would be either the very quiddity of the possible or a part of it, and each of these is invalidated because it is a relation between the quiddity and existence; or it would be additional to it, subsisting in it, and thus it would be an effect of it, since it is impossible for it to derive its intrinsic possibility from another, otherwise it would not be possible in itself. The cause precedes the effect by necessity (bi-l-wujub). That necessity is either essential (bi-l-dhat), which is impossible in the possible, or by another (bi-l-ghayr), and necessity by another is derivative of intrinsic possibility, so for the possible there is another possibility before its possibility. And another aspect: that it (i.e., possibility) is prior to existence, because a thing can exist in itself and then comes into existence from another, while a positive attribute is posterior. His statement: 'and one might exert effort'—the reason for the exertion is its need, in refuting the addition, for premises not mentioned in what follows regarding the refutation of the addition of necessity, so it does not accord with the reference mentioned in his statement: 'and we will refute it.' His statement: 'otherwise it would not be possible in itself'—it is clear that this only necessitates that the quiddity in itself is a requisite for it such that its separation from it cannot be conceived, so wherever it exists it is described by it, as is the ruling for necessary concomitants of the quiddity. This does not negate its being an effect of another, because it is possible for the quiddity along with its concomitants to be effects of another such that no separation between them can be conceived at all, as they said: making the quiddity is making its concomitants. Indeed, it cannot derive its possibility from another in the sense that it is posterior to it occurring after it, for that entails conversion. His statement: 'it can exist in itself, etc.'—so possibility is prior to existence by degrees in essence, because it precedes need, which precedes bringing into existence, which precedes existence. It may also be prior in time, as in temporal possibles. Due to the apparent priority in possibility, he said: perhaps it is used in necessity. His statement: 'and one might exert effort, etc.'—the reason for the exertion is that it requires, in applying that, additional premises that are not explicit in the second aspect nor can be considered by analogy to necessity, and that is clear. His statement: 'the cause precedes the effect by necessity (bi-l-wujub)'—meaning by the necessity of existence, because a thing does not exist until it is necessary, and it is not necessary until it exists. Thus what is imagined—that this necessity is a modality of the relation of priority to the cause, not a modality of the relation of necessity to the possible—falls away. Essential necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) impossible in the possible is only the second modality, not the first.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents a second, more elaborate argument against the existence of possibility as a real attribute, followed by a critique labeling it as a forced (takalluf) effort. The argument begins by considering three possibilities for existent possibility: it is either identical to the quiddity of the possible, or a part of it, or additional to it and subsisting in it. It quickly dismisses the first two because possibility is a relation, not a component. For the third (additional), it argues that possibility would then be an effect of the quiddity, but the quiddity cannot derive its intrinsic possibility from another, otherwise it would not be possible in itself. The cause must precede its effect by necessity (al-wujub). That necessity is either essential (impossible for a possible) or derived from another, but derived necessity presupposes intrinsic possibility, leading to a regress: the possible would have another possibility before its possibility. A second sub-argument states that possibility is prior to existence, whereas positive attributes are posterior to existence, so possibility cannot be a positive attribute. The subsequent commentary points out the weakness of this argument: it requires extra premises not present in the refutation of the addition of necessity, making it forced. The commentary further explains that the statement 'otherwise it would not be possible in itself' only shows that possibility is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, not that it cannot be an effect of another; it is possible for the quiddity and its concomitants to be effects simultaneously. The priority of possibility over existence is acknowledged, but this does not prove it is not a positive attribute, as the argument claims. The final note distinguishes two meanings of 'necessity' (wujub) in the phrase 'the cause precedes the effect by necessity'—one referring to the modality of the relation of priority, the other to the modality of the relation of necessity to the possible. Only the latter is impossible in the possible, not the former.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the second argument successfully proves that possibility cannot be existent because it seems to show a regress.
Correction: The commentator labels it as 'takalluf' (forced) because it requires additional premises not present in the first argument. Moreover, the regress is avoided if possibility is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, not a separate effect.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator consider the second argument 'takalluf' (forced exertion)?
A) Because it uses an infinite regress.
B) Because it requires additional premises not needed in the first argument.
C) Because it assumes possibility is a relation.
D) Because it contradicts the first argument.

Key Takeaway
The second argument against possibility is weak because it relies on extra assumptions, making it a forced effort rather than a rigorous proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator describes the second argument as 'takalluf'—a forced effort with extra assumptions. Are there teachings or practices in your faith that you follow merely out of habit or pressure, without being convinced of their rational foundation? Will you seek clarity even if it means letting go of comfortable but unexamined beliefs?
?
This passage distinguishes two meanings of 'necessity': one about cause-effect priority and another about a thing's intrinsic mode. When you say 'Allah is necessary,' do you truly grasp that His necessity is essential, not derived? How might confusing these affect the quality of your worship and reliance on Him?
?
The argument shows that possibility is prior to existence—you were possible before you existed. How does that reality humble you? Commit to one concrete way you will thank your Creator today for bringing you from mere possibility into being.

The Second Argument for Imkan and Its Application to Wujub
Al-Jurjani explains the second argument that imkan is prior to existence and applies it to wujub.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that imkan (contingency) is a mental consideration, not a positive attribute, and how it applies to wujub (necessity).

Core Idea
Imkan and wujub are mental constructs, not real attributes.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a sophisticated argument demonstrating that both imkan and wujub are mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya), not positive attributes inhering in things. Al-Jurjani first presents a forced application of an earlier argument to imkan, then offers a more direct 'second face' that shows imkan's priority over existence. Finally, he applies this same reasoning to wujub, citing Imam al-Razi.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the compact style of kalam reasoning.
2
Understand the forced argument
Follow the hypothetical: if imkan were an attribute, it would be either essence/part (invalid) or superadded (making it an effect, leading to regress).
This shows imkan cannot be a positive attribute.
3
Grasp the second face and application
Learn the direct argument: imkan is prior to existence, so it cannot be a positive attribute (which are posterior). See how this applies to wujub as well.
This second face is more straightforward.

قوله : ( وقد يتكلف وجه التكلف احتياجه في إبطال الزيادة إلى مقدمات غير مذكورة فيما سيأتي في إبطال زيادة الوجوب فلا يلائمه الحوالة المذكورة بقوله : وسنبطله . قوله : ( وإلا لم تكن ممكنة في حد ذاتها لا يخفى أن هذا إنما يقتضي أن تكون الماهية في نفسها مقتضية له بحيث لا يتصور انفكاكه عنها، فأينما وجدت كانت متصفة به كما هو حكم لوازم الماهية وهذا لا ينافي كونه معلولاً لغيرها؛ لجواز أن تكون الماهية مع لوازمها معلولة له بحيث لا يتصور الانفكاك بينهما أصلاً كما قالوا إن جعل الماهية جعل للوازمها . نعم لا يمكن استفادتها الإمكان من غيرها بأن يكون متأخراً عنها حاصلاً بعدها، فإنه يستلزم الانقلاب. قوله: (يمكن وجوده في نفسه إلخ) فالإمكان مقدم على الوجود بالذات بمراتب؛ لتقدمه على الاحتياج المتقدم على الإيجاد المتقدم على الوجود، وقد يتقدم زماناً أيضاً كما في الممكنات الحادثة، ولظهور التقدم في الإمكان قال : وربما يستعمل في الوجوب . قوله : ( وقد يتكلف إلخ وجه التكلف أنه يحتاج في إجراء ذلك إلى مقدمات زائدة ليست بصريحة في الوجه الثاني ولا هي مما يمكن اعتبارها بالقياس إلى الوجوب، وهو ظاهر. قوله : ( والعلة متقدمة على المعلول (بالوجوب) أي بوجوب الوجود، لأن الشيء ما لم يوجد لم يجب وما لم يجب لم يوجد فبطل ما يتوهم من أن هذا الوجوب كيفية نسبة التقدم إلى العلة لا كيفية نسبة الوجوب إلى الممكن والوجوب الذاتي المستحيل في الممكن إنما هو الكيفية الثانية لا الأولى .
عنه ) أي عن الوجود فإن قيام الصفة الموجودة بموصوفها فرع لوجوده، فلا يكون الإمكان صفة موجودة. وربما يستعمل هذا الوجه الآخر (في الوجوب) كما استعمله الإمام الرازي فيقال : الوجوب سابق على الوجود سبقاً ذاتياً (لأن إيجاب ماهيته لوجوده يستتبع وجوده عقلا ولذلك صح أن يقال : اقتضى ذاته وجوده فوجود الصفة الثبوتية يستحيل أن يسبق على وجود موصوفها سبقاً ذاتياً.
And one may labor to apply the second [argument] to imkan (contingency), saying: if it were existent, it would be either the essence of the possible or a part of it, but both are invalid because it is a relation between essence and existence, or it would be superadded to it and inhering in it, thus being an effect of it, since it is impossible for it to derive its essential imkan from another, otherwise it would not be possible in itself, and the cause is prior to the effect by wujub (necessity); that wujub is either essential, which is impossible for the possible, or by another, and wujub by another is a branch of essential imkan, so the possible would have another imkan before its imkan. And another face: that imkan is prior to existence because a thing can exist in itself and then comes to existence from another, whereas a positive attribute is posterior. His statement: (And one may labor [explanation]): the reason for the labor is its need for premises not mentioned in what will come concerning invalidating the superaddition of wujub, so the reference mentioned by his statement 'and we will invalidate it' does not suit it. His statement: (otherwise it would not be possible in itself): it is not hidden that this only requires that the essence in itself necessitates it such that separation from it is inconceivable, so wherever it exists it is characterized by it, as is the case with essential concomitants, and this does not negate its being an effect of another; for it is possible that the essence together with its concomitants is an effect of that other such that separation between them is absolutely inconceivable, as they said: making the essence is making its concomitants. Indeed, it cannot derive imkan from another such that it is posterior to it and occurs after it, for that entails change. His statement: (it can exist in itself etc.): for imkan is prior to existence by many essential degrees; because it is prior to need which is prior to bringing into existence which is prior to existence, and it may also be temporally prior as in temporal possibles, and due to the obviousness of priority in imkan he said: and perhaps it is used for wujub. His statement: (and one may labor etc.): the reason for the labor is that it requires additional premises which are not explicit in the second face and which cannot be considered in comparison to wujub, and it is obvious. His statement: (and the cause is prior to the effect by wujub): meaning by the necessity of existence, because a thing does not exist until it is necessary, and it is not necessary until it exists, so what is imagined that this wujub is a quality of the relation of priority to the cause and not the quality of the relation of wujub to the possible is invalidated; and the essential wujub impossible for the possible is only the second quality, not the first. (About it): i.e. about existence, for the subsistence of an existing attribute in its subject is a branch of its existence, so imkan is not an existing attribute. And perhaps this other face is used for wujub as Imam al-Razi used it, saying: wujub is prior to existence by essential priority, because the necessitation of its essence for its existence entails its existence intellectually, and therefore it is correct to say: its essence demanded its existence, so the existence of a positive attribute cannot be prior to the existence of its subject by essential priority.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the second face (وجه آخر) in the argument that imkan (contingency) and wujub (necessity) are mental considerations (amoor i'tibariyya), not positive attributes. The author begins by explaining the forced application of the second argument to imkan. If imkan were a positive existent attribute, it would have to be either the essence of the possible or a part of it, but that is invalid because imkan is a relation between essence and existence. Alternatively, it would be an accident inhering in the possible, making the possible its cause; but it is impossible for a thing to derive its essential imkan from another, because then it would not be essentially possible. The cause must be prior by necessity, and that necessity is either essential (impossible for a possible) or by another, which itself depends on essential imkan, leading to an infinite regress. Then the second face is stated: imkan is prior to existence because a thing's possibility for existence is prior to its actual existence, whereas a positive attribute is posterior to the existence of its subject. The commentary clarifies the subtle points, such as the meaning of 'priority' and the objection that if imkan were a positive attribute, it would have to be prior to existence, which is impossible. The passage also applies this face to wujub, citing Imam al-Razi: wujub is prior to existence by essential priority, because the very nature of the necessary being entails existence, and a positive attribute cannot be prior to the existence of its subject. This demonstrates that both imkan and wujub are not positive attributes but mental considerations.

The Argument from Impossibility of Posteriority and the Two Principles
Al-Jurjani presents a sufficient proof based on impossibility of posteriority and states two underlying principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani proves that wujub and imkan are non-existential (mental considerations) using the impossibility of posteriority and how he derives two underlying principles.

Core Idea
If an attribute cannot be posterior to its subject, it is not a real attribute—it is a mental consideration.

This passage presents a clever argument that avoids the need to prove priority. By focusing on the impossibility of posteriority, al-Jurjani establishes a criterion for identifying mental attributes. He then encapsulates the logic in two principles from the author of al-Talwihat, which form the basis of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall the debate about whether wujub (necessity) and imkan (possibility) are real positive attributes or mere mental considerations. This argument aims to show they are mental.
The argument is part of a larger discussion on the ontological status of modal attributes.
2
Read the argument
Read the first part of the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key premise: imkan and wujub cannot be posterior to the existence of their subject, unlike positive attributes which must be posterior.
This is the core move: impossibility of posteriority is sufficient.
3
Generalize the proof
Observe that the argument applies to any attribute whose posteriority is impossible, like origination (huduth). This shows the proof is not ad hoc.
This strengthens the case for the mental nature of such attributes.
4
Examine the two principles
Read the second part where a rule (dabit) containing two principles is given. The first principle states that any genus whose individuals are necessarily characterized by that genus is mental.
The first principle is the basis for the first face of the argument.
5
Connect principles to argument
See how the first principle applies: wujub and imkan are such that any subject must have them, so they are mental. The second principle relates to the sufficiency of impossibility of posteriority.
The two principles unify the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ويكفينا ) في الاستدلال على كون الوجوب أو الإمكان أمراً عدمياً ( امتناع تأخره ) عن وجود الموصوف، فلا نحتاج في ذلك إلى بيان التقدم فلا يتوجه علينا أنا لا نسلم تقدمه لجواز أن يكون معه . وحينئذ نقول لا شبهة في أن الإمكان أو الوجوب يمتنع تأخره عن وجود موصوفه وكل صفة ثبوتية لا يمتنع تأخرها عن وجود موصوفها بل يجب تأخرها عنه ويكون هذا الدليل مطرداً في كل صفة يمتنع تأخرها عن وجود موصوفها كالحدوث ونظائره. (ضابط) يشتمل على قاعتين ذكرهما صاحب التلويحات إحداهما أساس الوجه الأول الدال على كون كل واحد من الوجوب والإمكان أمراً اعتبارياً. والثانية : أساس الوجه الآخر الذي استعمل في الوجوب أيضا، إذا اكتفي فيه بامتناع التأخر. إن كل ما تكرر نوعه أي يتصف أي شخص يفرض منه بمفهومه فهو اعتباري ) أي كل نوع كان بحيث إذا فرض أن فرداً منه أي فرد كان موجوداً وجب أن يتصف ذلك الفرد بذلك النوع حتى يوجد ذلك النوع
(And it suffices us) in inferring that wujub or imkan is a non-existential matter (the impossibility of its posteriority) to the existence of the described thing, so we do not need in that to show priority, and thus the objection that we do not grant its priority because it could be with it does not apply to us. Then we say: there is no doubt that imkan or wujub cannot be posterior to the existence of its subject, whereas every positive attribute is not impossible to be posterior to the existence of its subject, rather it must be posterior to it, and this proof is general to every attribute whose posteriority to the existence of its subject is impossible, such as origination and its like. (A rule) containing two principles mentioned by the author of al-Talwihat: one is the basis of the first face indicating that each of wujub and imkan is a mental consideration. And the second: the basis of the other face that was used for wujub as well, when one is satisfied with the impossibility of posteriority. Every genus whose type recurs, i.e., any person assumed from it is characterized by its concept, is mental. That is, every genus such that if an individual from it, any individual, is assumed to exist, that individual must be characterized by that genus in order for that genus to exist.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage provides a sufficient argument (يكفينا) for proving that wujub and imkan are non-existential (عدمية). The argument is based on the impossibility of their being posterior to the existence of their subject. Any positive attribute must be posterior to the existence of its subject because it depends on it. But imkan and wujub cannot be posterior; they must be either simultaneous or prior. Since they cannot be posterior, they are not positive attributes. This argument avoids the need to prove priority, which might be disputed. The author then gives a rule (ضابط) containing two principles: (1) the basis of the first face, which shows that each of wujub and imkan is mental; (2) the basis of the second face, which suffices with impossibility of posteriority. The first principle is: "Every genus whose instances, whenever any individual is assumed to exist, must be characterized by that genus, is mental." This means that if a concept is such that whenever an individual is supposed to exist, that individual must have that attribute in order for the genus to exist, then that attribute is a mental consideration.

الصفة الثبوتية
ضابط

الأمر الاعتباري

Misconception: That the impossibility of posteriority alone is not sufficient to prove mental consideration
Correction: The author shows it is sufficient and yields a general criterion that applies to wujub, imkan, and similar attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the argument from impossibility of posteriority?
A) Imkan and wujub must be prior to existence.
B) Imkan and wujub cannot be posterior to the existence of their subject.
C) All positive attributes are prior to existence.
D) Imkan and wujub are simultaneous with existence.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of posteriority serves as a decisive test: if an attribute cannot be posterior to its subject, it is not a real positive attribute but a mental consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If the impossibility of posteriority reveals that many of our attributes are mental considerations, how often do you mistake your own intellectual projections for the truth about Allah? Are you willing to let go of comfortable concepts so you can encounter Him as He is?

The First Principle: Every Recurring Genus is Mental
Al-Jurjani explains the first principle and lists four benefits from the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first principle of kalam: that every concept whose instances constitute a recurring genus is mental (i'tibārī), and the logical reasoning that supports it.

Core Idea
A concept that cannot be postponed from its instances is a mental concept.

Al-Jurjani unpacks the first principle of kalam: 'Every recurring genus is mental.' This principle is foundational for distinguishing between real attributes and mental considerations. In this passage, he explains the logical necessity of essential priority and lists four key benefits derived from the commentary.

Guided Path
1
Clarifying essential priority and impossibility of posteriority
The passage first clarifies that the priority meant is essential, not temporal, and then argues that posteriority is impossible for such concepts, otherwise change would be possible.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the logical foundation.
2
The logical argument and its generality
Al-Jurjani explains that the impossibility of posteriority fulfills the condition of the second figure in logic and yields a general proof without needing to establish priority separately.
This shows the elegance of the argument.
3
The principle defined and its scope
The principle states that whenever an individual of a genus is assumed to exist, it must be characterized by that genus, making the genus a mental concept. The commentator adds that the use of 'genus' is only for illustration.
Thus the principle applies to any concept with that property.
4
Four benefits and a caveat about non-existence
The commentator extracts four benefits from the interpretation, clarifying the meaning of 'recurrence', 'assumed', the word 'concept', and the pronoun. Finally, if existence is not assumed, the characteristic does not apply, as with imkan and wujub considered as non-existent.
This caveat prevents misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( سبقا ذاتياً قيد هاهنا بالذات لامتناع السبق الزماني . قوله : ( يمتنع تأخره وإلا أمكن الانقلاب. قوله : ( بل يجب إلخ ) قال :أولاً : لا يمتنع ليتحقق شرط إنتاج الشكل الثاني أعني اختلاف المقدمتين بالإيجاب والسلب، ثم أضرب عنه لبيان أن ذلك السلب متحقق في ضمن الوجوب . قوله : ( ويكون إلخ) عطف على قوله : لا نحتاج إلخ يعني أن امتناع التأخر يسقط عنا مؤونة بيان التقدم ويفيد عموم الدليل . قوله : (أي كل نوع إلخ) لعل اعتبار النوع لمجرد التصوير وإلا فكل مفهوم يكون بتلك الحيثية يجب أن يكون اعتبارياً نوعاً كان أو غيره، وأشار الشارح قدس سره بهذا التفسير إلى فوائد :إحداها : أن المراد بتكرر النوع تكرره من حيث الوجود والثانية : أن المراد بقوله : يفرض منه فرضه موجوداً . والثالثة : أن لفظ المفهوم مقحم والمراد يتصف به. والرابعة : أن ضمير هو راجع إلى قوله : نوعه لا إلى ما كما يسبق إليه الوهم . قوله : ( إذا فرض إلخ) أما إذا لم يفرض وجوده فلا يجب اتصافه بذلك النوع كالإمكان والوجوب فإنهما إذا فرضا عدمين يكونان ممتنع الوجود في الخارج، وانتفاء مبدأ المحمول لا يستلزم الحمل كما سيجيء.
His statement: (by essential priority) is qualified here by 'essential' because of the impossibility of temporal priority. His statement: (its posteriority is impossible) because otherwise change would be possible. His statement: (rather it must, etc.) He said: first, it is not impossible in order to fulfill the condition of producing the second figure, i.e., the difference of the two premises in affirmation and negation, then he turned away from that to show that that negation is realized within wujub. His statement: (and it is, etc.) is conjoined to his statement: 'we do not need', meaning that the impossibility of posteriority removes from us the burden of showing priority and yields the generality of the proof. His statement: (i.e., every genus, etc.) Perhaps the consideration of genus is only for illustration; otherwise every concept that has that property must be mental, whether genus or other. And the commentator, may God sanctify his secret, pointed out in this interpretation four benefits: first, that what is meant by 'recurrence of the type' is its recurrence from the standpoint of existence; second, that what is meant by his statement 'assumed from it' is assuming it as existent; third, that the word 'concept' is extra, and the meaning is 'characterized by it'; fourth, that the pronoun 'it' refers to his statement 'its type' and not to 'what' as might be imagined. His statement: (if assumed, etc.) As for if its existence is not assumed, then it is not necessary for it to be characterized by that genus, like imkan and wujub, for if they are assumed as non-existents, they are impossible of existence in the external world, and the non-existence of the subject of predication does not entail predication, as will come.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed commentary on the first principle: "كل ما تكرر نوعه... فهو اعتباري". It first clarifies the meaning of 'essential priority' (سبقاً ذاتياً) and 'impossibility of posteriority'. Then it explains the logical structure of the argument, noting that the impossibility of posteriority eliminates the need to prove priority and yields a general proof. Then it elaborates on the definition of the principle: 'every genus whose type recurs' means that whenever any individual of that genus is assumed to exist, it must be characterized by that genus; such a concept is mental. The commentator points out that the term 'type' (نوع) is used only for illustration; any concept that has this property is mental. He then draws four benefits from the interpretation: (1) the recurrence of the type is from the standpoint of existence; (2) 'supposed from it' means supposing it as existent; (3) the word 'concept' (مفهوم) is extra, and the meaning is 'characterized by it'; (4) the pronoun 'it' (هو) refers to 'its type' (نوعه), not to something else as might be misunderstood. Finally, it notes that if existence is not assumed, the attribute is not necessarily predicated, as with imkan and wujub when assumed as non-existent.

Key Terms
